The Aroonaut publishes bright weekly letters from
New York, Paris, and London, from skilled correspondents
who are in touch with the intense life of those great cities.
It publishes frequent letters' from other points in the
aid world, and from the older world of Asia come occa-
sional letters from India, China, and Japan. Its short
stories are famous throughout the world. It is hard to
describe their peculiar tinge, but an idea may be gathered
from this, our standing injunction to our contributors:
“ Whatever you are, never be commonplace.” The re-
sult is that the Argonaut short stdries have an individu-
ality of their own. The Argonaut endeavors to present a
wpekly review of the literary movement. Brief notes on
books, farther than lengthy reviews, together with notes
upon authors and journalists, will keep the reader weH
up with the literary gossip of the day.

THE RED PAGE.
Marcus Clarke's Minor Writings.
A good, though not a complete view of Marcus
Clarke’s minor writings, is given in the memorial
volume published in 1890 under the editorship of
Hamilton Mackinnon. The editor, who contri-
butes an introductory “biography” and “mono-
graph, ” was qualified for his task in that he was
intimately acquainted with Clarke and Clarke’s
sincere admirer. His head and hand are so heavy,
however; his commentary occasionally so stupid
and conventional, and his sense of humour so wan
and meagre ; that the general effect of his prefaces
is unlucky, even evil. The nimble spirit of Marous
Clarke was the last that should have been en-
tombed in the leaden coffin of such a dull, well-
meaning friend. Yet the selection of pieces in
the volume is comprehensive and good. To soothe
the palates of “ respectable ” subscribers, and “de-
cently ” shroud the memory of the man whose whole
mind and life were in the bourgeois commonplace
sense indecent, some of Clarke’s most character is-
tic articles and paragraphs have been omitted —
notably the anti-theological article on “Civilisa-
tion Without Delusion,” and the controversial
letter which followed in reply to Bishop Moore-
house’s criticism; but more than sufficient remains
to adequately represent Clarke’s splendid talent.
The volume, for some time out of print, has re-
cently been reprinted in sections from the old
plates, and these are sold at a price which brings
them within the reach of all Australians who de-
sire a full understanding of the most brilliant
journalist and novelist that Australia has nour-
ished.
* * * *
Marcus Andrew Hislop Clarke was born at Lon-
don in 1846. At the death of his father in 1864 he
was sent to Australia with some £7OO in his
pocket, to make his fortune. His uncle, a Yic.
Judge, obtained him a clerkship in the
Bank of Australasia, which he held for a
few months only. He went to a station near
Glenorcliy in 1865, “ gaining colonial experience ”;
returned to Melbourne in 1867, and joined the
Argus staff; started the Yorick Club in 1868:
married in 1869 ; went to Tasmania in 1870, and
wrote “ For the Term of His Natural Life ” in
1870-71; was given the place of secretary to
Melb. Public Library trustees; for ten years
wrote articles, plays, stories, sketches, for half-a-
dozen newspapers and theatres, and was known
as Melbourne’s brightest, most improvident Bohe-
mian ; became insolvent and lost his place in the
Library; lost also health, spirits, capacity, and
dribbled to death in 1881 at the age of 35.
“ Reader,” says Hamilton Mackinnon with excru-
ciating bathos, “let us draw the veil over this
sad scene. The sorrow caused by the passing
away of so bright a spirit is too mournful to dwell
upon.”
* • * *
Seeing that Marcus Clarke was in many ways
bo lovable and sympathetic a man—he would strip
himself of everything for a friend in need—it is
singular that his shorter stories and sketches so
rarely show warm enthusiasm, simple emotion.
For the most part they sparkle coldly, illumined
by the hea'd but hardly ever heated by the heart.
Yet the pages in “His Natural ' Life,” for
example, which tell how Tommy and Billy “did
it,” have a true, natural pathos which Clarke
must have felt keenly ; and throughout the novel
there is ever and again a glow of genuine
humanity. But Clarke’s other work, with rare
exceptions, has no humanity whatever. He
writes as cleverly as Thackeray, and often in
Thackeray’s vein, but there is seldom a
glimpse of Thackeray’s heart. Clarke was
usually oontent to pile epithet and epigram
upon the surface of things, without attempt-
ing to sound the human depths. And when,
perhaps understanding his deficiencies, he deliber-
ately tried to move his readers by pathetic art, he
failed more dismally than Dickens at his most
mawkish moments. For Dickens at least was
sincere, though in the attempt to reach “effects ”
corresponding with his emotion hfe strained his
art to breaking-point, and his work was false.
Dickens has tried to cheat his readers, but it is
nearly certain that he also cheated himself. In
that grotesque and laboured sketch of “ Pretty
Dick,” which almost moves his dul-l editor to
tears, Clarke has never cheated himself. The
anti-climax shrieks throughout; and he was far
too good a workman not to have heard it. It is
the story of a child lost in the bush, elaborated
with portentous art, as if the writer were saying,
“Now I will write a pathetic tale. I will wring
out your tears, and play upon your emotions ;your
entrails shall be rent with compassion, and you
shall say, ‘ Oh, the fine writer ! the great writer !”*
And always the tale is mechanical; the psychology
unnatural. From the Dickensy beginning—
A hot day. A very hot day on the plains. A very hot
day up in the ranges, too. The Australian sun had got
up suddenly with a savage swoop. ... He eyen went
down at night with a red face, as much as to say, “ Take
oare, I shall be hotter than ever to-morrow! "
to the careful ending—such an ending from the
Clarke of the Moorehouse controversy !
Pretty -Dick was lying on his face, with his head on bis
arm. God had taken him home.
all is hard-ground artificiality. Clarke must have
known his failure. And he cynically sent it for
print, with his tongue in hfe cheek at the dull
audienoe of Mackinnons !
* * * *
Naturally Clarke, failing in emotion, wrote no
poetry. Clever verses, of Gourse, suoh as any man
with a little pen-skill can accomplish; and the
only two worth preserving, “Ten Years Ago”
and “For an Album,” are attractively touched
with sentiment in the Thackerayan manner.
Clarke’s verse-translations have no virtue.
* * • »
When Clarke was ait his best he had a peg to
hang his-thoughts on. Some of his fresh presenta-
tions of old .Australian stories are wonderfully
well done. He hhs taken a dull record and sown
it with flashes of wit aod phrase, as Dumas sowed
the work of his collaborators, tiH the whole page
shines and glitters. And he brings usually a
shrewd, critical head to aid his commentary.
Discussing Mitchelfs, the Irish patriot-convict’s,
escape from' Tasmania, he points out that the
Imputed chivalry was very hollow :
A word, however, about the manner of escape. It is
hard to say that Mitohell broke his parole, but I am afraid
that at best his esoapewsß duetto melodramatic quibble.
He certainty- gave up' his “ ticket-of-leave ” before he
attempted escape, but he made all the arrangements tor
escape by virtue of the liberty which that ticket-of-leave
afforded him. His parole obtained him interviews with
Smyth, freedom to plot, money, horses, and arms. To
march like a stage hero into a police-office, and with hand
on pistol (purchased by virtue of the parole) disdainfully
ask an unarmed police-magistrate to take him into cus-
tody, was not an honest withdrawal of his plighted
word. To fulfil the terms of his contract with the
Government, he should have placed himself in the hands
of the constable in the condition he had been in when
the parole was granted him namely, unarmed, a
prisoner, with bars and stone walls around him, and no
fleet horse waiting at the door to carry him to safety, or
bold companion at his side ready to withstand attempt
at capture. Poor Smith O’Brien, eating his heart in his
cell at Maria Island, better understood the nature of the
promise of a gentleman. I am willing to believe, how-
ever, that Mitchell—perpetually posing as a hero—was
blinded by the melodramatic heroics of the proceeding to
a true comprehension of its merits.
That is a gloss on the text of the escape which
John Farrell, in his recent recital of the facts,
omitted to give.
* * * *
One inclines to believe, then, that much of the
force of “ His Natural Life ” must have lain perdu
in the records on which Clarke based his story.
But Clarke had a fine faculty of dramatic insight
and dramatic expression. Given the ore he could
refine it; given the situation, the scene, the men,
he could bring all the contours and contrasts into
prominence. His style is an admirable
journalist’s style, but not always good from
the point of literature. It is full of
light, but lacks colour and harmony. The
clean, staccato sentences grow monotonous:
they want a varied rhythm and sweep. In “His
Natural Life, ” Clarke has often tried to get these
things, and often succeeded. His brain was
highly receptive and impressible, and seems to
have largely reflected the motion and hue of the
medium in which it had just previously been
merged. Some passages of Clarke seem modelled
on Balzac; some on Hugo; some on Disraeli,
Thackeray, and Dickens. He took his literary
property where he found it; and his contem-
poraries called him a not too scrupulous borrower.
Perhaps he followed the Thackerayan model more
closely than any other, since in mental vision he
was nearly kin with Thackeray. But he lacked
Thackeray’s breadth and depth. It is sufficient
that he is himself, and that the book which con-
centrates himself can never in Australia be for-
gotten.
Henry James.
If you attempt thus late in the day to discover
from English reviewers what position Henry
James _ occupies among novelists, you will meet
with little else than frustration. It would seem
that of an author who has been following the
trade of fiction for 28 years they have no new
word to say, and doubtless it is not worth while
repeating the old. His standing is assured ;so
much from their attitude seems certain —and for
the rest you can please yourself as to exactly
where it is. No literary paper ever neglects to
review his latest book, but you will find that is of
absolutely no help. The things that are said of
him can be said with almost as much truth of
about half-a-dozen other writers, and yet his work
is like that of no one else. Of what the story
happens to be about, you can, with attention,
gam a decisive picture; but what the book is,
what value it represents, how its claims as litera-
ture must be answered, are matters that are wholly
blank. Eor the most part, the “ critics ” have no
opinion—not even the wrong one they are entitled
to—and, in short, you have to conclude that for
them a book possesses nothing but its story, that
it has no greater measure of value.
* * * *
One outward fact which, if you wander through
weak columns of literary gossip, you are liable to
stumble on is that his literary existence is regarded
by affectionate readers as a plea for the publishers’
English habit of issuing uncut books. His flavor
is so richly delicate that you love to turn it over in
your mouth ; the atmosphere with which he sur-
rounds you is so luxurious that you hate to feel
yourself exhausting it. He is the apostle of fine
shades and subtle meanings; his extraordinary
intellectual vividness enables him to capture allur-
ing phases of the indefinable. The background of
human consciousness is his special playground.
* * * •
His literary career is sharply defined into two
parts. The first yields about twenty-four volumes,
the product of twenty years ; and the latter seven,
issued since 1895. There is between them a gap of
a few years, during which he published nothing,
his attention being directed to the stage. That he
met with but little success there can be no cause
for wpnder. One so essentially an expositor ‘of all
the human subtleties that the stage cannot present
was foredoomed to failure. The genius who is to
reunite literature and the drama belongs of neces-
sity to quite a "different order from that of Henry
James.
* * • *
Yet students of fiction have no cause to complain
of the desertion; his tribute of repentance has
been of the highest and best. He came back more
riehlyjuidowed; not indeed because of anything
he borrowed in the atmosphere of the drama, but
became in silence he could not fail to gather fur-
ther intellectual strength. As the reviewers put
it, he had developed a “new manner.” His style
had greatly altered; he came back to ask his
readers to give him a little closer attention than
was demanded by others, and on his side he pro-
mised adequate return. The compact was made,
and is being faithfully fulfilled.
* » * *
The chief elements of all the books issued up to
1890, the distinguishing oharaoteristies that mark
them as the product of his individuality, are their
depth of subtlety, their reality, and simple direct-
ness. What he has to say is presented with a
simplicity that reveals the absenoe of effort.
Every fine shade of emotion seems to have as
easily surrendered to the magic of his style as if
it were the merest commonplace. At the same
time his prose has not the highest distinction. It
would, for instanoe, be unwise to compare it with
the cultured simplicity oi Andrew Lang’s. It
rather lies between tjbat and the laboured plain-
ness, the constant effort to be exact, that has
marked the later work of Howells.
* * * *
In the minor matter of having a storyffio tell
his claim is distinctly weak. It is rather that he
has real human oreatures to present, people who
are possessed with natural passions, who act only
from natural motives. His fidelity to what he
knows to be true' deserves, in an age of rant and
falsity, our lovingbudmiration. The voice of the
shrieking superlative must in his younger days
have been as deafening as it is now ; the rewards
it offered were, in comparison, scarcely less sub-
stantial. But it had up attractions for him ;
through it all he has remained faithful to the be-
lief that life has 'little to do with superlatives, and
literature a good deal less.
But it is in his short stories, forming as they do
nearly half his output, that his best work has been
done. Idonotinsist that they are all of the one excel-
lence ; on the contrary, I am ready to acknowledge
that many of them fail. The difficulty of having
no story to offer clings with its old insistence, and
is, contradictorily enough, more noticeable here
than elsewhere. His wonderful talent is at its
best in depicting subtle phases of our existence, in
presenting scenes that Verily did happen, but
which life refused to round into completeness.
The most notable exception in this connection, the
one story that combines a finest subtlety and a
matchless completeness is “The Path of Duty.”
For this little piece of work, I would, in an only
moderately reckless mood, be anxious to assert its
olaim to be considered as teaching nearer to artis-
tic perfection than any other short story in our
language.
* * * *
It can be taken as settled that Henry James is
in no danger of popularity. He has even escaped
the infliction recently fallen on Meredith —that of
becoming a fashionable fad. I should like to
think that in his long career it has been his re-
markable distinction to have never been within
sight of public favor. But it is to be acknow-
ledged that twice he has been near the verge.
“ Daisy Miller ” and “The Other House ’' really
threatened his position. The case of the former
may be said to be due to the fact that in its
American little heroine it offered to fiction some-
thing- that was entirely new at that time, while
inferior merit is the only solution one meets with
for the latter. “The Other House” isn’t habit-
able —there are doors that won’t shut, and holes
in the roof that you could put your hand through.
How Henry James came to make the awful mis-
take of putting his furniture into it would, one
likes to think, tax even his subtlety to explain.
* * * *
It is in his later work, however, in his “new
manner ” that he is at his finest. It is here that
if you bring with you a little attention he will
take you with him and show you things that are
outside the vision of your everyday. It is here
that one feels safe in saying that in regard to
fiction he is, with the exception of Meredith, in-
tellectually the biggest man in England to-day.
But what specifically represents the added value
of his later work, one is left to discover for oneself.
Reviewers have no opinion, here as elsewhere;
the most they will venture on is that the analysis
is more acute and the style more epigramatic.
To which can be added that the style has also
acquired an intensity that rises, at the merest lift,
into majestic emphasis. This was something that
really required to be done, this illustration of the
fact that the highest note of emphasis could be
reached without raising the voice to a shriek.
* * * *
But this at most accounts for only half of his
new equipment; the rest is included in the fact
that his point of view is now at a much higher
altitude. He sees further and clearer. His char-
acters are presented with a completeness we are
totally unaccustomed to; his people live even out-
side his story. Ido not wish to say that they are
more human than in his earlier work, or than in
the work of certain others, but that they have a
wider relation to life. Novelists had long been
of opinion that their characters must have their
whole being centred in telling the story, that they
must not even think of anything else. Howells,
becoming recently convinced of the limits of this
creed, had tried his hand at giving his creations a
freer scope by letting them have part in the lives
of people unrelated to the story. But Henry
J ames’s achievement is much greater, and much
truer for artistic effect.
* * * *
However, he has not yet carried his method to
its perfection. In some of his books there is a
mixture of the new style and the old that is most
irritating. You have sometimes to drop the mood
of sympathy that gives you the author’s point of
view, finding it almost a hindrance, and suddenly
in the middle of a sentence you are again in need
of it. “In the Cage,” however (which is in point
of publication his second latest volume), keeps
magnificently to the one keynote. The story pre-
sents a young lady who by her positi >n is possessed
of a peculiarly appetizing outlook on life. As a
telegraphist in a small branch office situated in a
fashionable suburb of London she comes to lead
two lives. Besides her own barren existence with
its future surrounded by her engagement to the
perfection of the type of British tradesman she
comes to have a part in the existence of her cus-
tomers. Out of the telegrams that are handed in
to her she weaves her stories and “ lives more and
more into the world of whiffs and glimpses, and
finds her divinations work! faster and stretch
further.”
During her first weeks she had often gasped at the sums
people were willing to pay for the stuff they transmitted
—the much love’s, the awful regrets, the compliments
and wonderments, and vain, vague gestures that cost the
price of a new pair of boots. She had had a way then of
glancing at the people’s faces, but she had early learned
that if you became a telegraphist you soon ceased to be
astonished. Her eye for types amounted, nevertheless,
to genius, and there were those she liked and those she
hated, hetifeeling for the latter of which grew to a posi-
tive possession, an instinct of observation and detection.
There were the brazen women, as she called them, of the
higher anti, lower fashion, whose squanderings and grasp-
ings, whose struggles and secrets, and love-affairs and
lies, she tracked and stored up agaipst them, till she had
at moments, in private, a triumphant, vicious feeling of
mastery and power, a sepse of having their silly guilty
secrets in her pocket, in her small retentive brain, and
thereby knowing so much more about them than they
suspected, or would care to think. There were those she
would have liked to betray, to trip up, to bring down
with words altered and fatal, and all through a personal
hostility, provoked by the lightest signs, by their acci-
dents of tone and manner, by the particular kind of rela-
tion sjie always happens instantly to feel. . . . She
recognised quite as much as those of her sex whom she
would have liked bo help, to see more of ; and that
alternative as weH operated exactly through the hazard
of personal sympathy, her vision for silver threads and
moonbeams, and her gift for keeping the elues and
finding her way in the tangle. The moonbeams and silver
threads presented at moments all the vision of what poor
she might have made of happiness. Blurred and blank as
the whole tiling often inevitably or mercifully became,
she could still, through crevices find crannies, be stupefied,
especially by what, in spite of all reasoning, touched the
sorest place in her consciousness, the revelation of the
golden shower flying about, without a gleam of golcUfor
herself.
* * * •
An incompetent critic reviewing this book in the
incompetent Literary World, spoke of it as an
exhibition of rampant analysis. Well, one’s out-
look is necessarily limited by one’s insight—‘‘the
eye sees what the eye brings means of seeing. ”
The poor dear man was plainly in search of a
story—something to keep him awake till bedtime
—and found that this volume was for his purpose
really valueless. One can but praise the magnifi-
cent determination to do his duty, to enter his
protest against this sort of thing being palmed off
on a patient public. And so his criticism remains
a monument of conscientiousness. And at the back
of it all, you can hear (if you listen) his distracted
cry : “What in Heaven ! s name have I to do with
literature, or literature to do with me ? ”
* * » *
It is, however, far from my wish to assert that Mr.
James’s later work is all on the one plane, or even
that the best of it might not, had he wished, have
been something better. Fine passages are most
abundant, but false notes are scarcely less so. He
has a trick of choosing the wrong word, of exhibit-
ing a clever play of adjectives whose last purpose
is to simplify his meaning. His dialogue, which
once presented him at his best, now reveals quite
another phase of him. His stories are not de-
veloped ; one sees dimly the splendid thing into
which the motif of “In the Cage ” might have
been worked, but the book remains little more than
a fragment, with its difficulties of construct!.::
and solution plainly shirked.
* * * *
What may we expect from him in the future ?
It is a question that does not produce much agita-
tion in literary circles : and, on the whole, it need
not. Mr. James enters on his 57th year this
month, and one feels sadly that the chances are
altogether against one’s hopes. He lives at Eye,
in Sussex, and has been so long a resident of
England that one at times forgets he is an Ameri-
can. The house he occupies is some two hundred
years old, but would seem to have but a poor idea
of the honor now being conferred upon it. A
recent dastardly attempt to burn itself owes its
frustration to the fact that Mr. James was at work
at the deadly hour of three in the morning.
* » • •
On the whole, his work may be said to represent
a most brilliant contribution to present-day litera-
ture. But the thought that remains side by side
with that is, that it needed only the infusion 'of
some minor qualities into his individuality to have
enabled him to give us something that we will
never get from anyone writing now. Over thirty
volumes ! —by far the greater majority of them
reaching to a very bright standard of excellence.
But where is the masterpiece we had every right
to expect, that in justice to his wonderful talents
should have long ago arrived ?
Brisbane, Q.
JOHN BEAY WATSON.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Songs of Sin.
The songs the world has ever known,
The world has ever sung —
Since first the spinning star-dust closed
And in the ether swung—
Tell hut of women, wine, and fray,
And those who score the win —
While girls have eyes and men have strength,
While days have light and nights have length,
We sing the Songs of Sin.
What time the fateful Phryne sings
Her soft, seductive song,
The waltz-tune grips our hearts and swings
Us pantingly along;
Till, in the narrowing vortex sweet,
Whirl we to join her strain,
And through the warm, dark night repeat
The ling’ring kiss-refrain . . .
And, when at last the day is come
And sunbeams trespass in,
Still in her circling arms we hum
The siren Songs of Sin.
We fling a chorus from our lips
Unsteady all, but bold ;
While lucent luscious liquor drips
In crimson drops and gold.
A nude Bacchante beats the rhyme,
Astride a cask on high,
And swings a bottle to the tune
We drain our goblets by.
We may be out of time and tune,
Our voices cracked or thin,
But all is perfect when we croon
The swilling Songs of Sin.
We sing to make the wide world shake
From peak and plain and shore
The baton thrown aloft to wake
The blatant bass of war.
We feel the lust of fray anew,
We hear the empires made —
The sharp staccato rising through
The flame-fringed barricade.
The crash of guns, the clash of steel,
The battle’s deadly din,
Bings round us as we sing—and feel—
The slaughter Songs of Sin.
The songs the world will ever know
The world will ever sing —
Till Gabriel trumpets loud and low
The scattered souls to bring—
Will tell of ivoman, wine and fray
And all the joys akin —
While girls have eyes to flash and flit,
While men can kiss and drink and hit,
We’ll sing the Songs of Sin /
Yic. .
McG.
The Town.
A huge monstrosity
Man made. An octopus of stone—
Its arms o’er land and sea
Outspread, to clutch at flesh and bone.
All day it belches steam,
Putridity, and smoke, and grime ;
Its thousand eyes, the gas lamps, gleam ;
Its blood, the sewer slush and slime.
It feeds, and growls, and lies
Sullen, and all unsatisfied,
Tainting the virtue of the skies,
And ravishing the country side.
In fitful dreams it roars
At night, and sleeps in murky mist
And soddenness, and snores
Like some huge bloated sensualist.
Brisbane.
FRANCIS KENNA.
Very Rich.
“ Beggar 1 ” that’s the way she said it,
With a sneer—the little wretch !
Beggar—l am not a beggar.
No, I’m rich—l’m very rich !
Gold ! what’s gold but yellow metal ?
Those who lose it mostly gain :
I’ve a mine just full of rubies
And some castles built in Spain.
Wouldn’t have me, silly vixen !
Pretty ?—well, her cheeks’ alarm
When I told her, looked like roses,
And her mouth was very warm :
Warm, like blossom hiding honey
(Other bees may taste and cling).
“ Beggar ! ” Ah, I turned and left with
Lift of head and stride of king.
I make love to lovely ladies,
Queens in royal blue and red ;
Woo their lips in ancient story,
Call them from the lordly dead.
True, ’tis true they lack existence
Such as she has—Well, what thru ?
Kisses have a subtle poison,
Love puts cap and bells on men.
Money ! money isn’t riches !
Tried to sting, the little wretch !
Didn’t ? —no, of course she didn’t.
For I’m rich—oh, lam rich.
RODERIC QUINN.

yankee Doodle
IN AMERICA.
The Legend.
IT was in the summer of 1778, Wash-
ington, with his army ever active,
determined to oppose the British
general, Sir Henry Clinton, who, having
evacuated Philadelphia, was retreating
through New Jersey on New York. The
forces met on 28th June at Monmouth.
La Payette conducted the attack. The
heat was so intense that some refused to
light, and many fell dead from exposure
to the sun’s rays. Both sides fought
desperately. As the day waned, and the
flagging spirits of La Payette’s soldiers
were at a low ebb, a strange sight aroused
them. Marching with drum and life
was an old man, Jeremiah Wynton, and
his son, and grandson. The old father,
with silvery flowing locks, sturdily strode
through the field. The son with ban-
daged head fifing lustily, the youth,
catching the spirit of his grandsire, rolled
out with him the soul-inspiring rattle pf
the drum. Father, son, and grandson
had accompanied the troops as volunteei s,
and were ever in the forefront of the
fight. Jhe fierce light of battle was in
their eyes, they knew no fear, and many
a wild cheer arose, as on that fateful
day Yankee Hoodie was heard as never
was heard before or since.

SATURDAY, APRIL 29, 1899.
The Federal Fight—Sydney Against
Australia.
The passage of the Federal Enabling Bill
through New South Wales Legislative
Council—thanks to the special appointment
of 12 new members—once more remits the
question of Federation to New South Wales
electors. At last year’s polling New South
Wales electors approved the principle of
Federation, and by a majority of over 5000
approved the Commonwealth scheme adopted
by the specially-elected Federal Convention.
But the popular vote was nullified by Parlia-
mentary enactment. The Parliamentary
provision that 80,000 votes must be recorded
in order to carry the Bill (there was no pro-
vision that 80,000 votes must be recorded in
order to defeat it) upheld the minority
against the majority ; and the Bill, though
carried, was technically lost. The people’s
representatives in Parliament, themselves
elected by bare majorities, had succeeded in
stifling the direct expression of the will of a
majority of the people.
* • * *
Then New South Wales Premier Reid re-
opened negotiations with the other provinces
—Victoria, South Australia, Tasmania—
which had passed the same Bill that New
South Wales had passed. In these provinces
there was no Parliament-enacted minimum-
vote, and the opinion of the majority
was effective, as it should be. A Confer-
ence of Premiers was held ; and Premier
Reid secured the assent of the other Premiers
to some alterations intended to make the
Bill more favorable to New South Wales.
Among other things, he secured that the
capital of the Commonwealth shall be in
New South Wales, but not in Sydney. It
would be a public calamity to New South
Wales if it were in Sydney, since Sydney is
already far too huge, and has impoverished
the country districts by sucking to itself the
men and money which should make the
country districts thrive. It is this new Bill
which is now to be submitted to the electors
of New South Wales.
* * * *
In spite of the concessions made by the
other provinces upon the original scheme
approved by the Federal Convention, the
new Bill has been opposed and depreciated
by a section of Sydneyites just as persistently
as was the old one. In Sydney there are
many men who are the enemies of any
possible Federation whatever. Some of
them are landlords and property-owners,
who fear that their rents and profits will be
reduced if—by putting the capital elsewhere
than in Sydney, or by letting the interior trade
take its natural channels instead of dragging it
to Sydney by hook or crook—the Sydney
monopoly at the expense of the country is
in any way reduced. Doubtless the Sydney
landlords are right, from their own narrow
point of view.. The more people there are
concentrated in Sydney (people who should,
many of them, be engaged as producers in
the country), and the more Govt, money
there is expended in Sydney to help feed
these people (money which should be devoted
to assisting the producing industries in the
country), the higher will be rents and the
greater the value of Sydney property. The
Sydney landlords have strong reason for
opposing Federation. But do New South
Wales and Australia exist for the conveni-
ence and profit of Sydney landlords ?
* * * *
. other opponents of any possible Federa-
tion are the Sydney importers and their
allies among agents, brokers, and retailers,
the Sydney importers have built up a very
profitable trade by standing at the Sydney
gate and levying commissions on goods
brought into Australia from Europe and
America. Many of these goods artisans in
Sydney and other towns could be themselves
manufacturing with advantage to the com-
munity , since, when trade is properly organ-
ised, the artisan in the town and the producer
in the country are natural allies, exchanging
t e fruits of their labor to the common good.
But the policy of the Sydney importing riim
—which is an. enormous aggregation of in-
terests with influence in every path of
national life—has always been to discounten-
ance the growth of local manufactures, since
every fresh local manufacture means ’a loss
m commissions to the importers. The Sydney
importers understand that Federation—with
all the barriers broken down between the
provinces will mean an immense expan-
sion of inter-Australian trade, and perhaps
some temporary reduction of oversea trade
and the profits upon it. They understand
that Federation, with a Protective tariff, or
even a revenue tariff against the world,
will mean an immense expansion of local
manufactures, implying fresh markets for
local producers. The Sydney importers do
not want Federation because they exist on
foreign trade, and every development of local
industry tends to decrease foreign trade.
But do New South Wales and Australia
exist for the convenience and profit of the
Sydney importers ?
* * * *
Anotheropponent of any practicable Feder-
ation is the Sydney Daily Telegraph. This
important newspaper depends chiefly for its
prosperity upon the prosperity of 'Sydney,
and anything which tends to diminish the
importance of Sydney tends to diminish its
profits. It has the same reason as the Syd-
ney landlords for dreading that Federation
may to some extent reduce the Sydney
monopoly which has attracted 31 per cent,
of the population of New South Wales to the
overgrown metropolis. And, as it supports
the foreign-trade policy, it sees eye to eye
with the Sydney importers. But the
Sydney Telegraph has a more direct
and private interest in opposing Federa-
tion. It is endeavoring to secure a great
circulation in the country districts
at the expense of the country press
and thus to gain much of the Sydney adver-
tising support which at present is chiefly
enjoyed by the rival newspaper, the S.M.
Herald. While Australia is , unfederated,
and New South Wales isolated, the Tele-
graph proprietary may hope to continue in
possession of the enormous subsidy which it
receives in being allowed to send its papers
all over New South Wales free by post—to
the loss of the country press and at the ex-
pense of the general taxpayer. The amazing
system by which a private trading concern
has its goods carried free on the State rail-
ways.at the cost of the general revenue would
certainly not be permitted to continue under
Federation. New South Wales would be
brought into line with Queensland, with
victoria, with every other country—since no
other civilised community dreams of taxing
itself in order to pay dividends to a private
newspaper proprietary. Under Federation
the country press would enjoy its legitimate
circulation, but the Daily Telegraph pro-
prietary would lose heavily—directly in
postage, and indirectly in the limitations
put to the Telegraph scheme of using a
country ascendancy to acquire ascendancy in
Sydney. No wonder the Telegraph opposes
Federation.. But do New South Wales and
Australia exist for the convenience and profit
of the Sydney Daily Telegraph 1
* * * *
It is plain that the chief reasons connected
with the opposition to the Commonwealth
Bui are reasons connected with the aggran-
disement of Sydney. These reasons would
apply with equal force to any conceivable
form of Federation ; since any form of Fede
ration which sets a Federal Parliament sitting
elsewhere in New South Wales than in
Sydney, and which permits southern and
western New Soutli Wales to find its natural
trade-outlet elsewhere than in Sydney, must
be abominable to the anti-Australian party.
I heir reasons are not in the least connected
with the exorcised bogey of the Braddon
Blot, or with the exploded myth of crushing
extra-taxation, or with the acquiescence of
Queensland, or with any other of the stalk-
ing-horses under cover of which anti-
Federal arrows may be shot. The main, and
almost the only reason of the anti-Australian
party is that Federation, however advan-
tageous to New South Wales and Australia,
may diminish the Sydney supremacy—
temporarily, at all events. So they want
the capital in Sydney ; and they will damn
any scheme of Federation which does not
put the capital in Sydney.
* * * *
The capital in Sydney is out of the question.
It is against the best interests of Sydney
itself; it is much more against the in-
terests of New South Wales, which is
even now bled white that Sydney pro-
perty-owners and importers and the Daily
Telegraph newspaper proprietary the
whole anti-Federal ring—grow fat at the
expense of the Men on the Land, the men
who sweat and starve to make the country
prosperous. And the capital in Sydney
would certainly never be conceded by the
other federating provinces. The Federal
Convention Bill is not perfect, but it is, and
was, the best compromise obtainable. New
South Wales Premier Reid, at the Pre-
miers' Conference, found himself unable to
materially alter it—simply because he could
not propose anything practically better,
more generally acceptable. If, therefore, the
present chance is rejected by New South
Wales, we stand committed for years to the
squalid dissensions and grave dangers and
losses of disunion—with the certainty that,
vyhen the question is again approached, the
Sydney vested interests (which it is even now
difficult to sweep away) will have grown so
mountainous that nothing but the stress of a
war for Australian existence will be likely to
end them.
State Paper.
It is reported by many unreliable authorities
that Premier Seddon will probably seek to dish
his opponents at the coming Maoriland elec-
tions by announcing a vigorous borrowing and
public works policy. Also, many experienced
liars in S.A. circulate the same report as re-
gards Kingston; and Dreary Dickson, in
Queensland, has already gone to the country on
the good old loan and works policy, with very
' profitable results for Dickson aforesaid. The
report as regards Seddon and Kingston gains
color from the fact that, out of the seven Aus-
tralasian Premiers who have to go to the
country every three years or oftener, at least
five generally go there on a vigorous borrowing
policy. It is a thing that is always handy ; it
always furnishes an oppoil unity to say some-
thing bright and hopeful about the “resources
of this great country” and how they are going
to be “developed ; ” the dullest ass of an elec-
tor can understand it when he is told that
money is going to be shot about promiscuously
and a railway-station made in everybody’s
front garden ; also, the dullest ass of a Premier
can do that kind of work.
* * • »
The Bgeeetin also has ideas about a borrow-
policy. It has a pleasant theory about how
these provinces among them can raise
£8,000,00 ) or £10,000,000, or more, without in-
terest, with which to build reproductive rail-
ways, &e., the money to be repaid some day,
when the country is quite rested, out of the
earnings of these railways. That is, always
provided the country feels like repaying the
loan at all ; if it doesn’t feel that way, it
really doesn’t matter, and the earnings of these
railways and other works which cost nothing
can be set aside to form a fund for building
more railways, etc., which won’t cost anything
either, till the country is covered with
valuable public works which cost nothing
at all. A good deal of the liability would
gradually wear away, or get burnt, or fall
overboard and be lost, or get demolished
by rats, or blow away, or otherwise dis-
appear, and the rest could stand till the Day
of Judgment. In fact, if sufficient time were
allowed, it would practically all disappear by
its own volition. The idea is simply the old,
useful one of an issue of State paper-money, and
it isn’t proposed to start it on any wild or ex-
perimental principle, but to keep to the wmll-
trodden paths of precedent, and to be as deeply
conservative over it as the most virulent Tory
could desire.
* * * *
At present Queensland has an issue of State
notes, on a small scale and based on very stupid
principles. In the other six provinces there
are in circulation over ,£4,000,000 of the paper
obligations of the banks, which means that the
banks have the loan of £4,000,000 of the public’s
money. There is no visible reason why the
State shouldn t have the loan of that sum
instead. If the State chose to make its notes
legal tender and not redeemable in gold—for a
number of years, at least—there is no possible
reason why it shouldn’t do so. The public
really never wants gold for that £4,000,000 of
bank paper ; it keeps circulating year in and
year out, and though the small outer fringe of
the mass keeps coming in to be cashed, there
is always a reliable £4,000,000 that remains in
circulation. If the State was always will-
ing to guarantee the soundness of its own
paper by accepting it in payment of taxes,
there is no possible reason why it should
not remain at par as long as the State
lasts. Ihe State itself only requires gold for
the purpose of paying its foreign obligations ;
and so long as Australasia remains a gold-pro-
ducing country that will probably be forthcom-
ing. For the other three-fourths of its
outgoings—the purely local outgoings—it can
get through well enough if it keeps receiving its
own legal tender paper on account of taxes,
and paying it out again to cover local expendi-
ture. The State paper would be no dirtier, no
more infectious, no more walked-upon by
microbes, than the private banking paper now
in circulation. And it would have much better
ultimate security behind it.
* * » *
In arldition to this there is another side to
the case. There are £21,000,000 of gold re-
serves in the banks of the Seven Provinces.
Under the Canadian law, if it were applied in
Australia, the State would lay its hands gently
on over £8,000,000 of this as a practically per-
petual loan without interest. And the process
would do no visible harm to the banks while
doing a vast amount of good to the State
—provided the money was wisely applied.
The Canadian law requires the banks of the
Dominion to keep 40 per cent, of their reserves
in State-notes, and the application of a similar
law in Australasia would mean that the banks
would hand over ,£8,000,000 of gold to the
Government and receive paper in exchange.
The paper, being legal-tender, would answer
all local purposes just as well as the metal does.
The gold-reserve lies festering in the bank’s
cellars, earning no interest, and the reserve of
three-fifths gold and two-fifths State-guaran-
teed paper-money might fester there just as
well. But, while the bank’s reserves wouldn’t
be really weakened, the State would be
£8,000,000 to the good, which sum would cost
it practically nothing, while the same sum, if
borrowed at 3 per cent., would cost £240,000 a
year.
* * * *
And that £8,000,000, if it were spent in
building much-needed railways that produced
even 2 per cent, on their outlay, would yield
the State a clear revenue of £160,000 a year.
How much more it would yield in increased
land values, increased income-tax, Customs,
excise, etc., arising through increase of popu-
lation, it is impossible to guess. By the appli-
cation of the Canadian State note svstem in
these provinces Maoriland could raise about
£1,500,000, which would go a long way towards
finishing its three great trunk ra.ilways without
adding anything to the national burdens.
Victoria could raise about £3,ooo,ooo—suffi-
cient to build its principal mallee lines, and to
make the proposed great irrigation channel
from the Murray to the alleged lakes in the
Dry Country. N.S. VV. could find some
£2,500,000 in the same receptacle and lock the
Darling with it, and finish its Broken Hill rail-
way, and do other things. Even S.A. could
raise £900,000, and Tasmania about £400,000
under the Canadian State note system, and
£400,000 would do a lot to open up the road-
less West of the Isle of Lots of Sleep.
* * * *
The Bulletin isn’t proposing a wild or reck-
less experiment or a novel departure of any
kind. It is advocating a system that has been
tried in older and more conservative countries
and found perfectly safe and feasible. It
would be better, no doubt, if Australasia would
pay its way and construct its public works out
of revenue ; but, if it won’t, the next best
thing is to raise the money locally, and by the
cheapest possible process. The cheapest one
this paper knows of is that by which the in-
terest is nothing per cent, and the date of re-
payment practically never. The suggestion is
offered to Maoriland and Victoria in particular,
for various reasons. In Maoriland the demand
for the completion of the three big half-
finished railways is growing very loud ; and
the Opposition is bidding frantically for office
with impossible promises about how it will re-
duce taxation, and push on public works, and
not borrow, and get the needful funds’from
nowhere at all. If Seddon is going to bid against
this party, he also needs to have a public-works
policy, and to find the money from nowhere.
And as Maoriland has a larger note circulation
in proportion to population than any other
province, it is a place where a particularly
handsome rise can be made by the seductive
process advocated above. A promise to raise
£1,500,000 without interest with which to com-
plete the Otago Central and Midland and North
Island Trunk lines should make Maoriland wor-
ship Seddon as it never worshipped Julius
Vogel even in his palmiest days. And in Vic-
toria the community wants to see the railway-
building days back again, and the mallee
country is crying out for water and for means
of communication, and Turner loathes big
loans and interest bills, and he can’t screw-
more taxation out of his awful House of 48
Rich Landlords. Therefore a scheme for rais
ing the wherewithal without interest should
appeal to his inmost soul.
* » * »
The paper-money process wouldn’t really
make the country £8,000,000 or £10,000,000
richer, but it would make it feel that much
richer, which is practically the same thing.
Victoria was wildly affluent in ’9O, when pro-
perty-values were high, and it was miserably
poor in ’95, when they were low. Yet all the
land was still there, and so were all the build-
ings, and, in addition, there was more cash in
the country and less debt owing by it in the
bad year than in the good one. But the coun-
try felt poor in ’95 all the same, whereas, with
the same assets in ’9O, it felt exceedingly rich.
If the creation of a limited amount of paper-
money can make the country feel richer, then
to ail intents and purposes it will be richer
—and the feeling will be permanent, for the
State paper won’t vanish like the scrip of the
shoddy land banks which made up so much of
Victoria’s alleged wealth in’9o. One thing is
certain : if, by liberating say £8,000,000 of
the money which now earns nothing in the
bank’s cellars, and replacing it with a medium
which will answer the same purpose in the same
cellars, and by making judicious use of the
sum released, the country can come into pos-
session of £8,000,000 of useful public works,
returning 2 per cent, on their cost, it will be
these £8,000,000 wort h of assets to the good.
Also, it will be at least £160,000 a year revenue
to the good. And both these matters are worth
consideration.


PLAIN ENGLISH.
Justices’ Injustice.
“ Pandect ” to The Bulletin
At, frequent intervals you print scathing denunciations
of the unpaid magistracy : you have reason. Of course
file weak spot in the system is that J.’sP., fixed in some
country district, gradually, like all other men, come
to divide the inhabitants into two classes—their friends
and their enemies. This, also, is the weak spot in country
juries. P.M.’s and constables, being human, if left too
long in one place, suffer from the same dry-rot, and
become partisans; therefore they should be changed
round at short intervals; while, as for honorary J.’sP.,
as dispensers of justice they should be swept out
of existence at once and for ever That is my
deliberate opinion after 20 years official experience
of them North, South, East, and West. Still, with all the
will to make a mockery of Justice, how small are their
opportunities now compared with what they were? I
give three samples taken at random from the pages
before me—l could as easily give three score—they all
occurred less than 50 years ago in N.S.W., at a time when
a Justice’s power seems to have been practically un-
limited. Ist.—A.B. was charged with seizing and violat-
ing Juliana R (on whose information does not
appear), but Juliana tripped into the witness-box, and
deposed that, although she was seized, there was no
violation, as whatever had occurred was with her
free will and consent, and had been occurring
for months past. Result—accused discharged. Bub
are the police non-plussed ? Not at all. Accused
is at once re-arrested and charged with dis-
honest and disorderly conduct! !! in cohabiting with
Juliana, and, without being called on for a defence, is
sentenced to 12 months’ hard labor in irons ! 1!!! 2nd.—
A. is charged with stealing and eating a pig. B. and 0.
with eating thereof knowing it to be stolen. The evi-
dence—save the mark !—was (1) the arresting constable
swore that two black gins told him they had seen prisoner
kill the pig, and (2) D., a mate of A., 8., and 0., swore
he had helped to eat the pig, being too frightened to re-
fuse. Now, the first rule of evidence is that an informer’s
evidence, by itself, is tainted, and must be corroborated.
Here there was no corroboration, for the constable’s
statement was not evidence at all; but, without being
called on for a defence, A. was sentenced to 75 lashes—B.
and 0. to 50 each. 3rd.—A., an assigned servant,
charged 8., another assigned servant, with stealing
about £1 in silver from A.’s box. There was no
evidence, save A.’s—that the money had ever existed.
A. swore B. was the only man who was near the
hut that day. B. said he had never seen the money,
and never knew A to have had any. 0, the overseer,
being called, stated that in his opinion A was worthy of
belief, but B’s character was indifferent. Result—B to
receive 50 lashes on the bare back 1 and so on. What is
the hardship of the Armidale case quoted by you com-
pared with the above? Let us be thankful for small
merc ies—that the wavback beak has no longer power
to deal out lashes “ on his own.”

Medical Sweating 1 .
Editor Bulletin. —The doctors, egged on by
Bruck, are making a dust about the lodges —
but there is something to be said on both sides
of that question. In some districts it is pull
devil, pull baker—pull doctor, pull patient. The
doctor expects imposition, and the patient ex-
pects neglect and imagination generally
effects a good deal more than the facts would.
The doctor who caters for the classes, sets up
in a big house, with a carriage and flunkeys—
and waits wearily for patients, who may not
come, and, if they do come, may keep him
waiting for his money, or never pay at all. Lhe
doctor who caters for the masses, takes a lodge or
two, gets a great deal of experience, and earns a
definite, if not enormous, income ; he makes no
bad debts, and need not make an expensive
show. Between the two, the public is not as
well served as it might be. The day is not far
distant when the State will take on the healing
just as it has taken on education. A staff of
medical men will be employed at adequate
salaries to attend to all-comers. There will be
a better division of labor, and greater gain to
public health. The underworked doctor may
get the experience, but often enough it is too
much for his physique, while his neighbor is
rusting for want of something to do. When
they both become medical officers, matters will
be averaged, with advantage to ail. To some,
this idea may be novel. Once upon a time,
State education was looked upon as a deed of
the devil.— Patientia Docet.
Conscientiously Conscienceless.
N.S.W. Leg. Assembly, which in the matter of
Federation tyrannised over its constituents, is
itself tyrannised by its officials. Speaker
Abbott monopolises the limited accommodation,
and even stops ventilation of the Chamber be-
cause it makes a noise ; while the critics who
impute much of the bad manners of the
Assembly to his frequent absences from the
Chair are no doubt right. Everybody is sorry
for Speaker Abbott’s ill-health ; but he should
have resigned the Speakership as soon as it be-
came clear he was not well enough to thoroughly
fulfil his duties. And Speaker Abbott
grows impatient or worse. Ihe other night
John Haynes rose to speak, and sat down while
the Speaker intervened on a point of order.
Then he rose again ; and the Speaker ruled
that he had lost his right to speak—because he
had sat down in deference to the Speaker ! A
few days later Crick, M.L. A., had to com-
plain that seats were reserved in the ladies’
gallery—of this democratic Assembly, in this
democratic country —for “ the nicest people,
while ordinary plebeians who came first were
crowded into back seats to crane for sight and
hearing. The episode of Fegan, M.L.A., was
contemporaneous. He was chairman of a com-
mittee of the House to discuss Copeland's
measure permitting conditional Sunday-trading
in liquor; and wdien an emasculating amend-
ment was moved, the votes were equal. It
was the duty of Chairman Fegan, as an
official, to let the House decide the fate
of the measure later on ; but he voted
for the amendment—quashed discussion, and
quashed the Bill 1 “My conscience would not
let me act otherwise,” he said. But it was
Fegan’s duty, as chairman, to have a little re-
gard for the other fellows’ consciences. He
was not a member, voting as he listed ; he was
an official representative of the House, and his
private opinions were not in question at all.
It is the practice—and the reasonable and just
practice—for Speaker or Chairman, in an even
division, to give the House another opportunity
of consideration and decision. Fegan chose to
stifle debate. He may have been justified of
his conscience ; but he certainly was not justi-
fied by common-sense and common practice.
Until he learns his duty as the House’s tem-
porary head, he should be chairman no more.
The Boss Land-Grabber.
The notorious attempted Papuan Grab isn’t a
circumstance beside that achieved by R. G.
Reid, who practically ow'ns and runs New-
foundland. That island is one-sixtli larger than
Ireland, and Reid owns one-half of it in fee
simple. He is substantially feudal baron over
200,000 people. 7000 sq. miles of the country
are absolutely his very own, together with the
enormous mineral and timber wealth therein
contained, and every mile of its railway system
will eventually become his private property.
Reid, a penniless Scotch boy, originally started
life in Australia, emigrated to Canada, and made
money by building sections of the Can. Pac.
R.R. Then this genius in the art of exploiting
perceived the possibilities of the land-grant
system. In ’93 he built a railroad across New-
foundland for Govt, for £3OOO per mile. When
built, a poverty-stricken Govt, could neither
equip nor operate the line. Reid came to the
rescue and ran the concern, receiving, in addi-
tion to what he could make out of it, ,£ 12,000
a year as a mail-subsidy, and 5000 acres of land
for every mile worked by him for a period of
ten years. But with that he was not content.
He offered to operate the railroad without
anv subsidy after the expiration of 10 years,
provided that at the end of 40 additional years
the road should be his. In other words, the
Govt. Daid Reid his own price for building the
line, leased it to him free of all rent, gave him
an immense tract of land to run it, in addition
to the use of the line gratis, gave him a big
cash subsidy for 10 years, and is to give him
the line itself at the end of another 40 years,
free, gratis, and for nothing. Still this fear-
fully rapacious man was not satisfied. At the
time of making the 40-year offer he further
stipulated that the whole railway and tele-
graph monopolies of the island should be his,
and he should get more land-grants for taking
them over. An unspeakable Govt, agreed to
these terms, whereupon the long-suffering
people raised an outcry, and last year petitioned
Chamberlain, as Col. Sec., to cancel the con-
tract, on the ground that the island had been
sold for a song. Chamberlain replied to the
effect that, no^matter what personal opinion he
might hold respecting such a contract, he could
not interfere to prevent the management by a
self-governing province of its own finances.
At present, besides his railway business, the
insatiable Reid runs a fleet of steamers, ex-
ports 100,000 tons of iron-ore yearly, and is now
busy floating a N.F. Bleaching Pulp Co.,
capital, £400,000, and a N.F. Pyrites Co., capi-
tal £300,000. Beside these schemes the per-
formances of Hooley and co. appear child’s
play. Reid’s latest concession has been de-
clared by English experts to be worth a mil-
lion in hard cash, and the awful Whiteway
Govt, threw it away for nothing. Some day
Reid, oroneof his sons, may take it into his head
to re-visit Australia, in which case it would pay
the Federal Govt, to pass an Act prohibiting
his coming within three miles of the coast, on
the ground that he is a noxious disease and a
danger to the community. Half-a-dozen syn-
dicators of Reid’s sort would mop up this
fatigued country.
“ C.C.” on the great lodge-doctor question
Dear Bulletin. —About time the monotonous wail of
the lodge-doctor was countered with a few remarks from
the other side. Bulletin’s picture of “ Munificent Odd-
fellow ” idving doctor an extra 9d. for his 61 visits is a
fair shot enough, but, it isn’t, conclusive. The medico
who- howls in 8., 25/3/99, sets up the old bogey-Mimster
of the Crown with big pension who uses his lodge privi-
leges to the utmost. But take an average case-my
own As I make only just enough to live on, prolonged
illness would mean debt, and I therefore joined a lodge.
Having no ambition to prance around in gaudy mil-
linery with a bogus handle to my name, I should
have" preferred the “ System advocated by Bruck and
others,” but there was no other way. When the
loih’-e-doctor upon examination pronounced me a
gimd risk, he agreed to let me sweat him ; or, to put it,
the other way, he jumped at the chance of sweating me.
Probably said to himself—“ Here is a man who, barring
accidents, won’t need my services, and I shall geti lus
cash for nothing.” And he was right. I have paid him,
so far, about 2.1, and have only received from him one lot
of advice (a matter of five minutes) and a small bottle of
liniment. Yet there are hundreds like myself who don’t
whine about the unfair contract W'e have made. We don’t
want to be sick for the joy of getting our money’s worth
out of the doctor, and are only too glad to keep on pay-
ing him our dues with the comforting knowledge that a
broken limb won’t cost us a year’s income. In my case,
the lodge wouldn’t save me from paying the sweated
doctor ,65 for mileage before he looked at my r tongue.
Very irritating, no doubt, for the lodge medico to have a
few wealthy mean men on his list, or a crowd of hard
bargains who live on medicine, but the healthy sub-
scribers pay for the wasters. Besides, these unprofit-
able clients have to be examined before they are ac-
cepted, and if the doctor is too greedy, or doesn’t know
enough to refuse a bad bargain, that’s his private funeral.
And, in such a case as mine, it’s a fair wager. When I
insure my house against fire, the eo. lays me £2OO to 30s.
that it won’t burn, and I take the odds, hoping I’ll lose.
But, should I have the misfortune to “ win,” the co.
doesn’t hand over the stakes with an implication that I
am a mean man to take ’em. Your lodge-doet.or makes a
somewhat similar wager about my health, and I want to
know why his voice is so frequently like unto the croak
of Iket Mo parting up a double. [The doctor’s argu-
ment is that the premium isn’t enough—and, at L4s.
a year, he is probably right.]
The average S.A. politician is a careful soul.
Mostly he is naturally so ; bub if he isn’t, the
law does what it can to compel him to be while
he is campaigning. For he may nob spend
more than ,£5O if there are 2000 electors or less
in his district; and he may spend only £5 more
for every additional 200 —a not very fair
arrangement, by the way, where scattered
country populations are concerned. And, then,
he may not spend it upon whatever he pleases ;
he may buy electoral rolls, print addresses and
notices of meetings, buy stationery and stamps,
and pay for telegrams, hire halls, and engage
scrutineers and one election agent; but all
other ways are barred. Nor may a friend
pay for any service on his behalf; if any-
body wants to assist him the money must
be paid to him, and he must disburse it per-
sonally. and include it in the accounts which
have to be certified, filed and gazetted. Pre-
mier Kingston wants to go further even than
this : he wants to have part at least of a candi-
date’s expenses paid by the State. Local news-
papers naturally find it hard to accept these re-
forms philosophically ; it is a little galling to
the proprietor-editor to read in a2in. advt. that
“Messrs. Brown, Jones, Robinson, Thomp-
son, and Smith will address the electors as fol-
lows ” —a string of a dozen places being added.
So one advt., one hall, one conveyance will
serve the whole half-dozen candidates. This
of course applies particularly to the country
and suburbs ; but, then, nearly all S.A. is
country and suburbs. Even Ministers run
like this. Formerly the fairly Fat Man used to
“arrange” with the city newspapers for more
or less full reports of his speeches ; the price at
one time was £3 3s. per col. But even that is
now illegal unless the candidate’s name, or that
of his one agent, is at the foot of the matter.
Consequently S.A. newspaper readers have less
to skip than they used to have during the
season of what passes for political fighting in
that tired province.
Thus J. G. Drake, M.L. A., in Progress, on
the danger of the kanaka becoming a perma-
nent curse to Australian civilisation :
In a former number I showed bv statistics how, for the
last 10 vears, the total populat ion contained a continually
decreasing proportion of women. It must also be clear,
though statistics are not available to prove it, that year
bv year the totals include a larger proportion of kanakas
who have been over three years in the province. Some ot
them have probably now been in Queensland for six, tune,
twelve or more years. These are the survivors who have
become acclimatised, who make engagements, work for
the highest wages they can get, take occasional holidays
at Kanaka boarding-houses, and ape all the vices of the
white man. These are the men who lease land, marry
white women, and stand on their rights when the Govern-
ment in the interests of the people of Queensland pro-
fess to be anxious to stamp out disease. Inis
vast horde of 8589 kanakas does not now consist
to any extent of freshly - caught savages who can
be terrorised into submission and us d as something next
door to machinery. It is an army of semi-civilised bar-
barians who have settled down in Queensland and who are
continually tightening their hold, and who, when backed
up by capitalists, may become irremovable. It is mud
and filth entering into the stream of a nation s pure life
blood.
S.A. Assembly general election is fixed for this
Saturday (29th). It is remarkable in that it
witnesses Premier Kingston’s second successive
appearance before the country as Premier since
he took office in June, 1893. The Kingston
Government goes to the country with House-
hold Suffrage, alias Leg. Council reform, as its
main plank. Federation, which is certain to
be affirmed by a large majority, is in the back-
ground, and the chief interest centres around
the referendum on Household Suffrage. Last
year S.A. Assembly affirmed the principle by a
large majority, but the Council contemptuously
rejected it. Kingston now plainly states that
if a majority follows him, and the people
ask for it next Saturday, the Bill will
be re-introduced directly the new Par-
liament meets, and a second defeat in the
Council will be followed by a dissolution. The
disorganised S.A. Opposition, comprised of a
mixed crowd of ex-Premiers and other political
failures, has no settled policy on the question,
but merely howls that the Premier wishes to
destroy the Council. Kingston anticipates a
two-to-one majority for Household Suffrage,
and the prospect of the rolls for the Council
being nearly trebled is already causing dismay
in the ranks of the clique which has for so long
dominated the Upper House and wrecked
Democratic measures.
An ex-M.L. engine-driver to The Bulletin :
Re that M.L. railway-accident and the engineer’s state-
ment that the driver was travelling too fast. I can testify
that this dangerous practice on that province s railways
is encouraged by the bosses, and that) any driver who
didn’t make up* time would suffer for it. There is a
stringently-worded rule against excessive speed to make
up lost time, but any driver who honestly adheres to it
will get into disfavor, and the traffic bosses will worry
him all they can by putting him on to goods-trains or
shunting-engines, or shifting him about continually, ine
driver who will travel like the devil is favored, and if any
accident happens—providing it does not come under the
notice of a jury—always gets off scot-free. Ive known
a driver of this sort who had to give up his train through
ignorance of how to work a ticklish injector, and nothing
was said to him, whereas, had he been a man who obeyed
the speed-rule, he would have been put back firing.
Indeed, it is notorious that drivers who habitually in-
fringe this rule have the best chance of promotion. Even
N.S.W.’s late Mr. Eddy once dismissed a driver for want
of energy” because he refused to run very much faster
than the scheduled time betwixt two stations. Many ex-
drivers from both M.L. and N.S.W. railways are so be-
cause they wouldn’t conform to this dangerous practice.
Again, most of the “fast” drivers are boozers, whereas
even the smell of liquor on a driver should ensure his
dismissal. I could enumerate many accidents due to
half-intoxicated drivers neglecting signals and making
other mistakes.

“Shaker” re the new Q. mining law which
came into operation Ist Matcl) :
An all-night “stonewall” was productive of nothing
better than the exclusion of “ Chinese and other Asiatic
and African aliens” from those alluvial fields alreai.y
closed to them, thus practically leaving- them in posses-
sion of the whole of the alluvial ground in the province.
Even on a purely cash basis it would pay the State to
reserve the “abandoned” fields for its own people.
There is an ever-increasing number of fairly able-bodied
men who are quite out of the running for wages by
reason of their age or aged appearance, and who must
either support themselves by fossicking, or become a
charge on the State. At present they do (and, in the ab-
sence of the slant-eyed “gleaners,” would continue to)
make some sort of a living. That it is not a luxurious
one does not affect the question ; the men are satisfied
with it, plus the hope, which never leaves a digger, of
making a rise some day. But, then —what is to become
of the Chows? Well, Mr. J. A. Philp can have them to
grow cheap vegetables for Sydney.
“ The secretly-concocted Premiers’ Conference
Bill ” is, in their own opinion, the most deadly
label the “ Anti’s ” have so far devised for the
Federal Bill campaign. “ Secretly-concocted ”
is a large-bore double-barrelled charge, but the
devisers only think of this after they have come
—a long time after —to dislike the Conference
work. The right to meet which the Premiers
possessed went with a right to sit how they
liked. If the Anti’s resisted this they should
have done so at the time with the vigor they
now display against the Bill. Instead they
waited to extract a Bill to their liking, or no
Bill from the Conference sitting in secret, and,
failing to get what they wanted—a No-Bill foi
preference—they now endeavor to nail on the
Bill as proof of utter evil a label embodying a
condition to which they assented.

N.S. Wales Education Minister Bwue in-
formed an audience last week that if there
existed in any part of the world a more liberal
system of education than that of N.S. W. lie
should be glad to hear of it.” Is it not known
that a large number of N.S.W. children of
school age never go to school? Owing to
faulty wording of the Act they cannot be legally
reached. Again, what about the 3d. a week
(a reduction on quantities) which each N.S.W.
State school scholar must carry to the school-
plate or seek remission as a pauper ? No
country which includes a clause of that charac-
ter in its elementary education Act can afford
to talk like Mr. Hogue. To be justly Compul-
sory, education must be Free. To go no
farther, Vic. and Queensland are more liberal
than N.S. W.
The favorite Melb. plan of getting rid of a
contraband infant now seems to be the per-
sistent giving to it of unsuitable food (which is,
of course, cold-blooded poisoning), and on this
ground one “ nurse ” out of many similar prac-
titioners has just been mercifully convicted of
manslaughter. In this case the pi emium paid
the nurse was JB2O, while in another instance
the consideration for “adoption” was no less
than £9O, the putative father in this case re-
maining unknown. The “ respectable ” dailies
might in such cases very well omit the girls’
names, as also in affiliation cases. It is utterly
unfair that the mother should take all the
“ shame,” while the father shelters himself be-
hind the’girl’s loyalty. But the real culprit in
the matter is neither father nor mother, but
that hypocritical section of society which looks
upon the advent of an unauthorised youngster
as almost a worse crime than murder. The
dailies habitually pat themselves on the back
and congratulate each other at every officially-
reported increase of population ; yet here at
our door is a natural increase, and we carefully
give it pills or the battery, or smother it, or
leave it on a doorstep, to the moral and physical
harm of the mother and the bitter disgrace of
a community that sniffs in horror at the men-
tion of a foundling hospital. The establish-
ment of such hospitals, under Govt, inspection,
would largely diminish the at present rampant
practice of abortion, and abolish that large and
increasing class of “nurses” who are practi-
cally neither more nor less than wholesale baby-
murderers.

S.A. Register leads the howl against House-
hold Suffrage, which Premier Kingston hopes
will provide a specific for Leg. Council obstruc-
tion. The Tory mouthpiece was once Radical
and, therefore, in its gouty age, Democrats take
a delight in unearthing the ideas it expressed
before its blood became thin and its hair turned
grey. One charming reproduction is the fol-
lowing from the Register of 25/7/’57, when
Forster, a sturdy Democrat, edited the paper :
We want no “Upper” and Lower Houses. If two Cham-
bers are to be perpetuated, let them represent distinct
political ideas, but let them be elected on the same suf-
frage. . . • Our own opinion often expressed is that
legislation by two Houses is not the system best adapted
for this colony. . . . We go for such a modification of the
Council as shall make it in every respect as much the
creature of the popular will as the Assembly is. . . . So
long as the Council exists as a distinct branch of our
Parliamentary system, we contend that its basis should
be the following
Franchise of electors.—The same as in the Assembly.
Fixed term of duration.—The same as in the Assembly.
Qualification of members.—The same as in the Assembly-.
This is even more sweeping than the reform
which Kingston so earnestly fights for to-day,
and which the Register of 1899, owned body
and soul by Fatnian, so persistently opposes.
Loot and Plunder.
(Or the gospel of Brotherly Love according to
Sydney D.T.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Wise has made another blunder
Which our Daily bosom grieves—
Says we spoke of “ loot ” and “ plunder,
Says we hinted neighbors “thieves.
We indignantly deny it—
We, the Editorial We—
And we’ll stand or tumble by it,
Will the truthful Daily T.
No, we never, never said it—
Not a word to their diseredit;
You can search old musty Telegraphs m sheaves.
We said (and can you wonder ?)
They were bent on “ loot and plunder :
But we never, never hinted they were thieves.
We challenge demonstration
That by word or imputation
We ever once suggested they were thieves.
’Tis silly, we assever,
To speak our neighbors ill;
We have never done it —never —
And. we never, never, will.
Australians all are brothers,
And we wish them to unite ;
We wouldn’t dream (like others)
Of egging them to fight.
No, we love our neighbors dearly,
And if truth compels us merely
To state the few misgivings we may feel,
We say (we’re frank as Brutus)
They would plunder us and loot us—
But we never dream of hinting they would steal.
We challenge contradiction
When we state, with full conviction,
That we never thought it possible they’d steal.
To lots of other matters
Our method will extend.
We have torn the Bill to tatters,
Till it takes some time to mend.
We have raised a thousand “ bogeys ”
Which invariably are slain
(Then our cutest dodge in vogue is
Just to raise them up again).
Yet, after Referendum,
We can add a slight addendum
To explain the real opinions that we had ;
We have call’d it monstrous —rotten —
Misconceived and misbegotten—
But we’ve never even hinted that it’s bad.
We with emphasis assert it,
And you cannot controvert it—
We have never, never said the Bill was bad.
GAMMA.
MORTAL MANN.
There was a Mann who had a mind
To live a brave, unselfish life,
To help the lame and lead the blind,
And teach his feebler fellow kind
The wisdom of concerted strife ;
There was a Mann who ruled the mass
By moral strength—aye, there’s the rub !
That Mann, alas ! has changed his class,
And chuckt his job—to keep a pub.
There was a Mann whose outstretched hand
Could quiet angry dockers’ din ;
Their cause at “Good old Tom’s ” command
Spoke for itself throughout the land.
Now “Good old Tom ” (0, London gin !)
Who bade them walk in sober ways
Beside their wives who wash and scrub
Has cast aside his wreath of bays
To serve the masses—in a pub.

St. George’s Hall Dante.
V Lord and Lady Algy,” by R. C. Carton,
arrived at the Royal last Saturday, and were soon
found to be “an uncommon entertainin’couple, ”
as they would say themselves. The whole com-
pany acts as if the society atmosphere of the play
was its native air, and the wit and simple fun of
the piece are things of beauty and a joy for ever.
Brough fits into the part of Lord Algy without a
crease. There are moments when it is impossible
to say whether Algy was made for Brough, or
Brough for Algy. His jiart inspires him. He
plays neatly, crisply, and when he goes “disguised
m drink ” to a fancy-dress ball he rises to the top-
most heights of the deliciously maudlin. Also,
Mrs. Brough has just the part that suits her. She
is rather a Sans-Geney-Dandy-Dicky Lady Algy.
But, then, each r6le Mrs. Brough has ceased to*fili
echoes somewhere or other through the part she is
just Not being an actress who goes in for
the realistic, she soon exhausts her little tricks
and has to repeat them, confidently. As Lady
Algy she plays a smoking, betting woman of the
world, with an unworldly heart, in a bright,
modern way that puts everybody in a good temper.
A peculiarity of this play is that every character
has a claim on your sympathy. The dramatis
persona are remarkably well-balanced people, as
good as they are bad, or as bad as they are good,
u evident the author liked them all, and
the house unconsciously follows. The Marquis of
Quarmby, who is going to run away with another
man’s wife, never pretends to be a villain. Lovell
in the part shows that he can act with freedom
but still seems a little hazy about the sort of man
he means to be ! Mr. Mclntyre’s Mawley is a
gem of a lean little jockey, with a face like a back
door, and a weakness for whisky. Miss Grace
JN oble makes her re-appearance in a shadowy part
and leaves no particular impression. Mr. Leslie
Victor’s valet is a snappy bit of flunkeyism. Mr.
Rotheram, as the jealous nuisance with a heavy
hand on the china, makes the return of his wife an
impossible incident. The ‘ ‘ better half ” who once
ran away from those aggressive whiskers could
never return. Altogether, the company shows
itself off to more advantage than in its opening
play, and if this city doesn’t put all its money,
and a bit more, on the Algies, it deserve® “ twelve-
months hard ” at the Lyceum.

“ lolanthe ” has to run for a single week

m “ Pygmalion and Galatea ” a Gilbertian

Speaking of the latter : “lolanthe” was grandly
patronised. But it was a somewhat disappointing
performance. Curiously, the chorus was leagues
ahead of the principals. The Lord Chancellor of
Howard Vernon was, of course, a performance of
“heaven-kissing merit to quote Rignold or
Shakespeare. Miss Florence Perry was the
Phyllis of the piece. Very dainty, she looked
like a Dresden-china image that had fallen off
some old-world shelf in one of memory’s curiosity-
shops—but we have listened to more likeable
Phyllises. William Pauli was the Strephon of
the cast ;with a red, brick-dust face reminiscent
°j n? tain Mayne Reid and Fenimore Cooper
and Choctaws and Sioux. He was easily over-
topped by Mr. P. Bathurst (Earl of Mount-
ararat), and Mr. Chas. Kenningham (Earl Tol-
loller), whose singing and comedy-acting were artis-
tic in the very highest sense. Miss Dorothy Vane
was the lolanthe. There are no ferocious de-
mands, and she did very well. Mr. Stannis Leslie
was a capital Private Willis—but he’s too lean.
When one has become accustomed to his large and
portly Benham he takes not kindly to a thinner
man. Mesdames Nina Osborne, Beatrice Perry
and Minnie Everett were fairies for the nonce'
Celia, Lelia, and Fieta of Mddsuromor’s demesne.

and distinguished savoir faire —was held revallv
aloft by Mrs. Henry Bracy.
*# * *
At Sydney Lyceum the Holloway Co. has
elaborately staged “Man’s Enemy, ’’which turns out
to be mostly the same old Der-rink in a new disguise.
The Bulletin’s theory is that the original serpent
who crawled into Eden was named Der-rink
in Hebrew, or Chaldaic, or a mixture of the
two, and that he didn’t crawl, he slid down
Adam s throat with a loving gug-guggle and
inspired the First Man to challenge the landlord
to come out and fight. In “Man’s Enemy ” the
Holloway Co. has an unparalleled opportunity, or
something like that, to point out the beauties of
Der-rink, but theco. is far too much concerned about
its acting to supply the noble moral lesson which
the piece provides. It isn’t a dress-circle piece,
but it fills the gallery, and mingles laughs and
shudders with an admirable disregard of anything
in the way of the higher criticism. There is a
ballet of statues, also a wedding of tableaux.
Robert Inman and Stanford are concerned in the
play as hero and villain respectively; and they
thrive. Amy Gourlay and Ida Gresham supply
feminine graces agreeably : and there are so many
exclt i n ß incidents” that the audience feels at
the end as if it had never known how to be thirsty
before.
