“Othello” is still booming at the Criterion.
George has been showing the salt of the earth
how to strangle its wife. Last Thursday was a
“Command Night,” and the Chief Justice and
Lieut.-Governor and Captain-General of N.S.W.
went to study crime out of Court. On Friday the
other George and some Ministers (which sounds
like a title invented by John Oliver Hobbes)
dropped in to pick up a point or two from lago.
Reid, however, left chuckling, with a large, fat
aspect of out-Machiavellianing the Moor’s scurvy
Ancient. Meanwhile, people write letters to the
“ ail y Papers about editing Shakespeare (and
Walter Bentley thought he had a word to sav
thereto), the mode of Othello’s murdering Desde-
mona, and the archaeological correctness of turban,
ear-rings, and Oriental gowns.

always jammed full : to wit, the Tivoli. This

mg matinees. Contortionist Manuel Woodson is

** * *
Friedenthal, if not exactly “one of the
greatest of living pianists,” is one of the most
acceptable entertainers that musical Melbourne
has heard for years. Friedenthal takes the stage
tor about two hours, less ten minutes’interval, and
makes his piano interesting all the time, for he
plays nothing that is very heavy, and much that
is light and bright, or quaint. As an incentive to
amateur proficiency, this unaggressive German
artist will deserve all the popularity he earns.
1 here are jiianists—too many of them—who bid for
the priggish praise of pedantic critics by playing
sonatas of the kind which no ordinary performer
has the least desire to attempt, pieces which repre-
sent merely the composer’s ability to deliver a
long, dry sonorously-phrased sermon on a dull
theme. And there are piano-smiters travelling on
their power to execute ordinarily difficult pieces
m an extraordinarily difficult style, artists of the
acrobatic, magnetic order, who leave only a
brief sensation behind them. Friedenthal
deals, as a rule, with music that all prac-
tised pianists can play pleasantly, and he
shows them _ how much better they can
do it if they will only imitate him up to the point
where the amateur stops and the professional
begins. A Friedenthal tends to create reasonable
ambition m the average young female pianist.
When one remembers how much misery is wrought
by the misdoings of the girl next door, the im-
portance of this long-haired exemplar seems truly
great. Life in many cases is endurable, or other-
wise, according to the music of the girl next door
Herr ZAPF (Fitzgerald Bros.' trainer) at
practice with PRINCE, the lion, and LIZZIE,
the elephant, on the “Tricycle."
Miss Amy Castles, the Bendigo warbler,
presented to Melb. audiences in loose hair and a
rather short frock, was born at Capel-street, Melb.,
m Ju!y, 1880, says the register. This comes
rather rough on the enthusiastic scribesses (in-
cluding a Bulletin scribess) who put Miss Amy’s
age down as 17, and gave her credit for looking
two years younger, which she certainly doesn’t,
it is even rougher on the Age musical critic who
told the public to think of the little woman as a
growing girl,” with a great operatic career before
her. Amy Castles, according to the Age man,
may be expected to develope into another Tiet-
jens. The present writer, having a lively recollec-
tion ot that famous 14st. (or more) prima-donna
pouring volumes of music from her mighty chest,
would like to know whether the Age critic ever
saw and heard Tietjens, and, if so, whether he
would like to withdraw the comparison, now that
he knows little Miss Amy to be “rising 19”?
Both she and her voice are full-grown. The
size of her voice has been over-stated to the
same extent that her age has been understated,
but any further argument against the Castles boom
were a mere waste of time. At her second concert
the girl s voice was very hoarse through overwork;
the consciousness of failure made her nervous, and
she got out of tune, but the crowded audience, for
the most part, accepted Amy’s performance as
wonderful—“wonderful for a child” anyhow—and
next morning’s newspapers credited her with
another legitimate triumph, etc. Hoarser and
hoarser still she sang at other places within the
next few days, and the public took her voice at
newspaper valuation every time. Then a wise
patron rushed upon the scene and wrung from the
damsel a solemn promise not to sing any more, in
public or private, until some farewell benefit
concerts fall due. Her “wonderful” voice was
putting a terrible strain on public credulity when
benefit-promoter came along and persuaded
Miss Amy to preserve a diplomatic silence.

Writes “ Aletes ” to The Bulletin :
The Sydney Criterion “ Othello ” illustrates clearly the
power of an actor to make or mar the effect of another
Than lago and Othello there are not, probably, in dra-
matic literature, two parts so closely interwoven and of
so equal an importance. The occasion requires twin
stars In this case, because lago fails to rise to the
dignity of bis part, Othello’s runs the risk of seeming dis-
proportioned overdone. The latter should form a natural
sequence to the former, but here the effect seems rather
too great for the cause. In reading the play, the ade-
quacy of the circumstances comes home to one. How
vividly before us flames the character of lago—that
unique creation, with the shrewd, subtle brain, the
fiendish heart. A lover of villainy for its own sake.
rr’, not A how miserable—to himself, at least—the motives
that lead to such hideous achievement). In company are
the alert, keen-witted, consummate actor—alone, himself
—and the plotter, nakedly revealed. And, for redeeming
features? Well, his unfailing humor—bitter, cynical, aye,
and Qften revolting yefc, withal, humor. And his cheer-

There appears to be a great demand at present
in Victoria for all kinds of bolts and nuts. Mr. Mephan
Ferguson, engineer, &c., of Melbourne, is working night
and day in this department in order to execute the orders
on hand. The boiler-makers’ shop is also very busy just
now supplying orders for Western Australia, and alto-
gether the general foundry business in Melbourne seems
to be improving.*

have paid over £200,000 in divs. Here is the list to thp
end of March Long Tunnel, £1,227,1Q0 ; Garden Gully,
£751,618; Long Tunnel Ex., £489,120 ; Gt. Ex. Hustlers,

£308,820; Star of the East,' £276,000 ; South German Reef,

after paying £116,893.

The amended cyanide patent has been declared
valid by a majority of the Judges of Vic. Supreme Court.
Privy Council to be appealed to. The patent expires in
1901. Ten years after that time an authoritative legal
dictum may be expected.

The proposed revised Imperial duties will put
an increased 6d. per gal., or Is. per dozen, on all light
wine, including Australian. A move is being made to in-
duce the British Treasurer to differentiate in favor of
Australia, but it is quite unlikely to succeed.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Westralian stocks still boom, the rise of the
last few weeks representing millions of money, in som
instances the advances made during the last 14 m o
are absolutely phenomenal. This day twelve mont
Associateds were £3 Bs., to-day they are quoted at ±.7 ids.,
the difference in price being over two millions. Great
Boulders were then under 18s. —now they are just unde
30s. Ivanhoes, then under £5, are now over £ll. Lane
View Consols were just over £9 ; they are now just uwer
£lB, and £9 a share means just two and a quarter millions
sterling. Perseverance, a year ago, were just halt tneir
present price. Kalgurlies have almost doubled ; so, too.
South Kalgurlies, as well as the stocks of several
smaller mines in same belt of country. Little 'nines,
long thought dead and forgotten, have come out ot tneir
graves like rats out of their holes and are being honored
with market quotations. Shares that could riot be given
away a fortnight ago are negotiable for cash. The chances
are that a few weeks hence people will not be able to give
them away again, and holders will be sorry they bought
them at any price. The stock-boom is unaccountable.
Up to to-day writer has not seen any logical reason given
for it, anyhow. Developments have been good, particularly
in the Great Boulder, where the two bottom levels are
opening up remarkably well, but nothing in this direction
has occurred to warrant the boom, and outside the
Boulder nothing remarkable has happened in any ot the
mines. Sulphide plants in the Lake View and in one or
two other mines are said to be running, but their success
cannot be greater than that anticipated.
Mining generally lias been cheapened during
the last 12 months, but the limits of economy are now
surely being approached. Divs. by the chief producers
are great, but they only about represent bank-interest on
market-values ; and, between bank-interest and what
should be mining-interest, there is a mighty big differ-
ence, as the public who are buying at present prices will
find out ere long. The boom is unwarranted, however you
look at it. Probably it is not over yet—there may still be
“ pounds a share ” in ’em, but, as sure as the sun rises, a
slump will follow. Then, stand from under ! It is well-
known that the public have a mania for buying.m a rising
market and for selling in a falling one, and it is when
they stand not on the order of going out that the
rush will take place. Nobody in Australia quite
knows when that will happen, but that the time rests,
as it were, in the hollow of certain people’s hands there
is not much reason to doubt. Some six months ago, in
the face of developments on which the present boom has
been built, and in defiance of magnificent results in the
cyanidation of tailings and the filter-press treatment of
slimes, the London operators who had determined to jump
on W.A. stocks got them down in several notable
instances, particularly Associated, to about half present
prices. The miserable Fashoda incident was given in
London and accepted in Australia as a sufficient reason
for the slump, in which Ifalgoorlie brokers and mining-
managers, wiio, by reason of their location and oppor-
tunities, could best judge the value of the mines, "'ere
caught. Having, no doubt, got in at the bottom, the
same operators maybe depended upon to be dealing them
out to the public at the top. They engineered the slump ;
then the boom, and it rests entirely with them to bring
about another downfall the moment it suits them.
Peakhill, Westralia. 250,000 £1 shares, of which
all but 20,090 are issued, and they are now selling in
London at £9 a share. A market-value of over two mil-
lions —for what? It paid last year 2s. Bd. a share in divs.,
which represents something under 2 per cent, on its
market-value. Last year it was running 10 heads ; this
year it has started a new 20-head mill, but it is extremely
doubtful if it can keep both running. Formation, a sort
Of bedded plane of conglomerate kaolin cement, with
occasional small veins or splashes of quartz—usually
short-lived as they are rich. The co. has to fight the
question as to whether it is alluvial or not. It will pro"
bably win, but winning will cost heaps of money, the
big fight, instead of terrifying holders, seems to have had
an opposite effect. No true lode has been found, lhe
yarn that the cement must be over-lying formations is
rich, as the Kalgoorlie belt has been proved moonshine—
the cement has not been found to extend to any great
width, length, or depth ; is, in fact, confined to a narrow
channel, and the best part of it is dipping into blocks for
the working of which the co. has not got full control.
Yet the mine has a market-value almost equal to the
biggest of the Boulder group, while its production is not
one-fifth as big ; its divs. are insignificant by com-
parison, while its life might reasonably be put down
!is ten times as short. This altogether is the
most extraordinary thing happening in Westralia,
and must either be the result of a well-manipulated
pool in the shares, or remarkable credulity on the part of
the people buying. It could never have occurred had the
mine been in Kalgoorlie or within reasonable distance of
it. Isolated over 200 miles to the N.E. of Oue, with
nothing in the surrounding district to attract speculators,
with no hope of floating mines in the present condition of
the London market, it has been allowed to work out its
own salvation in its own way. What will happen when
the pool is over, if there is a pool, or when the public in
London realise what they are really getting for £9 a share
it is not difficult to contemplate, despite the assurances
of Moses Moss, of London Truth, and other professional
mining’ tipsters, that Peakhills will see £l2 and £l5.
Great Boulder, W.A. Proposed to issue 150,000
reserved shares, giving nine and a fraction for every 100
shares now held. Price to be asked not yet known;
private cables say that they are to be issued at a low
figure. Reason for issue not given. Surely the great
G B 00. does not want any more money. Holders, how-
ever are always ready to take more shares when they can
be had for nothing or cheaply. Probably a sop to stave
off the storm arising over the ignominious failure of the
directors’ milling contract with Konemaiin. And how
quickly that seems to have died out! For months we
had columns of Konemann-process for which the directors
were wickedly responsible. Now failure is proved,
£2O 000 of the co.’s money wasted, and a year s time lost,
shareholders seem to have forgotten all about it.
* • * *
“Rottnest”: While sympathising thoroughly
with the very general Westralian demand (8., 25/3/99) to
tax the absentee shareholder who owns the best of the
mines, I cannot see how an export tax on gold will effect
this result, except on the Herodian principle of killing
every baby so that the one wanted cannot escape. Ihe
export tax on gold would penalise every Westralian trans-
action with the outer world which involved the purchase
of a draft in W.A. The banks would raise their rate of
exchange for everybody, including the absentee. It w a
mistake to suppose that the W.A. gold-export is "'holly
on account of absentees. Vie., N.S.W., Q., Tas. and M.L.
export gold, simply because it would be absurd to keep
sovereigns in hand beyond the needs of local currency.
The system is automatic. The traders of a city work up
to a certain average—they draw out of the banks
enough for cash transactions, and they leave in
the bank all the surplus. The banker has his idea of
what margin he must allow beyond this average of re-
quirements. All beyond that margin would represent
dead capital—and to ship it to London, or to America, or
to the East, is a reasonable and ordinary business trans-
action, without any poisonous effect on the trade of the
citv concerned. In fact, Australia sells gold to the world
To the miner, an export-duty would mean that he would
get less cash for his gold, or, perhaps, that he would
nominally get the same; but the purchasing power of
nominally get/ re duced. Now, what is wanted is to
taxtoedividends of the absentee shareholder—not to tax
tax the mviaenua t he Australian trade
the miner or the America ; “ Xhe way to
in gold. me .. T he way to tax the dividends of
resume is to resume. ine t he dividends of the
absenteTshareholder. Victoria actually exported in coin
dnrino-1897 much more than it produced and imported in
bullion. The figures are, perhaps, interesting enough to

Goodman, of W.A. District Trading Corpora-
tion, London, has gone to gaol for 13 months. One of his
associates got six months and two others got off alto-
gether. No wonder moderately honest men turn boom-
sters when such an amount of villainy, as publicly Prac-
tised for two years in this instance, is rewarded by 18
months gaol for the chief. A few of the tricks played by
Goodman and co. have been enumerated heretofore
Amongst those not generally known was the sending out
of hospital nurses who found themselves landed in the
Westralian bush without money or hospital, and were not
long ere they realised there was no hope of getting either,
It was part of Goodman’s scheme, and when writer com-
pares his 18 months’ quod with the sufferings he inflicted
on some of his victims and the money he robbed from
others, he feels inclined to say something—well, unparlia-
mentary. »
The recklessness (vide cable) of the “ West Aus-
tralian (Gold District) Trading Syndicate Lira.,’ swindle
was remarkable, even for these times. The felony lay in
the payment of a bogus div. of 100 per cent, on syndicate
shares, for the purpose of enlarging the syndicate into a
“corporation,” and enabling the robbers to wade off their
inflated shares. It doesn’t seem to have occurred to them
that, in a multitude of shareholders, there was a certainty
of a great uproar when the fraud of the 100 per cent. div.
came to be discovered. Bull will stand any amount of
bleeding, as long as he isn’t punctured in too many places
at once? He mustn’t be made to bellow with the pain of
several hundreds of wounds, for, when he bellows, some-
body goes to gaol. In the matter of achieving occasional
consolation for his losses, the speculative Bull has an
advantage over the Kangaroo, who makes no row to speak
of.
* * .
Claude Vautin, the well-known Australian
metallurgical chemist, has got six months in London for
certain scrip fakings—a game he probably knew little
about. He was a smart man at his own business, anyhow,
but, as a rule, the best of metallurgical chemists are
babes in any other line. If he wanted to make money
and escape gaol he should have forged mines wholesa e,
floated “cats,”and started the printing-press making scrip
in a perfectly legal way—quite a common business, but
little understood by metallurgists.
* * * *
G. Darlington Simpson, a Charlton (Eng.) man,
who has blossomed into a mining plutocrat, owes his
success to the famous patch at Peak Hill, 200 miles north
of Cue the desolate. His first ventures on the Murchison
were failures, and the Bull co’s with which he was asso-
ciated dropped their coin. On two of their mines J)-
head batteries were erected, but very little has been
crushed at either. He got wind of Peak Hill, and in-
duced the holders of leases to give him the option tor
three months without a deposit. With a collection of
rich specimens from the place he headed straight
for London, and worked things so well that the
cash deposit was cabled out almost at the last moment.
The price was £llO,OOO, and the co. was floated with a
great flourish for £250,000. The Inspector of Mines,
Frank Reid, gave a glowing report, and he has since
resigned to manage the show at £2OOO a-year. By judi-
cious pooling, or some other process, the shares have
forced up to £B, so that the capitalised value of the Peak
Hill Co. is two millions !
* * * *
Westralia’s timber-export business grows. In
1897 it was worth £192,451 ; in ’9B, £326,195. And yet
timber-scarcity is troubling managers, who
begin to talk of the need of light lines thrown out
towards the nearest wooded areas.
• * * *
The annual report of the once-mighty Burra
(S A ) mines shows no trace of the copper-boom. The
receipts were just £656 18s. 3d., of which £4B 19s. 2d.
was the proceeds of ore sold. Expenses, £772. But, m a
dreamv sort of way, the directors hope to do a little more
in the way of exploration by and by. The co. owns the
mine—holds the freehold of it: that is the misfortune.
The Central (Sulphide Corporation) having sold
its “ tailings ” dump to European zinc-extraction people,
the South Co.’s big heap is now being nibbled at. some-
thing like £50,000 has been offered and refused. Apart
from this, South’s prospects continue first-rate ; a bonus
will probably be available with dividend.
B.H. British pays two sixpenny divs. early in
May—a total of £15,000. This is a notable recovery; the
previous half-year showed a loss—chiefly through the
vagaries of the concentrating-mill. Better mill-work,
better sulphides in the faces, and an increased extraction
of carbonates are responsible for the right-about turn.
The British has had awful bad luck (‘ bad luck m
minin" mostly means “bad management”) ; but if this
ceases" there should be a long procession of small divs.
No Broken Hill mine has more country to explore, and
few have lately had better results from what they have
explored. To this has to be added that the British has,
so far as can be judged, more carbonate ore left than any
other mine except the B.H. Prop. ; and carbonate is in
very strong demand for fluxing sulphides with.
Euriowie, on whose tin-shows <T. S. Reid, Rasp,
and others lost a good slice of the money they had made
out of Broken Hill, is to get another trial. But Melb.
and Adelaide aren’t taking any, and the best, or a good
many of the best, of the shows have been taken to Lon-
don If J. Bull won’t part freely enough Pans will be
tried- reports from there are even more encouraging
than from across the Channel. German tin-saving appli-
ances are said to have been much improved lately (that,
in fact, is one of the chief arguments used for the revival
of Cornish tin-mining’), so that the former dismal failure
of Euriowie enterprises may now be reversed. Anyhow,
the former trials were not satisfactory ; for the three or
four-score tin-pot co.’s hadn’t half-finished experimenting
when their capital was exhausted and the bottom fell out
of the little boom. If they had pooled their capital there
would have been just about enough to prove the field one
way or the other. , , ,
What is the English co. which has its hand on
so much of the Union gold-country in the Northern Terri-
tory doing ? D. D. Rosewarne, ex-Govt. Mining Inspec-
tor in S. A., is still up there ; work has presumably been
proceeding for four or five years ; great prospects have
several times been recorded —and yet there is no news of
gold Some Territoreans wink when the co. is men-
tioned. What is the game, if there is one ?

Cobar still suffers from drought. Unless rain
falls within a few days the great Copper Mine will have
to apply once more to Cobar Gold Mines for some of the
water in “ Lake O’Gorman.” The Great Western is dead
iust now, with the exception that it is erecting, a very
miniature cyanide plant to treat a still more miniature
heap of coppery tailings. # ,
Bingara diamonds, N.S.W. Published that
Capt. Rogers has sold his famous Monte Christo mine to a
diamond man from Kimberley at a “satisfactory figure-
Writer has long waited to know of a mine being sold at an
unsatisfactory figure. Had the captain got out as many
diamonds as anticipated by the Sabbath Shellback some
two years ago, he would have smashed the De Beers dia-
mond ring.
* * • •
Albury, N.S.W., having plenty of water round
it, is not going to be left out of the dredging-boom. It
has been found that the particular ground referred to
will not pay with ordinary gold-savers, but, with a dredge
—well, that is quite another matter. Sydney mining-
investors, it is declared, propose to erect a dredging-plant
that will cost £60,000. This is really tew mutch. The
figures are rather high, but, associated with Sydney
mining-investors, they constitute a huge joke, of which a
Sydney daily has been made the victim.
* * * *
Victory Forest Reefs : Plenty of good reports ;
seldom a wash-up and never a div. The latest is that
some other man’s half-oz. wash is dipping into its ground.
Not a word as to what has become of its own.

North Nymagee (N.S. W.). The manager “ cal-
culates” that the lode in the 200 ft. level will carry 6 per
cent, copper as broken. Other parts of the mine seem to
be looking as poor as ever. The shares are next to no-
thing. The writer hopes in time to sympathise lyith this
mine.

From a N.S.W. blue-book of 1882 : As ex-
amples of the richness of gold-veins the following may be
cited. At Beyers and Holtermann’s mine, Hill End, 102
cwt. gold was raised in 10 tons of stuff. From the same
mine a slab of vein stuff and gold weighing 6301 b., which
was estimated to contain £2OOO worth of gold, was exhi-
bited with other similarly rich blocks. The Mint returns
for the gold from 415 tons of vein stuff from this mine
was 16,279’630z., valued at £63,234 125., in 1873. Kroh-
man’s Co., also at Hill End, raised 436 tons 9 cwt. of ore
for 24,0790 z, Bdwt. gold, value £93,616 11s. 9d.
* • * *
A N.S.W. mining-expert visited a Northern
field recently, and talked so large that he was forthwith
engaged at a high fee by a local co. to inspect and report
on their mine, where they had the refractory-ore disease
acutely. His report wound up by informing the directors
that “ they had not got the right compliances for saving
gold.” On another field, principally worked by old fos-
sickers, same genius nearly caused a civil war by asserting
that it was useless sinking any further on a certain line
of reef, as they were down to ophidian, and gold was
never found with ophidian.

stated, there is enough tin to keep from three to tour

working of a tin-field. It is also stated that some Vic-
torians have invaded some part of N.S.W. near the border,
and are engaged in taking up a large area of alleged tin-
land - , . * *
« F.J.E.”: Be “ R.’s ” letter (8/4/’99). Easy to
criticise the management of a big concern if yon arenit
called upon to propound a better scheme. Good oia
Kayser ” managed Bischoff mine for upwards of 20 years,
took out over three millions sterling worth of stuff, paid
about £1,600,000 in divs., and “made” more gentlemen
than any other manager in Tasmania. Despite the heavy
rainfall at Waratah, some of the co.’s plant has to remain
inactive at times owing to scarcity of water, and were it
not for the manner in which the machinery is built up a
good deal of the essential fluid would run to waste. iffe
great strength of old man Kayser’s management lies not
merely in the fact that he gets out the ore, but that he
dresses it with economy and keeps divs. up right through
the year. “ R.” opines that “ any good tin-sluicer could
have managed Bischoff with less show than Kayser, which
is rather rough on those who managed the mine before
Kayser and didn’t make it pay. Anyone interested has
onlv to look at the half-yearly reports, wherein full par-
ticulars as to cost of mining and dressing a ton or
stanniferous material are given. Considering the exor-
bitant freights obtaining on Waratah-Burme line, os. 7*d.
per ton isn’t showing a big margin for experimental pur-
poses. That the big mine has seen its best days is pro-
bably correct, yet with economical management the aivs.
may'outlive “ R.” although, to judge from his screed, he
isn’t an old ’un. Kayser hasn’t practically resigned ;he
sent in his resignation last year, but the directors, not
being acquainted with “ R.,” persuaded him to stay on,
being well aware that a new broom mightn’t sweep half as
many divs. together. Kayser recently reported on some
E. Coast properties—perhaps this fact has raised K. s
bile? ,
“Potai”: Be query as to payable lodes being
disclosed per medium of road and railway cuttings, I
know of three instances, all on Zeehan silver-fields. First,
Govt, road being put through one of Silver Queen P.A. s
Blocks resulted in discovery of No. 2 Queen Lode from
which £25,000 divs. was paid. Again, the Montana b.M.
Co. while laying a tram-line from main shaft to a small
show near by, exposed a rich lode in a small cutting which
enabled the mine to pay divs., 3d. ones, but still divs.
Also, Oonah S.M. Co. in laying tram for firewood, cut
payable body of ore distinguished as the Western Show or
Lode; existence of these lodes in no case previously
known. , , * .
Two more N.Q. mines gone into J. Bull’s maw.
The Sweet William copper-mine and the Tyrconnel gold-
mine, an old and good Hodgkinson show.

Ed. Bulletin.—Re your note (25/2/’99) concern-
ing the big find of opal near Winton (Q.), your informant
is all abroad in saying that H. Newman’s representative
bought the pick of it, or that the remainder was commer-
cially worthless. I have some of the stone m Sydney
with me, and have shown it to several experts who have
been quite willing to buy. The remainder is now on the
field for sale. Looks as if some interested parties were
trying to crush the field by representing that it is most
difficult to reach and destitute of accommodation, whereas
Cobb and Co. run a weekly service thither, so that a
buyer can stay on the field three to ten days, and the
hotel is a fair one for an outside place. Besides the big
find there are some smaller parcels of first-rate quality on
the field.— Opal Miner.

Quantity of gold raised for ’9B in Queensland
was 918,1060 z. ; quantity exported for same period,
944 9060 z. ; worth £2,885,881. Total raised in Q. to date,
12 911,5690 z. ; valued at £45,190,491. At end of ’97 there
were over 18,000 sq. miles of auriferous ground being
worked, and in this area were known to be 2512 gold-bear-
ing’ reefs exclusive of alluvial areas. Machinery on fields
was valued at £1,067,106 ; number of people directly sup-
ported by mining was 63,229, or about one-eighth of the
total population.

The British Govt, has often, as a matter of
policy, differentiated its wine-duties in the way Australia
now urges in respect to its own little vintage. In 1703, a
50 per "cent, higher duty was placed on French than on
Portuguese wines, with the result that French wines, in a
few years, almost fell out of use in England, and Portuguese
took their place, Wines from the Cape and other British
possessions were also favorably differentiated. In 182
the duty on French wines was 13s. 9d. per gal., other
Continental wines 9s. Id,, and Cape wines 3s. fd. in
1860 all were equalised. Differential duties on stimu-
lants and narcotics have always been a matter of policy as
much as of revenue with Great Britain.
* * * *
According to Mr. Carroll, M.P., 700 of the 800
hotels in Sydney are “ tied ” houses belonging to brewers.
The Austral Otis Engineering Co. Ltd., South
Melb., has completed and delivered a very fine set of
winding engines for the Cobar Gold Mines, N.S.W., capable
of hauling a load of two tons from 1500 ft. at a speed of
1000 ft. per minute. The engines are a pair of 14 cylin-
ders geared to 7ft. loose drum, and are fitted with link
motion reversing gear, vertical indicators, and powerful
brake gear. The pair of engines are set to one side of the
drums, and all the driving levers are brought behind the
drums. The co. has also just delivered a fine set of
pumping plant for the Paddy Lackey mine for taking thp
shaft down to 1000 ft.
* * ♦ *
Alex. Macdonald, “King of Klondyke,” has
had his 78 gold-mines valued by the Govt, assessors of the
Dominion at over £27,000,000. The properties include
the famous Eldorado and Shookuro groups. The yearly
output is above a million sterling. Macdonald was
married the other day to the eldest daughter of Supt.
Chisholm, of Thames (London) River Police.

“D.”: Govt. Geologist Stirling and Govt.
Metallurgist Jenkins (recently imported) with the irre-
pressible Begelhole, arrived at Mt. Deddick alleged mines
23rd March, and departed on 26th. What they expected
to do in two days I cannot imagine. A competent
geologist (Ferguson) with two assistants spent the first
half of last year industriously exploring the field, and his
report has never been published. Why doesn t Minister
Foster, the member for Deddick, publish that report in
justification of his unsuccessful appeal for a Deddick rail-
way? . • ,
There is a lull in the dredging boom; public
have neither time nor money to be engaged in two booms
at once, and the Westralian stock-boom is occupying the
whole of their attention. The two or three dredging
schemes floated in Sydney have marketable shares, and
the public have the assurance of the Sabbath tipster that
they are all going up.
* ♦ * •
The racecourse at Johannesburg for many years
was never thought to have any mining value, but the
“ deep levels ” have extended right out there, and now
original-members’ rights are worth about £5OOO a-piece.
Was ever so much money made on the turf as under it
in this instance? Many Yanks and a few Australians are
in the thick of the Rand stock boom, which is greater
than the two or three others preceding it. F. Drake,
formerly mining manager at Lucknow, and who before
that looked after the metallurgical end of several Aus-
tralian silver-mines, is a director on eleven boards of
Rand mines, the united value of which at ruling market
prices comes out at 17J millions—a little fact which, while
pleasing to some people, will undoubtedly make others
squirm. * , * *
A man just over from W.A. has the cheek to
tell this story—and show the slugs in proof. While work-
in a non-div.-paying claim he struck three fine slugs,
which he immediately secreted on his person. Coming to
the top, before being searched he coolly took out the
slugs, and, placing them one by one on the table, said:
“ Well, that’s mine ; that’s mine ; and that’s mine. Now
you can go on with the search.” The slugs could not be
identified as stolen, so all they could do was to sack him—
which they did, quick and lively !

• a . wee k After Gov. Tennyson’s arrival
in Adelaide, hardly a day passed without a con-
gratulatory and grovelsorne ode being printed in
one or other of the dailies. One of the most
hideous was. the effusion read from the stage of
Adelaide Royal by Wybert Reeve. The Governor
is himself to blame for a>l this, in that he went
out of his way, in his first speech, to express
admiration for 8. A. poetical talent, specially men-
tioning several local bards whose claims to im-
mortality are poor indeed.
* * * *
What hope either in this world or the next
for the paper (B.A. Register) which solemnly
prints such verses as these?—
There is a Governor somewhere in Aus-
tralia who occasionally exhibits his domestic
authority in the open. The other day his better
(much better) half ordered a small barrow-load of
trifles from the stall-holders at a semi-charity
bazaar to be delivered at the vice-regal mansion,
lhe articles were trotted along, and His Ex. met
them on the mat and glared at them wildly with
a my-God-can-this-be-true light in his eyes. Then
he repudiated the bundle with uncalled-for heat
and refused to allow himself to be ruined bv this
mad extravagance. So the bundle was returned
to the bazaar where charity now sits, moulting
and disconsolate.

cases, became cliques. But Barton revived the
old style. Young didn’t like crushes; four at a

long line. He has practically knocked off mem-
bers dinners and seldom slakes an orator’s thirst
I his, to many members, is the deadliest of sins. ’

At a recent Bendigo
(Vic.) Rechabite din-
ner some thirsty speak-
er of common sense told
the saints that when
they replaced cold tea,
lemonade, ginger-pop and all the
other abominations with a tem-
perance drink more exhilarating
than wine, more quenchful than
the long beer, more biting than
rum, and generally more de-
lightful than theaccursed Der-
rink at the same price, the
“Cause” would boom something —i
tremendjus. And some sensible
Rechabites agreed with him. The satis-
fying teetotal drink will come with the mil-
lennium and the discovery of the source of per-
petual motion.
J.D. : gaol-yard identifica-
tions. A man I know lately had a
valueless cheque passed upon him.
Himself and son of 15 were taken
to police-station to identify a man
arrested on suspicion. The father
could not identify, but the youngster
did so positively from amongst "four
others. Afterwards father asked son
how he could be so sure, and that hope-
ful replied: “Well, yer see, I noo
all the others; Splodger Barkly,
Casey’s old swabber-out, and two other
old boozers from The Queen’s. I could
pick the one left all right.”
* * * *
‘ ‘ Commercial ” : Re police
identification. Some time ago a man
tried to pass a forged cheque on me.
He was afterwards arrested, and I was
taken to identify him. Before en-
tering the gaol, the constable (who evi-
dently wanted to make sure of a
“case”) was kind enough to tell me
what color clothes he was wearing, so
that I could easily pick him out of’the
rank ranged up. I thought it was a
bit too strong, so I didn’t identify
him. J

During the temporary absence
of a pupil teacher from a N.S.W. pub-
lic school, a fashionable young lady
visitor was one afternoon placed in
charge of the infant class, and dictated
the following, in her very naicest
voice: Wives,” “mice,” “tiles”
rice, kite, and, on receiving
the slates for correction, found to
her surprise, that the juveniles had
written—“ Waves, mace, tales, race,
Kate. When asked if they had
never “seen the cat after maice ”
they replied, “No, miss; we never
seen no maces,” and when the
teacher wrote “mice” on the black-
board, the benighted brats exclaimed
Oh, we know them, Miss, but you
said ‘ maice.’ ”

When a white man marries a half-caste
aboriginal he should support her. But if, whether
through being a loafer or out of employment he
doesn t, does the half-caste woman lose her claim
on the Govt, subsidy? That is the troubleN S W
Aborigines Board recently tackled and unani-
mously answered in the affirmative. The answer
is unsatisfactory It means that if a gin marries a
black man hen claim continues, but if a white one
she loses it. The grant to aborigines cuts no such
fine distinctions, and the Board is not such a sue-
cess that it should be allowed to stray from the
letter of the law. What the Board has in its
head is to make the white husband maintain his
wife. But this is not the Board’s province
Meanwhile, the full-blooded or half-caste wife’s
claim on rations is a legal one.

The Dungey-Bromley conspiracy scandal
is not yet securely buried, as Vic. Crown Law
department would like to believe, for it seems
that the smellful action of Mr. Panton
P.M., at least will be discussed on the floor of
Parliament. Pwo or three members are named as
being 1 ready to take the matter up vigorously at
the suggestion of Castlemaine M‘Cay, the man
who fought Govt, and beat it badly on the Vac-
cination Act. M’Cay is a deadly marksman, and
Attorney-General Isaacs always winces when he
stands up to shoot.

The woman Potter—witnes.s in the Dungey-
Bromley farce, at Melb. City Police-court-was
brought from Pentridge in prison uniform. The
same is the case with prisoners being transferred
from Melb. to up-country gaols. As the Vic. Law
authorities are so fond of flying to England for
precedents, why don’t they follow in this, the more
humane British custom, of allowing a transferred
prisoner, or one brought from gaol to give evidence
to wear his own clothes? Also, why shouldn’t
accompanying constables be in plain clothes?
I hey have the “bracelets” and the revolver if
required.

At the adjourned inquest on Jessie Bent-
ley, who lately suicided by cyanide of potassium, it
was remarked as a “ peculiar fact ” that she had
washed her feet and changed her clothing, by
way of preparation for “being found dead in a
seemly clean condition.” But there is nothing at
all peculiar” about Jessie Bentley’s action.
Women invariably put on clean underclothing
when they go abroad from home—in view of acci-
dent giving the hospital-nurses an opportunity of
noting the condition of their linen. Woman
dreads the other woman. The sex’s opinions as
to the manner in which one of its number is
dressed, or is washed, is as abjectly feared as is its
dictum on morals. Writer once heard of a man
who, weathering a terrible period of storm and
financial stress, broached suicide to his wife
“Very well, Bill,” she replied, “I’ll do it with
r°u' fl I }'? glve me tlme t 0 chan £ e all my clothes.
I should like to be found clean and properiy dressed
when I’m dead.” Whereupon the humor of the
situation struck Bill.

WELCOME-LORD TENNYSON.
The Southern breezes softly blow
’Neath early sunrise roseate glow,
All Nature seems to whisper low,
Welcome, welcome, Tennyson.
The feathered songster sweetly signs
An Austral paean of welcome rings
As ocean palace quickly brings . ’
Baron Hallam Tennyson.
Now the bells are brightly ringing
Hear the merry children 'singing
For the boat is swiftly bringing
Baron Hallam Tennyson?
Young Athens of the Southern Pole,
“ Artistic, cultured, full of soul,”
Ad’laide, thus your praises roll
From Baron Tennyson.
Lofty words,"and noble praise,
Potent influence to raise
Austral s sons to noble ways,
Thank you, Baron Tennyson.
For lofty aims and noble deeds
Will build a nation more than creeds,
Bright words to help us in our needs,
Wisely spoken, Tennyson.
A GOOD PEN'ORTH
If the truth must be told, the lawn fete for the benefit of the Mission Church fund of Dahrswahr was not
going too well, It was decidedly "show," and people MUST be got to talk somehow. The new missionary
oppreached the canital belle, and more for the sake of conversation than anything else, observed pleasantly:
"A penny for your thoughts, miss!"
"I was thinking," she replied, naively, "whether you would be better stewed over a slow fire, like the last
pracker man, or staffed with bread-fruit and baked whole for a change. YOu gib it penny-what?"
Then, welcome, Baron Tennyson
A noble Father's worthy son,
You have our hearts, the battle’s won,
Welcome, welcome, Tennyson.
Long we lov’d your noble sire,
We now to your respect aspire,
Our hearts have all but one desire,
To love our Hallam Tennyson.

BRISBANE, QUEENSLAND.
On one day of Easter encampment of Q.
volunteer-forces, at Toowoomba, the headquarters
band mutinied, and was ordered back to barracks,
where instruments and side-arms were taken
away, and the members sent back to Brisbane.
The incident occurred during the march through
the main street. The Brisbane men were aggrieved
that Toowoomba band should have precedence in
the order of march, and refused to strike up,
whereupon the parade was halted, and returned to
camp. Hundreds of spectators witnessed the
affair, yet not one Q. paper said a word about it.
ONCE BITTEN.
TIMID LASS: “Won’t you come, my brother:
Salvation is free.”

W.A. is blessed with a Public Works De-
partment that “ lays over ” everything else of the
kind in Australia. A sample of its work is the
new observatory. The observatory site is the
best possible— i.e., the highest part of Perth. But
the department was instructed to build quarters
for the astronomer. This was done at a cost of
<£7500 (and astronomer Cook has to live in them
with a screw of £5OO a year). The quarters are a
massive, imposing two-storey structure, with a
tower built so as to block a big portion of the
northern sky from the telescope in the observatory
proper !

WIFE (waiting up) : “Pooh! drunk again!”
HUBBY (leeringly): “No, m’dear, not ‘boot-trunk
again,’ only toddy.”
A donkey and a pony, fco be hired by chil-
dren, have appeared on Manly Beach. Many,
many years ago there were many donkeys for hire
on the sands at Manly ; and the donkey-provi-
dores, having made much money, founded fami-
lies. The families were, of course, ashamed of the
donkeys, and have ever since, so it has long been
alleged, exetcised their potent influence in the
direction of having retail donkey-hiring inter-
dicted locally.
“ Constance C.” : Says a Sydney
daily: “ Why are workwomen, journey-
ing to and from their work by trains,
not allowed the same privileges with
regard to fares, etc., as are workmen?”
Which reminds writer that in Maoriland
once a politician advocated letting all
women travel for nothing. This might
do in M.L., where the men resemble
men, but in this land of the weed-loving
and weedy youth the number of travel-
ling women would unaccountably in-
crease, and dress-skirts to be hooked in
at a moment’s notice would come to be
a regular part of the economical
man’s outfit.

Dear Bulletin. —Bi - sexual
“ pigging-in ” is not unknown in Syd-
ney. For instance, 12 people occupy
a rookery near Woolloomooloo wharf,
containing two rooms and a kind of
kitchen, and their five children (both
sexes and all ages up to 14) are dumped
together into a cellar without super-
vision. Also, at Central Police Court
recently, a wife said she could not live
with tier husband at his mother’s
house because there were already 8
persons (with children) living there
in three rooms. —C.S.

The happy mediaeval time, when
a brute-beast might be tried, con-
victed and duly executed, might seem
to have returned at Croydon, N.S.W.,
the other day; but it was merely a
Solomonic question of ownership, dur-
ing the hearing of which goat and
magistrate forgathered on the bench —
magistrate examining goat’s mouth for
signs of age, and goat doubtless inves-
tigating magistrate’s shirt-cuffs with an eye to
chewableness.
* * • «
Ceeile Andersen (Vic.), the Danish woman
who shot a man from whom she had received gross
injury, and who was sentenced to life imprison-
ment, has now served five years in gaol. Many
think she has suffered enough. The circumstances
under which the crime was committed were of a
nature that appealed to public sympathy on behalf
of the offender. Her wrongs had undoubtedly
unhinged her judgment. Anyway, there was
primitive justice in her action. A movement is
afoot to have her sentence quashed, and the poor
woman released, and there is a chance that this
will be brought about. There are ways of collaring
the Law by the ear without invoking its machinery,
and this is a case in which they should be put in
action.

An Italian warship “struck” S.A. the
other day and fired the customary salute. It
happened that only one member of Largs Fort
garrison, the cook, happened to be home at the
time ; and as he wasn’t sure which were the right
guns and is frightened of firearms, anyhow, he took
a cab, hunted up the garrison, and the return
salute was fired about shrs. later, much to the
relief of the Italian commander, who was
beginning to fear that the local fire-eater had some
grudge against him.
A divorce-case was once settled in
Sydney with an axe as intervener. The
lady was charming, and she sued her
husband, an athlete and a son of Anak,
on the ground of faithlessness. Madam
eschewed all Yes-No considerations, her
mind was adamant. She instructed her
lawyers to go on to the very bitter, to
listen to no overtures of compromise. On
the morning set down for the hearing,
the lady’s sharks received peremptory
instructions to stop proceedings. They
rushed to her hotel, and were waited
upon by a bearded man with colossal
shoulders, and in his wake meekly fol-
lowed their little client, now and again
appealingly glancingat her consort. “You
must withdraw the case at once,” said
the big man. “ Yes, at once,” assented
the little woman. “ Don’t let it go a step
further. This is my husband. We have
made it up. He got into this hotel last
night—with—with an axe. He knocked
at the door of my room ; didn’t you,
Dick? I—l didn’t answer him. Then he
Called out my name; didn’t you, Dick?
And he tried the handle of the door. The
key was turned in the lock ; but he took
his axe and broke the lock off ; didn’t you,
Dick ? ” And there was nothing more to
be said.
In the Divorce Court the snide soli-
citor who battens on the lowest and most
debased of humanity’s instincts finds a
profitable and congenial arena. The
Judge in Divorce often labors to bring
mutually-weary man and woman back
into the conjugal fold ; but these eleventh-
hour reconciliations suit not the book of
the Gent One. The other day the Judge
postponed his decision in a suit so that
the parties should have a chance of mak-
ing it up. Quite by accident the male
person met his suing better-half in the
street, near the Court. Seeing a chancs
for an explanation, he asked his wife to
walk with him to the house, of/a mutual
friend and talk matters over. But hubby
was seen by Mr. Capias Sat., who rushed up hat-
less, and, drawing his client by the arm and
hurrying her into a cab, shouted, “ Don’t you
listen to him ! Don’t you listen to him ! ” And the
Judge’s kind intention was snapped in twain by
the shark, who had no perception for anything
but costs.

“Melb.” : A R.C. print is indignant at a
published statement that a number of nuns, of “ re-
gular ” orders, were conspicuous at a recent shivoo
aboard the Sunbeam. A genuine nun is never
allowed to attend any pleasure-making of the
kind, or desires to. On the other hand, it is just
where the Protestant nuns are at home. Brothers
Tadger and Snutflebottom are deliciously sarcastic
on their Wesleyanie sisters of this kind, the pretty
ones going off in wedlock like ripe cherries in the
market, appetised by their bibs, tuckers and veils,
while the pla-in-phizzers oh, so devoted ! You
can tell the genuine nun from the bogus half-'a-
mile off. The real article apparently never sees
you —the bogus one evidently alwavs does.

PERDITA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The sea-coast of Bohemia
Is pleasant to the view
When singing larks spring from the grass
To fade into the blue ;
And all the hawthorn hedges break
In wreaths of purest snow,
And yellow daffodils are out,
And roses half in blow.
The sea-coast of Bohemia
Is sad as sad can be,
Tlie prince has ta’en our flower of inai Is
Across the violet sea ;
Our Perdita has gone with him,
No more we dance the round
Upon the green in joyous play,
Or wake the tabor’s sound.
The sea-coast of Bohemia
Has many wonders seen—
The shepherd lass wed with a king,
The shepherd with a queen ;
But such a wonder as my love
Was never seen before—
It is my joy and sorrow now
To love her evermore.
The sea-coast of Bohemia
Is haunted by a light
Of memory of lady’s eyes
And fame of gallant knight;
The princes seek its charmbd strand,
But, ah ! it was our knell
When o’er the sea our Perdita
Went with young Florizel.
The sea-coast of Bohemia
Is not my resting place,
For with her waned from out the day
A beauty and a grace :
O, had I kissed her on the lips
I would no longer weep,
But live by that until the day
I fall to shade and sleep.
Tasmania.
JAMES HER[?]ETHWAITE.
THE SWING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We sat on a seat in a garden old,
And your lips were close but your eyes were clear ;
My heart was hot hut’my feet were cold,
And love is a flower the frost turns sere ;
So we called a truce to our whispering,
And we walked across to the children’s swing.
I called it a scaffold, where cruel girls
Were swung till love in their hearts was bred,
And you skimmed the air till the amber cnrls
Were a flying halo around your head :
Then I taught you a new and a cunning thing—
How two could float on the selfsame swing.
And sweet I found was the swift kiss pressed
With the taut rope poised in its steep incline,
And sweeter the lingering fall to rest
While your lips grew tender,.and clung to mine ;
But sweetest the passionate murmuring
When the moon looked down on an empty swing.
Now you are a matron, and I grow old ;
We have both gained weight, and the swing-rope’s
rotten ;
That evening’s lesson is now enrolled
With the numberless things we have long forgotten :
To recall it now were a heinous thing,
But I always laugh when I pass a swing.
P.

We hear a great deal about “voices ” nowadays.
More than any other part of the human organism,
le voice is a “topic” for tea-parties, dinner-
parties, even after-dinner parties. Wherever you

Fort-street children went to see “ Othello ” last
Today. There were no fig-leaves strewn along
the play for them—and Rignold’s edition is singu-
larly bare—but a bevy of teachers kept guard, and
guided the applause into proper young places.
Ihe children joined in; not one heavy passage
went unsaluted. All was going splendidly, and a
fine discrimination for Shakespeare seemed to be
beaming out of those hundreds of young eyes
when suddenly Othello threw himself on the floor
and rolled about, moaning and roaring with
anguish. This is an inspiration of Rignold’s
ownest own. You have to be very grave and
reverend here, or a sense of the ridiculous will
play havoc with ymir peace of mind. The teachers
were taken off their guard. There was a moment’s
silence. Then Fort-street lifted up its voice and
roared with laughter! The teachers came down
with an awful “ shish, ” but it was too late. Fort-
street seemed to believe that the big, fat, black
man was rolling on the floor for their sakes, and
bursts of laughter and giggles turned every one of
those elephantine rolls into low comedy.
Saturday night at the Broughs is quite a fore-
gone conclusion now. The d.c. wonders if it can
be the same d.c. that used to hold people with
hats and peppermints. Snobility comes out in all
its fiercest raiment, and feels so nice and intellec-
tual as it turns the light of its own diamonds on
the wit of the play. “Lord and Lady Algy”
brought about the gayest house of the season.
; Mitchell glittered from a box in white
and steel with red roses in her hair, which has re-
turned to the top of her head. Mrs. Ernest Wil-
shire, the latest bride, underwent a heavy fire of
opera-glasses. Coming out, it was painful to find
oneself rubbing against velveteen wraps on all
smes. 1 saw one man shiver as if he were struck
molar-twinge as two red-velveted sisters
sought his arm.

note in a strange fawn-silk skirt, with red stripes

topped with a happy red-velvet blouse, and a band
of black velvet round the throat. Finally, a racy,
mghly-becommg toque of red velvet, lifts up the

Rumor avers that Gilbert himself says Flora
Perry is his ideal Phyllis, as far as looks go.
ohe looks a most dainty, chic little person, with a
strong resemblance to an animated egg-boiler in

Wedding presents are becoming “curiouser and
cunouser down South. It is “ quite the thing ”
now to present the bride with a canary. In the
three most fashionable Melb. weddings chronicled
recently, the announcements varied as follows:
Canary, canary in cage,” “canary in brass
cage. Other gay and festive offerings are bath-
shoes, head-shawls, “work,’’and other things far
too weird to mention in a sober paper.
Many wives of N.S.W. legislators abhor politics
and are particularly perturbed when a new session
is nigh. They cannot be induced, under any cir-
cumstances, to visit the Ladies’ Gallery. The
better-half of an idol of the electors declares she
will yet wean him of politics ; while another
nightly prays that Parliament House may be re-

From Miss I. E. Wyndham I have just received
particulars of a new departure in women’s work—
new to Sydney,_ that is. It has been followed
advantageously in Melbourne for some time. The
scheme calls itself “The Artistic Work Dep6t,”
and will help ladies to dispose of their fancy work
more profitably. Also, you can leave orders all in
one breath for mending, renovating hats and bon-
nets, making evening and day costumes, decorat-
ing dinner and supper tables, and catering for
afternoon teas and suppers. The depot will be
opened May Ist at 167 Vickery’s Chambers.
Tii o crusade to crush flowers is waxing bolder.
1 hey have already driven flowers out of funerals.
-But those who can’t die can get married. So now
High Church clergymen are swooping down on
nnf blossoms that always cling about a wedding.
Ihe other day a very High Churchman refused to
let a band of girl friends decorate his church for a
certain wedding. The girls came in armed with
their flowers and greens, but were remorselessly
f™n away by the parson, who told them
that they mustn’t dream of bringing natural blos-
soms into that high, aesthetic worship-shop. In
vain they pleaded. Rose-scents had to give way
to incense. Finally, the decorators went away in
great wrath and worked off their injured feelings all
over the staircase and gas-lamps of the bride’s
house.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
Already the ‘discoverers’ of Amy Castles
have moderated their transports concerning her
voice, and begun to express doubts about her
qualifications, physical and mental, for a great
Career. The damsel has thrown off the authority
otherfirst patronesses—Mrs. Doubleday and Mrs.
. B°b Best—who worked like niggers to bring her
into notice when there was little prospect of a
bcxim, and she is now being managed by Messrs.

tT o * h £ Z e * k wbea he robbed Melbourne of
Ernest Moffitt and Carl De Beer—the latter a
brilliant boy of 22. Belonging to a talented,
cultured family, Carl De Beer took to learning as
his natural provender. Music, mathematics,
rhetoric, debate, nothing seemed to come to him
amiss. As m the case of Ernest Moffitt the
weather was indirectly the cause of his death
Commencement Day at Melb. University, with
bitter winds and drenching rains coming suddenly
after the heat, chilled the spirits of the under-
grads., and so it happened that Carl De Beer, out
ot pure enthusiasm, started a fresh song, and
shouted so wildly that he twisted an intestine like
a corkscrew. An operation was instantly per-
formed but the part was dead—the too-demonstra-
tive had in fact, begun to die as soon as the last
shout left his lips.

“The ‘charitableness ’ of the late James Hosie,
Melbourne s original pie-shop-keeper, is averred
to have been the result of a quarrel with
his nearest blood-relation. Having determined
not to leave any money to his married
daughter, Hosie built a new wing to Mel-
bourne Homoeopathic Hospital 10 years before his
death, and would, no doubt, have given further
vent to his parental feelings if unwise land-boom
speculations had not relieved him of his riches.

By a Specialist.
Iti is dawning on the public to try and prevent,
or at least to arrest, disease. It is in prevention
that Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa plays an important
part, acting solely as a first-class nourishing food
—it strengthens the system to resist, oppose, and
overcome the attacks of disease. You’ll hear
someone say, “So-and-so has a strong constitu-
tion ”; follow that up, and you’ll find that So-
and-so follows the golden rule of being temperate
in all things, and pays attention to diet and exer-
cise. Does he or she keep up this strong constitu-
tion by taking medicine or swallowing pills? No
indeed ! They have discovered that prevention is
better than cure. Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa places a
means in the hands of everyone to build up and
maintain a sound constitution, which enables its
possessor to travel his life’s journey without the
aches and pains which are in many cases pre-
ventible. Thus we come round again to sound
common-sense based on experience.
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa can be obtained from all
chemists, grocers and stores, or from Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa, Limited, 269 George-street, Sydney.

Jack Want is said to have made a fortune
out of a mining venture :
A better man is Want with pelf,
For now he’s raised above himself 1
Artist Nerli lately arrived in
Sydney from Westralia, en route
for Maoriland. A few years ago he
was living in Sydney, and making a
reputation as one of Australia’s first
painters. His study of the head of
an old Italian peasant woman is a
perfect gem, and his portraits of
Dr. Marano and Miss Myra Kemble
are things of joy. By the way, Nerli
is now married and clean shaven.
He is also a Marquis of the king-
dom of Italy. His mother was a
Miss Medwin, daughter of that
English Captain Medwin who was
the friend and memorialist of the
poet Byron.
That marvellous draughts-player, James
Wyllie, “ The Herd Laddie,” is now in Heaven—
no doubt playing checkers with the angels and
prefacing each game with “ Put doun yer shullin!”
Out of 8000 games played in Australia, Wyllie
lost but 25 and drew 100.
English dockers’ leader, Tom Mann, is the
most conspicuous of many agitators who have
purchased pubs, and attached themselves to the
fringe of the plutocracy. Several men who used
to help the N.S.W. worker to “higher things”
now serve out drinks to the thirsty toiler. Andy
Kelly and Tom Davis, ex-M’sL. A., both became
hotel-proprietors, and Eight-hour secretary Furse
also runs a pub. in a Sydney suburb. Also George
Black is writing for the Conservative S.M.
Herald.
James Service’s father. Robert Service,
used to be familiar in Melb. He was a little man,
his face a quizzical burlesque on that of James,
with the nose a lot more on one side, as if it had
been flattened down with a thumb. His vocation
was that of a humble Baptist preacher, with no
anxiety about the Plate, because there was the
mahogany at James’s magnificent mansion, “Kil-
winning,” at Balaclava, to stick his legs under.
James was an Agnostic ; no one ever heard of his
going to kurruk.
Felix Faure’s demise was, after all, not a
case of Felix 0/>portimitate Mortis. When it was
telephoned to London, the French Embassy ar-
ranged for High Mass at Westminster, to be
attended by the Ambassador and English notabili-
ties. In consequence, evidently, of another mes-
sage from Paris, the Embassy sent a particular
direction that the function must be Low Mass—
which it was, at first greatly to the surprise of the
press. Tongues were kept buzzing when the news
came that the Parisian religious ceremony, with
all its glory, was only Low Mass. The case will
be a reminder of that of the English Judge who
died at an equivocal house in the provinces after
he had been suitably lecturing the sinners at the
assizes. Not long ago a Pompeiian exhumation
fetched up a fine woman and an elderly gentleman,
smothered in the ashes at one of the houses with
the frescoes. Paris L ’lllustration gave an en-
graving of the pair. Providence repeats itself.

such remarkable horsemanship feats as are in this

Trades Hall. Barker and others continue to say
“no-ledge ” instead of knowledge, “ boorzh-wah ”
instead of what they used to call “boor-joys.”

eagle.

N.S.W. Speakership is in an almost direct
line to an Imperial title. Those who took the
Chair as plain “misters,” and developed into
knights, baronets, and so on, were Nicholson
(Dr.), Cooper, Murray, Hay, Allen, and Abbott.
Alex. M‘Leay remained Mister, but Arnold would
have stepped up next session had he not been
drowned. Barton was offered a title, ana Young
would have been now a knight had not the
potato-market slumped at a delicate juncture.
Dear Bulletin.—Recently died in Sydney
a citizen who was for many years a liberal contri-
butor to several churches. Where did he die ? In
the Benevolent Asylum, at 79. A warning to
those who give liberally to churches. When your
turn comes to be destitute you may die in the
gutter. Those who have taken freely from you
for “Assistant Clergy Fund,” “Mission to the
Chinese,” &c., will forget that you 6ver lived.
Mission to the Chinese ! —while a good white man
dies in an asylum ! —Rough On Rats.
A jolly old soul. Archdeacon Stretch, the
other day, died at Malvern, Melb. Ballarat and
Geelong loved him of yore, this. Sydney Smith of
a man, not burdened with theology, but a rare
judge of crops, a born bucolic, and better than a
Roman priest at wheedling cash, whereby he got
the solid A. 30,000 needed to persuade Thornton to
understand that the Lord called him to Ballarat
from the Birmingham slums. Stretch, with more
brains, was such another as jovial Toorakian
pastor Walter Fellows, brother of the Judge.
For those who knew him not, he micht be
described as a compound of Harcourt, the English
politician, and comedian Harwood.
Father Kavanagh lectured a
few weeks ago, in Cork, on “ People
I H ave Met,” touching on two emin-
ent Sydneyites, Archbishop Vaughan
and Dailey. The Archbishop, he said,
simply wrote out a sermon and then
knew every word. Chas. Gavan Duffy
had the same gift, in his political
speeches. Dailey, on arriving in Dub-
lin, of course fell in with the intelli-
gent carman. He found at once that
Dailey was a very superior man, and
formed the theory that he was a
Fenian General. Dailey, of course,
humored this, with mysterious talk.
At parting, the cabman said, “Now,
tell me who you are.” Dailey whis-
pered in his ear, “I’m a Polynesian.”
Cabby, overwhelmed by this disclo-
sure of another Branch, said, “ Ah, I knew you
was. I’m one of the Bhis meself. But be
careful of thim polis.” Father Kavanagh credits
the yarn about a girl being christened Pat on an
immigrant-ship. He says the priest did the job
again, saying, “I can’t lift the whole name off
her. She must be called Patricia.”
When Varney Parkes visited the Albion
Park (N.S.W.) show, Hassall, of the Gwydir, was
“on the spout.” A very old woman nudged this
scribe and asked, “Is yon Varney?” Writer
nodded. Then she took a long, steady look at the
big Gwydir man and exclaimed, “ Mon ! but he’s
like his father.” Somepeople will identify anybody.
Ex-Gov. M‘Gregor lately wrote to Premier
Dickson that he had named a lofty mountain
(11,584 ft.) in Papua after the Q. Premier.. “Mt.
Dickson,” M‘Gregor writes, “is very bare of trees
and shrubs on the top. It has no native name. I
have given it the name of an old and highly-
valued friend, a Prime Minister of Queensland, of
which this note will bear witness to your descend-
ants when you and I are silent.” The delicacy of
the allusion to Dickson’s baldness ! The moun-
tain is very “ bare on top.” And some folks say
the Scotch have no humor.
Vasey, recently-deceased, sec. of Melb.
Gas Co., was a perfect realisation of Fatman.
Short, stout, bald, red-faced, and side-whiskered,
Vasey was the ideal Conservative, and acted up
to his appearance. A heavy shareholder ip the
concern he managed so successfully, Vasey rigor-
ously cut down expenses and worked the office
chiefly with boys who might, if they were very
good boys indeed, in time draw A 1 a week. He
was a leading light of the now moribund Y. M.C. A.
in the days when Matt. Davies loomed also large
in connection therewith, and a heap of Vasey’s
cash is alleged to have gone into the white-
elephant Bourke-street pile since sold to and now
run by the Salvarmy.
“MEDOR,” Melb. Eastern Market Murderer.
(A sketch from life.)
Medor, the cranky astrologer who mur-
dered a fellow-tenant in Melb. Eastern Market,
the other day, is well-known by sight to nearly
everybody in Melb., being a big, broad six-footer,
always frockcoated and belltoppered, and his
long brown hair waving over his collar. He is
supposed, on good authority, to be the son of one
of Australia’s early surveyor-generals. He is close
on 60 years old, and his “rattiness ” is supported
by documentary evidence of long standing, show-
ing him to be a firm believer in his own craft.
Archbishop Redwood, of Maoriland,
spoken of as a coming Cardinal, was only three
years of age when he landed in M.L., and so may
be regarded as a local product. His. brother
Henry, the “ Father ” of the Maoriland turf, says
that “ Frank ” even now is as good a judge of
horseflesh as he is. But the Archbishop’s only
known hobby is the fiddle; he possesses a genuine
“ Strad ” valued at over four figures.
The acceptance by the Duke of Connaught
of succession to the sovereignty of Saxe-Coburg-
Gotha reminds old Sydneyites of the visit made
to Sydney, 29 years ago, by Prince Philip and
Prince Augustus, of that Dukedom, and of the
whirl of festivities which accompanied it. Those
princes were Queen Victoria’s cousins, so that, by
Edinburgh and Connaught, she gets a step nearer
Saxe-Coburg, instead of veering farther off.

The Bulletin happened early the other :
morning upon a spectacle which has consider- '
ably damaged its opinion that the typica If j
unhumorou 4 person is the parson. Outsioe j
the Diocesan Registry in Phillip-street, Syd- j
ney, was a fantastically-arrayed little crowd, i
which upon enquiry turned out to be the law-
students’ contingent of the University Commemo-
ration procession. The desperate seriousness, nob
to say solemnity, with which tlie-e embryo sharks
appeared to take the clumsy grotesquerie
of their “make-ups” was suggestive of nothing
so much as a prospective departure to
speedy and collective execution per the lorry
which stood in waiting for them ; but as this, un-
fortunately, was not in contemplation, it is, per-
haps, to be supposed that these wooden-visaged
youths, who seemed to be all more or less ashamed
of their idiocy, regarded themselves as furnishing
an artistic exposition rather than a grievous spec-
tacle of puerile mountebankery. The only dis-
coverable glimmer of humor about the lament-
able exhibition lay in the wearing by one
young man of an ass’s head, unintentionally
emblematic of the litigating public. All else
was of such pathetic w ..odenness that The Bul-
letin, by way of extracting some fun from the
function, is prepared next year to contribute a
“ fiver” or so towards the cost of engaging a con-
tingent of, say, the Balmain “Liver Push” to
“deal it out” to these dreary processionists on
their way to the University, and so stir things up
a bit. The dry bones would have to live then with
a vengeance!
However appropriate a jovial kicking-over-the-
traces of ordinary life might be to young fellows
of Bohemian kidney—free, joyous souls who were
justified of themselves with song or story—lyre, or
brush, or chisel—such attempts at high-jinks sib
piteously ill upon the utterly prosy, unoriginal
young prigs whose distinction in life is plain, hard
“ oof’’-grinding—grasping with both hands after
the sordid, unromantic dollar! “The elephant
hath joints—but none for friskery ! ”

Aboriginalities.
“ Silas S.”: le the hallucinations of the
perishing. Droon had been missing for several
days when we discovered him. Search parties
had been out from Morwell, and he was given up
as a cadaver when we went along the side of a
Gippsland gorge, hunting for an alleged coal out-
crop. At a certain point Coleman, the tired man
Of our small party, drew us back on our tracks
with wild yells and extravagant pantomime.
Coleman was pointing at the hollow trunk of a
great white gum, and sitting in that retreat,
staring at us out of the shadow with what looked
like enormous feline eyes, was a gaunt, hollow-
cheeked pallid wretch. “Good God! what’re
you doin’ there, man?” said Sandy. The man’s
lips moved, and a dry, parrot-like voice said :
“ Can’t get out ! ” “ Rot! ” said Coleman, grow-
ing bolder, “come out,” and he thrust in his hand
and seized Droon. “ How can I get out through
that little crack ?” “ Why, the way you got in.”
“But I was a little feller when I came in,”
croaked Droon, “an* I’ve swelled since—swelled
enormous. Look at me.” Droon was about
thin enough to have been plaited through a
picket-fence, and the opening in the butt was big
enough for a bullock to have bunked in. We
dragged him forth to his immense amazement and
he was packed off to hospital. According to
Droon’s own belief, he had been sitting in that
hollow for three days, growing bigger every
minute, and watching the tree close in around
him.

“Track”: Travelling cm N.S.W. South
coast, struck a settler’s house about dinner-time,
and was invited to “ have a bit.” While the meal
was progressing, I heard a grunt behind, and a
2cwt. pig reared himself upon his hind-legs and
almost put his forefeet into my plate. The per-
formance was repeated three times, but the old
woman told me not to mind him, as he was a pet,
and it was “only his thricks he was up to.”

“ Ben S.” asks a question ;
How is it that the comfortable eockies in populous dis-
tricts are mostly British, Irish, or European of some
sort, while the hand-to-mouth stragglers in the back-
blocks are mostly Australian ? Does it show that the
former got there first and are long-livers (which isn’t
conclusive, because the Australian has had time to come
along), or that they are better hustlers on small holdings ?
Or is it because the latter are less provident, or because
they love the lonesome? Or is it that the Australee
woman, more than the imported Jane and Bridget, sighs
for the sewing-machine, and the baker’s loaf, and the
dressmaker, and the slavey, and other correlatives of
competence, and by dabbling in them too soon helps the
rising tide of competition to drift her and hers to the
outer rim ? Is it want of elbow-room, or love of adven-
ture, or independence of spirit, that will plough the sun-
cracked ‘'Never-Never,” and live on kangaroo and
possum rather than rent a farm from any “ blanky land-
king?"
THE SAMOAN SITUATION AND SOLUTION,
J. B.: “I think, Sam, we could manage him better if we out him up amongst us. You know, the
good book says; 'Blessed is the piece-maker, '”
The casualness of Australian children was
well exemplified by the two boys (7 and 5) recently
lost for 42 hours on Borumbil. Started out on a
Wednesday rabbiting with a dog, a half-axe, and
a bag for the game. As they did not turn up at
night, half the country side went out to look for
them next day, but it wasn’t till Friday that they
were picked up along the river—quite cool and
unconcerned. They had gone on with their hunt-
ing, and had three rabbits in the bag. When
asked what had become of the dog—“ Oh, a snake
bit him and he croaked ! ” Same day they had
opened the public gate along the road for the
mail, but had made no moan. Had previously
struck a carrier asleep in his waggon, but didn’t
wake him to ask their way.

“ Old Burketown ” on aboriginal married
life: —
An Australian aborigine considers that his wives are
his private property, to cherish, punish, or even kill at
his pleasure, and, as a mark of esteem, he will send his
favorite wife for a time to reside in the gunyah of one
who has greatly distinguished himself. When visitors
from other tribes arrive, temporary wives are sent to
their camping place, and so strongly fixed is this
“ society ” custom that some of the early white travellers
have had great difficulty in declining the compliment
without giving offence. Black couples, despite the
rigorous mode of correction resorted to hv the husband,
often live long and happily together. The women are
forgiving, and believe that if their husbands do not well
and frequently heat them it is a sign of indifference.
Married women in some tribes (like the great Dieyeri)
have sweethearts or paramours (Pirra-ooroo) allowed to
them as a tribal custom. But in all Australian tribes a
woman who, outside of her tribe's regulations, violates
marital fidelity is severely dealt with. Usually either
speared through the thigh, or led to the front of her own
gunyah and speared through the foot into the ground.
In cases where husband is unreasonably and too fre-
quently severe upon wife the woman sometimes seeks the
protection of another man, who, if so inclined,
will fight the husband to decide whether or not
the new arrangement is to be permanent. Girls
are married and allotted mostly before 12 years of
age, being then virtually women. When a young titan,
having completed his initiation into manhood, happens
to possess sisters who (through death of father and
father’s brother) are under his control, he can exchange
these with some other guardian for other young women.
A man may in this way become possessed of many wives.
Also, he may inherit them by the death of his brothers,
whose wives become his. But perhaps the strangest (and
wisest) of all strange customs is the universal Australian
one that no married blackfellow must hold any com-
munication whatever with his mother-in-law.
* * * *
“D. 8.” on a way back malady :
Inland and away from the mountains prevails the
malady familiar to bushmen as “ Barcoo vomit.” Sytnp-
toms—sudden abnormal flow of saliva, followed by violent
retching immediately after a meal. The keener the
appetite, the worse the retching. Also, the young and
robust are most subject to it. Most prevalent in good
seasons, when grass is green and flies abundant. Have
never known case of it in winter. Country doctors
ascribe it to indigestion ; to too much meat; to too little;
to lack of vegetables; to had water, flies, over-heating,
etc. Shearers also have varying explanations, the most
favored of which lays the blame on that “blanky
poisoner”—the cook. Vast quantities of patent medi-
cines are consumed to combat, the affection, but, as far
as I know, without benefit. Any Bulletinite know of a
remedy?

“ Yar ” ; Dingley was a terrible old cur-
mudgeon. Nothing but salt-junk, spuds, and
treacle week-in, week-out ; but we got home on
him at last. Yarded three wild colts one morn-
ing, and, when Dingley got into the yard to try
his hand with the rope, the colts charged the slip-
rails together. Big smash ! The three rails splin-
tered and sent flying among a flock of turkeys 20
yards off. Casualties six, and we ate poultry for
days.

“ Baktrax ” : At least one variety of Aus-
tralian frog is a cannibal. He is an olive-green
pirate with an alligatorish head, possesses teeth,
and lives in the Gulf country. When the mis-
sionary is a large one, the swallowing trick is an
interesting spectacle, as, in consequence of the
power of distending their bodies, common to all
frogs, the dinner often appears bigger than the
diner.

“W.M.F.” : Two of us rode out to get
ration-sheep. Sighting the mob, we sent dog to
bring it. As he was good dog, took no further
notice. The sheep were a long while putting in
appearance. Looked over and saw dog splitting
mob into twos and threes, and chasing them about
for amusement. Called him up and slapped his
ears. Sent him away again. He then got behind
sheep, and raced them clean out of sight. Called
him back and talked to him with the double of a
saddle-strap. Sent him after the sheep third time,
and he brought them back in real workmanlike
style. Instinct or reason—which ?

“ Scotty ” : Ever since the stations stopped
giving or selling rations there have been bad
seasons. “He that giveth to the poor lendeth to
the Lord.” The traveller now, after being re-
fused tucker by a “bank’s” manager, says:
“You bailiffs mean to make the stations pay.”

The pay of these “bums,” where it used to be
.£3OO to £5OO a year, is now £7O to £l5O. They
have to come into town, now, to loaf on the
doctors and lawyers, when formerly the reverse
was the case. And it’s now tennis, debating-
societies and cheap sports, instead of “ Fill ’em
up again, boys.” Station traps are like cookies’,
now dirty buggies, dirty harness, and dirty
horses. Also, dirty riders.

“Ken.” : I know a sheep-dog that has
taken out a flock of sheep for years. He is the
shepherd, and sits all day on the stone wall, or
on a log or high ground. If the boss wants the
sheep home he rings the cow-bell. The sheep
know the dog, and are nob afraid of him, rather
like him and obey him.
* * * *
In Westralia the law doesn’t actually com-
pel the nigger to apprentice himself for many
years at about nothing a year to the squatter who
flogs him for the good of his soul. But it runs him
in for vagrancy—for sleeping out in the bush, and
having no visible means of support except kanga-
roo and the like, for not having a stone house and
a bell-topper and a visiting-card—and gives him
six months. And it is explained to him that he
will go on getting six months fbr vagrancy unless
he gets a permanent address on the station of a
white boss and visible means of support there in
the shape of rations. But there is nothing com-
pulsory about the business.

“ A.K.” : An old horse I had with a team
of bullocks knew the bullock-bell as well as I did,
and would move towards sound long before I
could hear it. If I stopped to listen for the bell
he, too, would stand rigid as a statue, listening.
Have known same horse lie flat on his side and
wriggle under the waggon to get at a bag of chaff.
• * * *
The Queensland pastoralist who finds the
tick zone is harmful to his sales takes a few of the
treasures south as many miles as seem necessary.
Soon after his arrival in the locality ticks are dis-
covered, reported to the inspector, and the ob-
noxious tick-line goes south a few degrees. It is
an open secret, on the Downs at any rate, that
ticks have travelled over the N.S. W. boundary—in
a bottle.

“ Do you know Murphy’s place, on the three-chain
road?” Cockie : “ Shu re, i very body knows it.”
“Where is it, then?” Cockie: “ Shure, where
would it be but on the three-chain road ? ” I rode
on.

Be life tenacity. The fresh-
water turtle takes the bun. Bothered with one

boots. Took pains to put him out of pain. Half-
an-hour afterwards he startled me by slithering
down the sloping bank into the water.

“ Coaster ” :
Although the
kangaroo is now
becoming very
scarce i n most
N. S. W. popu-
