“Dolly E.” : Near our Blue Mountain
home, when I was a young girl, was a big cave in
the rocks, on the roof of which were strange
aboriginal markings in red, white, and black. I
noticed that the blacks who used occasionally to
come our way never failed to visit this cave, in
which, 10ft. from the ground, a narrow shelf of
rock projected about 3ft. for nearly the full length
of the cave. I had often tried to reach this shelf,-
but failed ; but, grown bigger, I one day after
many attempts succeeded and found on the ledge
a small bundle tied up in bark and covered with
dust. I took it up and shook it, when out dropped
a little yellow skull and some yellow, mouldy
bones. I promptly dropped the bundle and was
down from that shelf in a twinkling, and never
went kick to what was in reality an aboriginal
sepulchre.

A likeness of John Reay Watson, author
of “In a Man’s Mind,” is printed herewith. Mr.
Watson is known to Bulletin readers as author
of several excellent short stories, and his first
essay in literary criticism appears on i he Red Page
of this issue. Mr. Watson whose new.book—“An
Earthly Fulfilment ’—has just been published in
London, is a young Australian, born at Tinonee,
Manning River, N.S.W., 23rdMMfr,' r , 1872. Aus-
tralia celebrates the event of the 24th of the month
—a mistake that will some day have to receive
attention. Both Mr. Watson’s parents were
Scotch ; but his mother, from whom he inherits
his literary instincts, came here at a very early
age, and her memories were wholly Australian.
The young Watson was soon taken to Queensland,
and has ever since been a resident of that province.
The genesis of his ambition to write a book was
early dated, but his present ambition is to
write a good one. Meredith, Henry James, and a
few of the younger men, define the limits of his
reading. “ Writing,” says Mr. Watson, “is
hard work —with fascinating compensations.”
Some time back, an insane boomer (once
a well-known auctioneer), now in Yarra Bend
asylum, prepared elaborate plans of that place,
mounted the stump, and sold the lots, taking long
bills. The price beat boom-time. A big row is
now brewing—the ratty buyers want possession.

“ Thibet Landor ” is beginning to smell a
bit Rougemontish. In a recent Alpine Journal,
a well-known climbist ridicules Landor’s state-
ment that in 16£ hours, without food and carrying
a 601 b. load, he ascended to a height of 22,000 feet.
The writer, by comparing records, shows that
Landor, according to himself, must have climbed
three times as fast as the hitherto fastest climber
—and that with 10 times the load 1 But still,
Mungo Park had a rough time for a season !

native bear can go without water ? My dog
chased one up a gum on banks of Mary River, Q.,
and steadily besieged that tree for five weeks,
watering at river close by, and having his food
brought him by me. Meanwhile bear skinned
every leaf off the tree, and though I wanted to see
how long the creature could go without water, in
the 6th week pity made me take dog away and let
bear decamp.

•‘Waikato”: W.A. Government's pro-
posed wholesale purchase of cats to check increase
of'rabbits recalls a M.L. incident in the early
days of the local rabbit plague. A squatter,
having advertised that he was a buyer of cats in
quantity, a daily procession of juveniles used to
arrive witli bags, the feline occupants whereof
were duly freed in the surrounding hills. Finding
that the cat-supply did not diminish, however,
the squatter investigated, and found that, after
enjoying temporary change of air in the uplands,
the cats duly went home and were re-sold to him
again and again. In fact, a regular industry in
“ homing cats ” had arisen.

fact for experts to worry.
Throughout Victoria the
presence of she-oak is an
almost unfailing indica-
tion of auriferous wealth.
The rich gullies of Ben-
digo, Ballarat, and Cas-
tlemaine in the early
days were all covered
with she-oak. Ironbark,
too, has a partiality for
golden soil, but she-oak is
much more to be depend-
ed upon, and was very
affectionately regarded
by the old hands.

o nce, where the cries of

At the recent “ electrocution ” of a mur-
deress at Sing Sing prison, U.S., “ the two women
physicians, who were present, stood close by, and
their gowns hid the buckling of the electrode on
Mrs. Place’s leg near the knee. This done, one
of the women doctors pulled down the skirts so
that the leg and the electrode were covered.” Then
the 1700-volt-current wiped the woman out of
existence in an instant.
What a Colossus among giants
London Times still is ! On Jan. 3, under
the usual modest ‘ ‘ From Our Correspon-
dent,” The Thunderer printed a full des-
cription of the farewell banquet to Lord
Elgin, given at Calcutta on the preced-
ing night, together with a verbatim report
of the Viceroy’s speech. The telegram
fills over four columns, each 22 x 3 in.
—and could not possibly have cost the
paper a penny under £3OO. In all, there
are 6339 words. Money talks !
Eye-glass Samson (onetime “ Uit-
lander ”of the Argus), who has lately
been travelling the N.W. wilds of Wes-
tralia on the look-out for diamonds, states
that the land-owners in that tropical
region never see any newspaper except
The Bulletin and Australasian. Sam-
son’s trouble, as he passed from place to
place, was the difficulty in arriving at a
new Bulletin. Everybody had the same
old batch of back-numbers on the table.
“6.Y.H.”:Ee those recent fatal
N.S.W. boiler-explosions, should say that
Q. also needed immediate legislation on
the subject. Know a N.Q. alderman and
J.P. whose saw-mill hands are kept in
constant fear of their lives through the
vagaries of an ancient boiler. When
701 b. pressure is shown, all hands make
a bee-line for the scrub—out of harm’s
way. Know also of the boiler of a steam
merry-go-round which has no safety-
valve ; and this with scores of children
enjoying a circular ride, while a crowd
of adults stand around. When the anti-
cipated “ bust-up ” happens, the popula-
tion will be reduced probably in two
figures.

“At Brokenhillionaire Kelly’s birthday-
party, the other night (says the Argus), Mr. Neale
Wigg, chairman of Broken Hill Proprietary Co.,
entertained the guests by recounting reminiscences
of the early difficulties of the promoters. Aparty
of them, he said, were gathered one hot summer’s
night in a room in Menzies’ Hotel, abutting on
Bourke-street, discussing their prospects, and the
conversation turned on the millions which they
saw before them. When a momentary lull came
in the discussion, the silence was broken by a
deep, husky voice at the open window with the
words, ‘ I say, boss, for the love of Heaven, chuck
us one o’ them millions. I can do with a bit.’ ”
W.A. Chief Justice Onslow recently
passed through Sydney on his way to M.L.
He it was who, at the trial of Anderson for
flogging several aboriginal girls to death, said :
“ The jury have found you guilty of man-
slaughter. I cannot agree with their verdict. It
was a cruel, wanton murder. You with your
brother, after having captured these unfortunate
blacks, actually went into your homestead, had
dinner, and then came out to flog these creatures
to death. When you became tired of flogging,
your brother took up the murderous task, and
when you had had a spell you began again. . . .
I never, in the oourse of my experience, have
heard of such a case, and I pray God that I never
will again.” He then gave accused life imprison-
ment as “a warning to his class in the nor’-west
who treat human beings worse than cattle ”

boat up the Clarence River, died the other day at

THE TOTALISATOR.
DR. BEVAN’S VIEV.3.
improvement on bookmaking.
Adelaide, Monday.
The Rev. Dr. Bevan, of Melbourne,
"was shown over the totalisator building,
on the Adelaide racecourse, this morning,
And the working of the machine was ex-
plained to him by the secretary. Dr.
»evan intends publishing a book on
gambling. Dr. Bevan said: “ The
totalisator is an improvement on the sys-
tem of bookmaking. Supposing that
betting is essential, and could not be got
aid of, it is the host thing to do to ueo
the totalisator, because it is absolutely’
fair, as far as it goes.”

“ Mac” : As to the Chinaman’s delight in
torture. I remember, when a boy, a Chow gar-
dener coming to our place every morning for some
boiling water. Wondered what he wanted it for,
until I went over one morning and found John
smiling blandly as he let the boiling liquid trickle
on to an unfortunate rat in a cage-trap.

The complexities of Yic. postal service are
endless. Attention is now drawn to the fact that
a boy entering the telegraph service as messenger
receives .£24 a year, and that if he lives to be a
hairy old patriarch, he’ll never get any more.
Formerly, boys could join the service in the sure
and certain hope that by the time they reached
senility they would be earning £6O a year. But
that Elysian state of things is past. There are,
however, 398 boys who, having entered the service
under the former happy conditions, enjoy the
maximum of £OO, and the growl is that they don’t
do any more work than those who have to
struggle along at £24. The Anomalies Board
has got hold of the conundrum by the tail. But
as yet it hasn’t managed to work out whether
it is a v\ hite elephant or a Paradoxus Duffyii.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

Poor old London Figaro is dead—really
dead at last. It was a light and bright weekly a
quarter of a century ago, as compared with its
dull and heavy rivals, and under the editorship of
James Mortimer it flourished for ten years or
more. Then he lost his grip of the reins, and the
original staff retired with him, and Figaro came
out in a succession of “ new forms,” more or less
readable or foolish. Some months ago Mor-
timer got hold of the paper again (what was left
of it), and collected the remnants of the original
staff, with the hope of being able to make Figaro
boom once more. A pathetic, ghostly enterprise.
The new generation knew not Mortimer, “ Cheru-
bino,” “ 0. P. Q. Philander Smiff,” and the other
revived identities whose pens were light in the
early 70s. Popular taste in light, bright, jour-
nalism had changed, and the old readers were either
dead or indifferent to poor Mortimer’s appeal for
renewed patronage. Figaro’s attempt to live on
the original lines failed miserably, it seems, and a
few grey-haired mourners have lost a hopeless job.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
27 of this issue.*

Business Announcements.
TO the MEMBERS of the AUSTRALIAN MUTUAL
PROVIDENT SOCIETY. Ladies and Gentlemen.—
In accordance with the By-laws, I shall be one of the
three retiring directors from the Board of the Society
at the next Annual Meeting in May. Being eligible for
re-election, I offer myself as a candidate for the honor
of your appointment to a seat at the Board. If I meet
with your favor, my best services shall continue to be
given to the interests of the Society ; and I remain,
Ladies and Gentlemen, your most obedient servant,
19th April, 1899. TUPS. LITTLEJOHN.
TO THE MEMBERS of the A.M.P. SOCIETY. Ladies
and Gentlemen,— ln Sept, last I was appointed to the
Seat at the Board rendered vacant through the death of
Deputy-Chairman Mr. J. R. Hill, and under the By-laws
l am now a retiring Director, and a candidate for elec-
tion. Should you honor me with a Seat at the Board,
whatever experience I have gained during a life-long con-
nection with Financial and Commercial affairs will be
given to promote and safeguard the interests of our
Society.—Yours faithfully, REGINALD J. BLACK
Bond-street, Sydney, April, 1899.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Q. Worker has dropped its much-dis-
cussed “ Socialism in our time ” motto.
Two recent appointments by the Boodle-
wraith were so hideously smellful that even the
sycophantic Courter mildly requests them not to
break it too suddenly to the long-suffering Mon-
golian.
Some anomalies : In N.S.W. women may
vote for aldermen in municipalities but not for an
M.L.A. in their division ; male lodgers may vote
for an M.L.A. but not for aldermen. Again,
uncertificated insolvents can sit as M’sL.A. but
not as aldermen.
One of the most humorous arguments used
by the belated anti-Federalists in Q. is that “ all
their customs and postal revenues will be swal-
lowed up by the hungry Federal Parliament.”
Considering that the Q. postal-service is con-
ducted at an annual loss of about .£75,000 !
The Queensland “Yes-No” Premier
Dickson. Should a majority in the Mongrelian
Assembly declare for Federation- as seems pro-
bable—Dickson will sacrifice his last hair for Aus-
tralian unity. If not, he will weep—and wait.
Everything by turns, and nothing longer than it
pays.
An amazing feature of the recent Queens-
land general elections was Brisbane Courier’s
dread of the Socialistic bogey it declared to be
looming large behind the Labor-Opposition party
and its utter inability to see the scaly horror of
the leprosy-fiend that stalled in front of its own
gang.
• ie P resen t S.A. general election, accord-
ing to S. A. Tories, was to witness the triumphant
return of Kingston’s old enemy, Symon, Q.C., to
S.A. Assembly—to be followed by other dire con-
sequences. Symon, however, jibbed at the last
moment, and went to England on a long holiday
OFF THE FENCE AT LAST.
during the week preceding Kingston’s policy-
speech.
Darling Downs farmers are against Federa-
tion because somebody has told them Queensland
will have to contribute J>500,000 per annum
towards supporting a Federal Parliament, com-
posed of t’othersiders, who will, in return, pick
Queensland’s pocket. The Downs farmer is
mostly a German, and, having got one idea into
his head, has no room left for anything else.
Henry Copeland’s N.S.W. Sunday Trading
Bill trailed so over open ground that enemies
were easily able to jump on it. Instead of strag-
gling over Good Friday, Xmas Day, co-operative
societies, compulsory regulation of clubs and other
questions, if he had put to the House a Bill with
one issue—open or not on Sunday afternoon and
evening his measure might have danced to
victory.
Sydney Sunday Times alludes rather cir-
cumspectly to the fact that the N.S.W. Anti-
Convention Bill League is ignoring the assistance
of members of Assembly in fighting against the
Federal Enabling Bill. As the League’s main
supports are the Tory M’sL.C. and others of their
kidney, and as the Parliamentary party is mainly
the Labor-party, the dislike is more than probably
mutual.
“ C.C.8.” : America is becoming supreme
in the iron-trade. Yankee locomotives ordered
for India, and even for English railways. America
gets the latest big Yic. contract for steel rails ;
also, the huge W.A. contract for steel pipes. And
she absolutely knocks out all N.S.W.’s show in
the agricultural-machinery business. A Yankee
firm ships reapers and binders right through from
the U.S. to way-back in N.S.W. without unpack-
ing, dumps them down in front of the cocky, and
sells them to him on a two-years’ bill. Wanted
Federation and a strong Protective tariff to make
Yankeelanders set up factories at or near Sydney.
“X”: The most serious aspect of the
delegation of Australian defence to Imperial
officers, is that in a time of extremity they wowJd
have the decision as to which parts of the
“Empire” should be abandoned to fate.
Alleged-Australasian-Admiral Pearson might
very easily decide, if he had a discretion,
that the defence of Australasia was hope-
less, and he would add his forces to the de-
fence, say, of India, or the Cape. Austra-
lasia is, undoubtedly, an important base of
operations, as testified by Capt. Mahan,
and his disciple, H. W. Wilson—but the
glamor of “saving ” India would count for
much in a big w’ar, even if it were at the
cost of deserting Australia. Few admirals
would be able to resist the temptation.
Furthermore, there might come explicit
orders from the boss of the Navy to let
Australia go hang. To an Australian such
orders would be idle wind, but with an
“Imperial officer” the case would be far
different.
Q. Govt, is good to its friends. The Crown
Proseeutorship, lately vacated by Attorney-
General Rutledge, has been handed to barrister
Kingsbury—a legal neophyte rejected by Brisbane
N. at the ’96 elections. Kingsbury was admitted
to the bar in ’94 and has fed sumptuously on Govt,
briefs ever since. His appointment, over the heads
of abler, more experienced and more deserving
men, is an 18-carat job.
Rumored that Hugh Nelson, the moth-
eaten Q. Tory-Premier who jumped the Leg.
Council Presidency on the death of Arthur
Palmer, is organising the ancients in the House
of Decay to solidly oppose every step towards
1 ederation. But the high priest of “Let it rip ”
and “Never mind” is not likely to be long toler-
ated as a piece of wreckage in the path leading to
Australian Nationhood.
The fixing of the salary of the Australian
Commonwealth’s Chief Justice (an office which
“ Toby ” Barton is daily twitted with ambitioning)
should, be, preceded by a general revision of the
States’ C.’s-J. At present they are remarkable
for an apparent haphazard want of proportion.
Darley draws <£3500, Way £2OOO, Griffith £3500
Onslow £l7OO and Madden £3500. If Onslow and
Way be levered up, the Federal C.-J. will probably
claim £5OOO ; but if the States’ C.-J. be revised to
a uniform £2OOO, the Federal C.-J. would probably
be content with £3OOO. The salary of the Federal
C.-J. will certainly be fixed in the light of the
salaries paid to the minor C.’s-J. and these latter
should, therefore, be subjected to revision by the
States as soon as the Commonwealth Bill passes.

Now; that the Federal Enabling Bill has
passed in N.S.W., every one entitled to an elec-
tor’s right should get one, and every holder of a
right should use it for or against when the Bill
goes to the people. At a time when the political
constitution of the continent will no doubt be
changed, a man who will not vote Yes or No is
not fit to be a citizen. It is a pity Parliament did
not make voting at the referendum compulsory
under a stiff penalty.
The N.S.W. Federal referendum is de-
finitely settled for June, probably about the 2Lst.
As Jack Want cannot possibly land in Sydney
under six weeks, if he comes at all, it will be
necessary to find another leader. The choice
seems to lie between Jonhaynes, Heydon, John
Norton, and G. Pile’s Dog—or perhaps it will be
John Norton’s wicked little pup, Barney. Kohen
with a “K.,” seems to have given it up; and
Dibbs can t sacrifice <£l3oo a year.
Round about Nambour (S.Q.), there is a
beautiful little object-lesson in full swing. Scores
and scores of farmers (all European) are engaged
m the sugar-industry ; nearly all doing fairly well,
and (mircthilc dictu) they hardly employ a solitary
kanaka, Hindoo or Syrian among them. And a
few score miles away stands the Bananaland Par-
liament House, where ancient imbeciles have the
audacity to spout, night after night, on the im-
possibility of “raising sugar profitably without
cheap alien labor.”
Queensland is just now making a fuss
about a University scheme to cap its Grammar-
school system. Out in the West, Bananaland has
hundreds of white youngsters running about
almost wild, totally unwitting of school and
schoolmaster, often ignorant of the meaning of
the words morality and decency. Surely the
needs of these children should be considered
before a penny is spent on University frills. An
area of 78,000 sq. miles in W.Q. exists wherein
there stands not one solitary schoolhouse.
Q. Govt, is preparing to push on the
building of the last link (Gladstone-Rockhamp-
ton) of the line connecting the Brisbane and the
Fitzroy. Had this been done six years ago the
province would have spared many thousands use-
“GOOD BUSINESS."
lessly expended in connecting Rockhampton with
a coastal port that didn’t exist, and in building a
terrifically expensive wharf where there wasn’t
any water. The trade of Oentralia will go to
(jladstope sure as a gun—the moment railway
connection is complete, and all the log-rolling in
the world can’t stop it.
general election, is recognised as leader of the Q.
anti-Federalists.. On the other side, Chief-Justice
Sam. Griffith is president of the Federation
League.
Denny, R.C. capitation-grant candidate,
is tipped in some quarters to head the poll at
West Adelaide to-day (Saturday), leaving* Premier
Kingston and Labor-leader Batchelor to fight for
the other seat. The bitterness of the struggle is
increased by the open quarrel between R.C. Arch-
bishop O Reily and the Laborists. But, even
should Denny be elected, the Roman Catholics (or
any other sect) will never get a denominational-
education capitation-grant from S.A. Treasury.
The moon will turn into green cheese first.
Ex-Agent-General Playford, contesting a
Tory country constituency, wobbles badly on the
Household Suffrage question—the main plank in
the Kingston programme. Playford opposes
broadening the franchise, then suggests alterations
tending to double the number of Council voters,
and concludes by suggesting postponement until
after Federation.” Poor old “ Honest Tom ”is
a political enigma. At heart, a hardshell Tory,
he has been either Premier or a member of every
Democratic Ministry in S.A. during the last 10
years.
Times have changed, and now things have
begun to go all wrong for Sydney Daily Tele-
graph. They first published that Premier Reid
would not dare to swamp N.S.W. Upper House;
then that the Lieut.-Governor would not sanction
the appointments ; then they were confident that
the people would resent the appointing of so large
a number as 12; then that 12 was not enough.
Finally, they were sure that the Council would
never back-down—only to be left as usual. And
when the referendum comes, the Telegraph witt
be sadder than ever.
Canvassing is being resorted-to to pave the
way among N.S.W. M.’sL.A. for a substantial
increase to Chairman M‘Court’s salary on account
of that official’s having so often of late filled
Speaker Abbott’s place. If Mr. M‘Court does extra
work pay is certainly earned, but the way in
which Speaker Abbott (whose health, unfor-
tunately, is very bad), has treated this office for
several sessions past should make it necessary to
have the future better defined. The employment
of Mr. M'Court in lieu of Speaker Abbott means
the employment of a roster of temporary chair-
men in lieu of Chairman M‘Court, all of whom,
on analogous grounds, have a right to hold out
hands on pay-day.
A grotesque creature is the N.S.W.
Protectionist who shouts down the Federal
Bill because, through the Braddon clause,
custom-taxed commodities will be increased
m price. “Thirty shillings,” he yells, “ will
not go as far as J2l goes now.” Every in-
telligent Protectionist has met and slain that
dragon at the entrance of his fiscal path.
Ihe Australian is getting too poor to
be born nowadays. As late as ’9l the Seven
Provinces had 128,000 births for the year, but
in ’9B, with 667,000 more population, the
births were only 117,000! The deaths in-
Txrif Sf -the births diminished.
What is it—poverty, depression, loss of the
habit of getting married, boot-trunk, or what?
The Victorian Customs seems to have
been encouraging, “unbeknownst” to the
public, the influx of Chinese to the province
by allowing the already-settled Chow to im-
port his children from China. The Chinkies
have been importing juveniles in droves, and
it did not occur to officialdom that it was
blundering until a pagan whose papers showed
he had been in the country 10 years asked to
be allowed to bring out his little boy of seven.
Then the office went into arithmetic, and
resolved to turn off the baby Chow at the
main.
At time of writing, N.S.W.' ex-At-
torney-General Jack Want’s friends are not
pleased with him. Most people believe that
his resignation was brought about by Cab-
bage-tree disapproval of the Federal Bill, but
Jack has not said so. He does not cable his
friends : “ I resign, and come to help you in
the fight ”; or even, “I am too ill to come
and help you ; but my heart is with you in
the battle.” Jack lies low, and remains a
sealed oyster up to date of this. Also, Pre-
mier Reid says King Cabbage-tree has made
a £30,000 or ,£40,000 dab in aQ. inine-spee.—
though it’s alleged that mine and money be-
long to his sister, and Jack only gets some
thousands commission.

“Alia”: When M‘Farlane, M.P., made
the statement that the N.S.W. sugar-districts
were likely to vote anti-Bill at the referendum,
the wish was evidently the father of the thought.
“We are not sugar-growers all” in these dis-
tricts, however; and lots of the sugar-growers
have sense and patriotism enough to view the
question outside the rails of their cane-patches.
M'Farlane has been a consistent opponent of the
Bill all through the piece, so he cannot be taken
as speaking without prejudice. Ask Ewing,
M.L.A., what Lismore will do.
THE JACKAL IN THE PATH.
. At the head of the cross-benches sat Mr. Lyne, wait-
ing, Micawber-like, for something to turn up.”— Syd/ne g
.Telegraph leading article, April 12.
At the head of the cross-benches sat William John,
And his pose was suggestive of hoffis ;
He was waiting for something to turn up anon,
And you bet that this something was hoffis !
He s the purest of patriots, William John Lyne,
For his voice never raises the old-fashioned whine:
Let “My country !’’ go hang—if it means he shall pine
For hoffis ! sweet hoffis ! paid hoffis !
And the glare in the eye of the said William J.
Lit lurid ambitions of hoffis :
And you felt that Bill John, if he ever did pray,
Must pray most devoutly for hoffis !
His troubles ! the fate of the Federal Bill,
With a nearer and dearer Bill waiting his fill;
So down with Australia—you can if 3 7 0 u will
And shove Long Bill Lyne into hoffis !

Duniqp Tyres'

Dunlops
Will carry all cyclists along.
No need for further anxieties,
All cyclists can ride on in peace ;
The new Multiflex tyres are beauties,
Guaranteed to ensure comfort and
ease.
We have taken up the white man’s
burden,
In helping the mighty throng
Of cyclists who wheel o’er Australia
In the delights of wheeling along,
Safe on their Multiflex Dunlops,
To accomplish by deeds on the wheel
The art of seeing the country
By means of the Dunlop wheel.
We have taken up the white man’s
burden
By giving a tyre that will stand,
To those cyclists who ride for plea-
sure —
Those of the happy band ;
So that the roughest of roads have
no terrors,
Nor the puncture-fiend the power to
detract,
From the pleasures derived from
wheeling
Way back on the Old coach track.

agreed to charge, Half-yearly, a fee of Five

next at Head office and branches, and thereafter

year.

With Branches in all the Australian Colonies and New
Zealand ; and Agencies in Tasmania, India and the East,
the United States, Hamburg and the United Kingdom.
The Bank allows interest on fixed deposits ; collects for
its customers dividends of public companies, and interest
on debentures; and invests money on their behalf in
public securities in the Colonies or Great Britain ; under-
takes the agency of other banks ; issues drafts, circular
notes and letters of credit, and conducts all customary
banking business.
The Bank also issues Circular Notes in sums of £lO or
£2O, negotiable on the Continents of Europe and America,
or in India and the East.

The cure of piles by the new “Canalin”
treatment is fully described in the pamphlet,
“Piles, and How to Cure Them,” by Albert
Sharp. Copies, with testimonials, sent free to
any colony from the dep6t, 257 George-street,
Sydney.*
The Sydney St. George’s Rifles com-
memorated St. George’s _ Day with much en-
thusiasm. Strange to say, in the published reports
of the junketings it doesn’t appear that one word
of sympathy was expressed during the proceedings
for Lieut.-Colonel Neild, commander of the regi-
ment, now under “open arrest” for alleged
insubordination.
The Commercial World (London) writes re-
garding the Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society
“ We always confidently anticipate a good year from this
vigorously-conducted Mutual Society. The circumstance,
therefore, that the figures for 1897 show no falling away
from the steady and substantial progress which is inse-
parably connected with the Society’s past career occasions
us no surprise. As a matter of fact, the Society earns its
success bv deserving it, and, that much being conceded,
the best possible has been said for the continuance of the
large measure of public support which it has hitherto
falfen to its fortunate lot to receive.” Pleasant reading.*

Bicycle Don’ts for 1899.
♦
DON’T buy an auction or sales wheel.
DON’T buy a wheel assembled from parts which are bought at a
few shillings a set.
DON’T buy a wheel because a friend recommended it to you; the
friend gets a commission.
DON’T buy a cycle from a factory that does not manufacture
bicycles exclusively.
DON’T buy a cycle from a factory new to the business; unless
you’re paid, let someone else do the testing.
DON’T buy a cycle from a dealer who sells a new make every few
months (they have no reputation to lose). _
DON’T pay higher than =£ls for a cycle with outside joints and cup
and cone bearings, unless you don’t believe in value for
money.
DON’T buy a wheel without a direct factory guarantee; you want
some security for your money.
The Go old Bicycle Co. Limited.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Melb. Eight-Hours’ people showed marvellous
ability in drawing up a programme in the worst possible
way. On Saturday last there was not a single cycle final
run during the afternoon, and people were invited to sit
down and watch four hours of preliminary events. Even
the three appearances of Bilmartin were insufficient to
atone for the tedium, though all Bill’s races were run in
that old warrior’s pluggiest style.
Bill had passed the post first three times, and
had two places to his credit when he came in for the final
of the half-mile handicap in the night. The smash oc-
curred ai thj-turn where all the smashes occur, the colli-
sion of Corbett and Crowell being the reason of Bill’s
bump against the wire fence. Bill says there was a con-
spiracy to block him out of all the races, and certainly
some of the afternoon events bore that appearance. Any-
how, it was his call for an opening which led to a swerve
in front and the subsequent smash.
* * • •
Bill’s three broken ribs will soon mend. If
there were a stake worth the while on the board next
Saturday, those ribs could be glued sufficiently to satisfy
their owner. But the doctors fear something inside has
been touched, for the Plugger’s breathing is suspiciously
heavy and difficult'. The hope of all Australia is that the
lungs of the junky, plucky Yank have escaped, and that
next season will open to him catching the field on his own
as in the days of old.
• * * *
The Eight Hours’ Wheel Race itself fell to
Wiloock, the South Australian lad, who has been scoring
splendidly of late. The smash of Martin, and the weak-
ness of Walne’s eyes, left Body, Barker, and Jackson to
fill the places of the 10-inile, and the public wandered
out disappointed that they had not been accorded such
another bri'liant battle as when Martin and M’Donald
rode a dead-heat after five miles of hot pace earlier in the
week.
* • • * /
Newhaven’s easy winning of the important City
and Sub. Handicap, with top weight, or thereabouts, on
his SJ-.vear-old back, puts Australian “ form ” very nearly
on a level with the best they can produce in England,
where Derby heroes are confined as a rule to w.f.a. races.
Within the next three months we may find the son of
Newminster (who is much more Australian-bred than
Sup ivor, the runner-up in last week’s event) pitted
against some of the classic “cracks,” and then the
Anglo-Australian gee-gee question will be practically
settled for the present. Newhaven, judging from
the fact that he gave Survivor 191 b and a beating,
is now the same animal (improved by age) that ran
away with the Melbourne Derby and Cup. He represents
Australian form of the highest grade, not necessarily
phenomenal, for they were not great horses that New-
haven donkey-licked in ’96, but undoubtedly first-class
“form.” If he can land one or more of the richest
English w.f a. stakes thisseason, real Australian thorough-
bred horseflesh, i.e., the flesh born of Australian-born
parents, will improve its market value. From an A.N.A.
point of view, the successes of a Newhaven (by New-
minster) and a Merman (by Grand Flaneur) are more
satisfactory than the news that Trenton and Carbine are
getting good stock in the land where their father was
born. Trenton and Carbine, by the way, were not even
Australian-bred. They came to light in Maoriland.
• * » •
“ H.S.”: That Carbine gave Highborn 531 b. and
a beating in Melb. Cup during the same season that High-
born won Sydney Cup, carrying 9st., does not prove that
when both horses were fit and well Carbine was anything
like 531 b., or even 331 b., better than Highborn. [The
Bulletin never said it did. But it certainly suggests
that Carbine was a better performer than Bobadil, which
was the matter under consideration in the par. of April
15. Maybe Highborn’s chance was discounted by the
fact that he carried a small boy, the inevitable disad-
vantage of a light-weight.]

It is an open secret that the “Colonial Con-
sultation ” crowd sought to obtain from Tas. Govt, per-
mission to conduct a sweep at Hobart, but failed ; and it
is pretty well understood that the Raspberry Land autho-
rities don’t mean to license anybody except “Tatt.”
Adams. The pronouncement is: “One sweep is quite
enough—we don’t propose to turn the island into a Monte
Carlo.” So that the one hope of the virtuous crowd
which is now adroitly attacking Adams in all sorts of
underhand ways, with a view to promoting a big opposi-
tion consultation-concern with a prominent bookmaker
at its head, is to get a license at Brisbane.
* * * •
The two chief events (Goodwood Handicap and
Adelaide Cup) of S.A.J.C. meeting, which begins May 6th,
look fairly good for Diffidence, with her re-handicapped
weights of 8.9 and 8.7 respectively, but it must be re-
membered that the “ pony-wonder ” has had a rare
“bucketing” of late, and maybe Alix will represent the
stable in the just-mentioned race, for which she must
have a good chance. Wild Olive, Veloce and Barleycorn
also appear well-treated. In the Cup, Le Var. Flintlock,
Dreamland and Dearix have prospects, provided Diffidence
is not at her very best.

terbury (Sydney) Cup on Saturday, a special and an
ordinary, but, from all accounts, sweep-money is not
quite so plentiful for owners as it used to be. Dr. Bow-
ker’s Beiwood was a hot favorite, but he went down
before the Lochiel mare, Blanche Florence, who started
at 10 to 1.

* * • •
The attention of Australians will be particu-
larly turned to England this year as not only will
the performances of Survivor, The Grafter, Reso-
lute and co. be eagerly followed, but also those of
Carbine and Trenton’s ’ two-year-old stock, whilst R.
Lewis and W. Delaney, two of the best of our horsemen,
will pit their prowess against Yankee Tod Sloan and the
English cracks. In swimming, F. Lane will take on the
best of the ’Ome performers ; and last, but certainly not
least, the possession of the “ashes” will be stubbornly
contested by the strong team that now guards them.
* * * •
Bendigo magistrates, including the P.M., see
the absurdity of the dictum that Ikey Mo on a box is a
person to be Put Down, while Ikey Mo off a box is a
person to be patronised. Several Ikeys got on hat-boxes,
&c., at recent goldfields sports; but when the police
proved only one transaction per case the P.M. rushed for
that way-out, and dodged the box dictum by holding
that at least two wagers must be proved in order to sus-
tain a charge of “ using a place ” for betting.
» » * *
At Maitland Show, there were 15 competitors
for the £3O high-jump, eventually won by the Parramatta
horse, Sunshine, who cleared 6ft. lJ4' n - (in a failing light),
with 13st. 81bs. up 1 ’Frisco, the Sydney runner-up, and
the two Gunnedah greys, Rattler and" Quondong, each
cleared 6ft. The water-jump was taken by Crackshot,
with 25ft. 9in. Pippo, the lively little chestnut, ridden by
clever McGee, was second with 23ft. 6in. Thelma, the
Sydney winner, refused to jump, capsizing her rider at
third attempt. Mexican Ashton, better known as
“ Warrigal Bill,” won the buck-jumping contest against
five others. All the horses ridden left nothing to be
desired, as they simply bucked themselves for all they
were worth, especially the one ridden by Phoenix, son of
the lately-deceased pugilist “ Yellow Jimmy.” Phoenix’s
mount bucked continuously for 4£ minutes at one stretch.
Thiebaut, a noted Tamworth rider, who mounted his
horse bridle-less, was the only one unseated.
* ♦ * *
One trot at Bathurst (N.S.W.) show would
make a good bar-room Yankee caricature. Driver was
under weight, and carried a bag of stones to “ draw the
scales.” When in the lead the ballast shifted, and the
driver was in trouble trying to hold it in with one hand
and drive with the other, but the strain was too great,
and the bag fell out, whereupon driver pulled up, hauled
it back into the sulky, and started in pursuit. Next
round string came untied from neck of bag, and the gib-
bers poured out on the track, amidst the jeers and cat-
calls of the crowd.
* * * •
The loss of Capt. Clark in his yacht Queenie, in
Hobson’s Bay, is quite parallel to that of Capt. Coles, in
the ironclad Captain, of his design, in the British
Channel. Clark’s Queenie was nicknamed “The Coffin”
by Melb. yachtsmen, who considered her build dangerous.
Clark flouted them by going in her on every possible
occasion. Thus did Coles voyage in the Captain, on her
trial trip, when she turned turtle, and was lost with all
hands, about 220. She had cost the nation about a
quarter of a million. Childers, as head of the Admiralty,
had such faith in her that he sent his son, as an officer,
on th > trip, and he was drowned. Reed, the eminent
naval constructor, and other high authorities, had con-
demned the Captain as unsafe.
* » • •
Rarely is such a rattling' contest seen in an 8-oar
race as that at St. Ignatius’ regatta, last Saturday, on
Lane Cove River. Three boats started, representing
Glebe, Sydney, and North Shore rowing clubs. Sydney
led for a part of the distance, but Glebe fairly rowed
them down, and won a magnificent race by a few yards.
* * * *
Sydney Alhambra A.C.’s 9st. novice boxing
tournament was continued and concluded Saturday
night. The second round supplied a gory battle —Poet
and Jones were the principals. How they did thump-
and smash each other for five rounds ! Blood smothered
their classic mugs, especially Poet’s, which received so
much attention that two teeth were shifted, while a third
was forced through the upper lip. The house yelled
again as the pair of pugs, alternately rushed in and reeled 1
back before a hail of blows. No gamer man than Poet
ever doffed a shirt. Both went through the stipulated
term, and, despite the tremendous punishment adminis-
tered, were still strong and fit to go. Jones got the-
decision on points, but Poet was not far behind. Read,
thought to have a cinch on the big end of the £3O prize-
money, went under in this round to Williams, who-
stepped into the ring looking more like a schoolboy of 17
than a candidate for the honors of pugdom. He wore
blue silk trunks, white shoes, and black socks, was of’
slight build, though well put together, had skin the color
of a girl’s, thin arms and a good-looking, youthful face.
The roped square was about the last place where Wil-
liams should have been, as far as appearances went, but
when he got into action a different aspect was placed l
upon the matter. Beneath that nancyfied exterior were-
bulldog grit and all the instincts of a born fighter. Wil-
liams met Jones in the final on Saturday night, and was:
declared winner after a very exciting and evenly con-
tested bout of eight rounds, during which not a single-
knock-down occurred, nor was a single drop of blood
spilt.
* * * *
Veteran pug Abe Hicken would seem to have:
lagged superfluous, for his well-boomed benefit at Golden:
Gate A.O. (Sydney), last week, only drew 40 people all
told—deadheads included. Abe is a much-benefited man.
He had a couple in Sydney years ago, two or three ini
Melbourne, a similar number “down under” recently,
and went back to Melb. last Saturday for another “ fare-
well,” after which he goes Englandwards again, there to-
remain for good and aye. Pity to see the old-timer in
such straits, especially when one recalls that, not so many
years ago, a cheque of his for twenty thou, would have
been marked good. Abe told writer that when Chester'
was beaten for Sydney Derby he lost £lO,OOO, which
amount was laid to £Booo—both parties putting up their
money beforehand. Hicken has two sons—jockeys. One
n England, and doing well, lately sent his mother a draft
for £lOO.
* * * *
Says Abe Hicken : “Jem Mace, England’s last
prize-money champion, was, when I left London a few
months ago, running a paying pub. He is 68 years of
age, and has his fourth wife, by whom there are four
children—the eldest six years, and the youngest eight-
months.” Jem is pretty keen on business, and doesn’t-
allow friendship to affect him in that quarter. Abe paid
the old warrior a visit, and, while there, had to part up-
like an ordinary traveller—and Abe was none too well-
fixed.
• * • *
After vainly trying for months to corner Yankee
Lucifer, N.S. Welshman Harry Cansdell, literally spoiling
for a go, took on Teuton Lorenz at Sydney Alhambra,
Friday night last, for the middleweight supremacy of the-
province, under French rules—miscalled Graeco-Roman.
Lorenz is somewhat of the weedy order, though fairly
well - developed about shoulders and arms. Cansdell
stood sturdy and hairy as a young bull—brown, too, as
tanned leather, and certainly much above middleweight.
On appearances, ’twas a guinea to a gooseberry in the
local champ.’s favor : but the German, wiry and wary, let
Cansdell do the graft and twist, turn, squeeze, wrenchi
and lift to his heart’s content, Lorenz being alert with a
“stop” for every hold, albeit watchful the while that
the hammer-lock or dreaded hug had no show. Twenty
minutes of this, then 10 minutes’ rest and a further 20
minutes of ’rastling without a fall, sort of filled onlookers
up. Several went home. Up to now Cansdell was
aggressor, but lacked activity, thus missing several
chanoes ; also, the severe strain sustained so long was
telling on him palpably. Fifteen minutes after third
bout started both went sprawling on the mat—Cansdell
under—a fair fall to Lorenz. Facing each other for the
fourth time, Cansdell looked badly used-up, while Lorenz
seemed fit for another hour or two, and went to work
prepared to take risks. Five minutes later the German
got a bear-like neck-grip on, and bore his man down amid
intense excitement, until latter, almost black in the face,
was forced over on to one shoulder and both hips. Second
fall, and the matoh (which called for first two out of three)
to Lorenz.
Writer came away satisfied that the winner is
nothing to rave about, whilst Cansdell, without a win to
his credit up to date (if memory serves), has gone off con-
siderably since Mons. Victor and Perryman beat him, and
he’rastled that all-night draw with Sam Matthews, nine
years ago. America and England are full of middle-
weights who could “lay over” both in one ring, and
think no great “ punkins ”of the job, either. The task
would have been an exercise-canter for M’Caffery, who
came to Sydney with “Buff” Costello, the pug., in the
eighties.
* * * *
Sydney “ Rocks ” phenomenon Griffo, perhaps
the most marvellous boxer the world has seen, is now at
the end of his tether. From Chicago comes word that he
is what everybody on this side suspected long ago, a rav-
ing maniac, and will probably wind-up in a straight-
ja' ket. Commenting on the matter, N.Y. Journal says
Griffo was “ a villain of splendid physique and tireless
energy.” [Fancy the Griffo we knew being called a vil-
lain !] While in gaol he was put to work shovelling coal,
and was a happy man then. He could get no liquor in
prison, and regarded his term as a training period, of
which he took ample advantage. This sad termina-
tion to Griffo’s career will cause many a pang in the
hearts of those who knew him best. Few studied the
little champion more than the writer. Griffo’s nature
was all right. Environments were responsible for his
bad breaks—he had never been taught better.
* * * *
Billy Barnes, the Nottingham cricketer who
died the other month, scored at least one record per-
formance in Australia. Some 12 years ago there was an
exciting match on Sydney ground between Shaw and
Shrewsbury’s team and N.S.W., the local men winning by
a few runs. The Britishers didn’t like it—they had
backed themselves to pull the game out of the fire—and
on the night of their discomfiture “ words ” arose in a
pub., with the result that Billy B. let out his left at one
of the victorious Cornstalks, missed him, and put his fist
through the panel of a swing door. A wonderful drive
for “ nowt.”
* * * *
In this issue will be found portrait of the late
Edward James Gregory, the eldest of the great Sydney
cricketing family, the “Graces of Australia.” Outside
his cricketing capabilities, Ned, as he was familiarly and
lovingly called by the cricketing public in his younger
days, was a prominent member of a Sydney amateur
theatrical club, from which blossomed several actors who
have made their mark. Their meetings were held in an
old tin-covered building in the vicinity of Circular Quay,
a godsend for the Rocks push, who went to shower stones
on the iron roof whilst the acting was going on. In
1851 Gregory was a Fort-street schoolboy, and in that
year carried off the Sir Charles Nicholson prize. He was a
good elocutionist, with a remarkably retentive memory,
and at cricket and other gatherings Ned was always
one of the first to be called upon for a contribution. In
’67, N.S.W. started a run of losing games against Victoria,
and it was not till ’74 that she secured a victory, due
chiefly to the batting of Ned Gregory and Charley
Bannerman. In the ’75 match, with 7 wickets down for
21, Gregory scored 65 (not out) out of a total of 116, and
18 out of 102. But, perhaps, the item which will live
longest in Ned Gregory’s cricketing career was the part
he took in the historic single-wicket match in ’72, in
which the brothers Ned, Dave and Charley Gregory re-
presented N.S.W., and defeated Wills, Cosstick and
Conway, of Victoria, by 5 runs, after an exciting three
days’ contest.

“Saved!” “Has one aspect of the Sidney
Safe Deposit occurred to you? Most people insure
against loss by fire and receive money compensation for
property destroyed. But there are some things—valuable
papers, heirlooms, etc—which can never be replaced, and
the loss of which often means the loss of a fortune. The
Safe Deposit, for a comparatively small premium, not
only insures against the loss of these things, it saves the
articles themselves. What is a guinea or two a year com-
pared with a document upon which thousands depend ? ”
Safe Deposit is in Ash-st., back of Palings, off George-st.*
Dear Bulletin. The insurance - man
would like to tell his version of the “Daly”
story. Certainly he was had, badly had, and he
did believe that Daly was waiting to deal out
black eye. Also he was nervous, for he had lived
long and had experiences, and it would take him
more than two days to repent of his sins and
transgressions. But the little joke was too much
for one of that merry band of fakirs ; he bubbled
over. And the insurance-man caught on. He
paid his tenner, stood the chaff, and took his
drinks with the rest. Then he turned nasty,
threatened prosecution for the obtainal of money
by false pretences, and finally got his tenner back
intact. So each side enjoyed its bit of fun, but
the other lot paid for the drinks. The insurance-
man reckons he came out top dog, and the writer
of your par. is advised to invest some cash in
sticking-plaster in preparation for his meeting
with yours—That Agent.

MASSEY-HARRIS COMPANY Ltd.,
TORONTO, CANADA.
Australasian Head Quarters : William Street, Melbourne
Branches : Dunedin—Sydney—Brisbane
Adelaide—Hobart—Nortliam.
Depots and Agencies almost everywhere
The Wolseley Sheep-shearing
Machine Co., Ltd.
The Wolseley Company was originally started in
Sydney in 1887, but was floated into a large con-
cern in London in 1889 with a capital of £200,000.
The company have up to the present time fitted
over 450 sheds with an approximate total of 10,000
stands, the number of sheep shorn last shearing
season by Wolseley machines alone in Austra-
lasia being estimated at 25,000,000, while in South
America, the Cape, and other sheep - bearing
countries we are informed equally large and grow-
ing business is being done.
The Wolseley Company are in the unique posi-
tion of being the pioneers, and have held the
supremacy over all other makes of machines dur-
ing the 12 years of their existence. This speaks
volumes for the duality and superiority of their
manufactures.
The company’s swnding challenge to all comers
has been in force for years, but has never yet been
accepted, viz., it will erect an equal number of
stands with any other maker, the loser to pay
the winner’s expenses only. This shows their im-
plicit confidence in their own machine, as with the
reputation it already possesses it has nothing to
gain excepting the increased confidence of the
squatter, and if beaten it would be a great adver-
tisement to its yictor.
A notable fact is that shearers purchase for
themselves Wolseley machine shears and sundries,
when proceeding to a machine shed fitted with
those of other makers.
The following are a few records made by shearers
using Wolseley machines, and indicate what a
great advantage they possess over hand shears :
•T. Power shore 315 sheep in one day of eight
hours ; Norman shore an average of 200 per day
for over six weeks ; Louis J. Pulley shore over
31,000 in one season. These records were made in
Queensland.
The benefit accruing to the squatter through
using machines is at least a gain of Jib. of wool
per sheep, this in stations contiguous to railways ;
while those still further away in the back-blocks
save, it is estimated, at least fib. of wool, owing
to the cut with hand shears there being much
rougher.
The M'Caugheys, who shear about 1,000,000
sheep annually, have the following sheds fitted
with Wolseley machines — viz., Dunlop, Toorale,
Doree, G-oolgumbla, Rockwood, Barenya, and
North Yanko. Henry Ricketson has the follow-
ing sheds fitted, viz.: —Bitlabong, Barratta, Ara-
tula, Delatite and Yarronvale. The Osbornes
have fitted Douro, Redbank, Kulki, Curran-
clooley, Orange Plains, Foxlow, Bangaroo, Thorn-
dale and Dirnaseer. Cobb and Co. have fitted
Murrumbidgerie, Burrenbilla, Buckiinguy and
Hereford. These sheds have not been fitted
simultaneously, but one has succeeded another
owing to the satisfactory results obtained at the
initial sheds. At the company’s works in Birming-
ham, which are considered second to none in
equipment of machinery and up to date tools, 350
hands are employed, all parts of the machinery
being made there. In Sydney, the company also
have well-equipped workshops, where repairs to
the machines, &c., can be effected when neces-
sary. . •
It appears to us that it would be materially to
the advantage of all squatters to fit up their sheds
■with machines, the continued drought having
lessened the number of sheep to such an extent
that every half-pound of wool gained will mean at
least fourpence, as with scarcity of the commodity
its value must be enhanced. For example, a
station shearing, say, 30,000 sheep would gain
15,0001 b. wool, which at Bd. per lb. would equal
£5OO.
The approximate cost of all necessary machinery,
including engine erected and ready for work,
would be within £350, leaving a clear profit of
£l5O and all machinery paid for on the first shear-
ing. We would strongly advise our squatting
friends to digest the above.
Every inducement is offered by the company’s
agents, Messrs. Dangar, G-edye and Co., who are
willing to supply the shearing machinery on four
months’ credit, thus enabling the grower to have
his wool shorn and sold before being called upon
to pay for the machinery. As a further instance
of the gain to squatters it may be mentioned that
in the case of all machine sheds having had the
misfortune to be destroyed by fire,, the owners
have in each instance fitted up their new sheds
again with shearing machinery.
The bicycle shown in successful operation at the
recent show (Easter) of the Royal Agricultural
Society of N.S.W., at Sydney, goes to prove that
this company is up-to-date. This was its initial
trial and it is hard to say what the future pos-
sibilities are likely to be for such motive power,
and as the bicycle is fast growing in favor with
shearers in search of employment, it is only
reasonable to suppose that they will have the
patent shearing gear attached to. their bicycles.
The gear complete and fitted with sharpening
wheel will weigh in all about 151 b. It is estimated
that one man pedalling will be capable of driving
two shearing machines. This will be of great
benefit to the selectors, and enable, them to have
the advantage of shearing by machinery.
The company was awarded first and only prize
at the Sydney Easter show of the Royal Agricul-
tural Society of N.S.W. for their sheep-shearing
machinery.*
The Bulletin is asked to mention that in
its recent reference to the death of Mr. Sackville
Kidman, “Sack,” the familiar abbreviation of
the first name, was inadvertently printed “Jack.”
Mary Fairney writes The Bulletin from
20 Pomeroy-street, Cardiff, S. Wales, asking it to
find out for her “about some relatives of mine,
viz. Henry Golightly, who went to Ballarat about
40 years ago, also Elizabeth Golightly (maiden
name), both of Howdon-on-Tyne. I am niece of
the above, and desire to hear from them.”

Judge Rogers, at a Cootamundra Quarter
Sessions, has laid down that a bona fide lodger at
aN.S.W. hotel may treat a visiting friend after
bar-hours, if the lodger pays and the drinks are
consumed outside the public bar. In the case in
hand the drinks were consumed in a room behind
the bar, at 12.30 a.m.
An exchange prints :
Some mischievous, ignorant newspaper scribe in Sydney
has discovered that Federation will be injurious to
Catholics, and will mean the deification of the Yellow
Pup, and a return to the days of the rack and thumb-
screw. No Catholic, it appears, will ever be permitted to
cross the awe-inspiring portals of the Federal Upper
House. All which we take the liberty to characterise as
pure asinine drivel. Federation will kick bigotry once
and forever out of Australia, and under the one flag
Catholics will receive the public support to which their
individual merits will entitle them.
To which The Bulletin says Amen. Sectarian
bigotry is civic, and even provincial, but not
national. The wretched things who formed the
rank-and-file of Parkes’ political tail in the days
when he worked the Kiama Ghost would be im-
possible in a Federal atmosphere. The bigger the
men, the less court they pay to priest or parson.
Arthur Russell, American dentist, 8 Bourke-
street E., Melb., makes beautiful teeth or teeth
beautiful, whichever is necessary. Also painless
operations.*
“ Old North ” : Recently asked a Queens-
land M.L.A. for a farming constituency why he
opposed Federation during his campaign. His
reply throws light on a whole lot of things.
“Well,” he said, candidly, “they’re a damned
thick-headed lot up my way. I tell ’em Federa-
tion means flooding the local market with Southern
produce. They understand that all right. My |
opponent tried to explain the matter in detail, and
got his auditors and himself hopelessly fogged. I
don’t understand the Commonwealth Bill any
more than a reaping-machine does, but I do know
my constituents and the extent of their intelli-
gence.”

In a recent Worker (Q.) article, editor
Higgs farewells his readers, and in so doing
takes credit to his paper for routing Mcll- |
wraith out of the country, exiling Tozer, and j
driving Hugh Nelson into the Council. Without
wishing to depreciate the Worker’s really valuable
services, it might be well to point out that that
organ’s former “ whole hog ” policy did more
towards retarding reform than the devices of all
the cronk Boodlewraithians put together. If the
Parliamentary Labor-party had been as narrow as
the Worker was in its early years, Democracy
would not stand half as well as it does to-day in |
Queensland. |
A study of the voting at Q. general
election reveals some interesting figures. Old
Ministerialists secured 375, new Ministerialists
700, old Independent Ministerialists 1219, new
Independent Ministerialists 918, new Laborists
765, old Laborists 979, new Drakeites 576, old
Drakeites 926—average votes per man. Also, out
of seven Cabinet Ministers with nine portfolios, |
four with six portfolios represent the Centre and
North which return only 11 Ministerialists
while the South returning 31 Ministerialists
has only three portfolios. Under these conditions,
an agitation for Southern separation may break
out at any moment.
The April number of The Bookfellow,
to be issued on 29th instant, will be still better and
bigger than its forerunners. Among the contri-
butions are articles on a French poet —Baudelaire,
and an Australian one—Mary Hannay Foott. j
There is a notice of artist Tom Roberts and his
work, with portrait; new books are summed and
reviewed; and “The Bookfellow” gossips rele-
vantly to everything and nothing. The price of
The Bookfellow is 3d. post-free; and some
copies of the three previous numbers are still for
sale at the same price.

A Baby lion!
♦♦♦♦♦♦♦♦♦
Did you ever see one? It is as playful and
harmless as a kitten. You might have one
with you for weeks and not suspect danger.
But, beware! Some day it will surely
spring, and the fight for life is at hand.
It is just so with a cough. You may carry
one with you for weeks and not think of
danger. But the danger is there. Some day
the fight will be on, and it is a question which
will win, Consumption or You.

promptly cures all baby coughs: but it can-
not always cure the full grown coughs of the
most advanced stages of disease. Yet even
here it will bring comfort and ease. Then
use it early for acute colds, coughs, bron-
chitis and all pulmonary troubles.
Beware of cheap imitations. See that the
name, Ayer’s Cherry Pectoral, is blown in the
glass of each bottle. Put up in large and small
bottles.

Land-selection in pretty big blocks con-
tinues in Albany district of W. A.—unfortunately,
by absentees. One thousand-acre block in the

mining M.P., with a view to apple-for-export
cultivation.
“Scrip:” How’s this for loss and luck?
One night a while back I lost a .£L note. Three
nights afterwards, while battling to the office
against a gale of wind, a piece of paper blew
against my leg and stuck there. Examined it
wdien I reached the door ; it was a pound note !
And here’s another (present scribe can support his
tale by affidavit from three reputable citizens who
witnessed the occurence): Went “ drifting ” for
flathead off Great Mackerel Peach, Hawkesbury
River, and while trying to pull up fish, lost a big
hook with brass attachment. Landed and had
lunch, and on next drift, an hour-and-a-half later,
same fisher caught big flathead with his hook and
brass wire in its mouth. Mind you, the same
fisher caught the flathead—-though there were
three other liars in the boat. What are the odds
against such an occurrence ? HoW many millions
to one against the boat happening upon exactly
the same track, or the wounded fish accommodat-
ing itself to the boat’s drift, and biting again—at
the bait of the same fish-liar ?

“Lemuria”: Was watching a cinemato-
graph show Saturday night; picture of a Harbor
steamer emptying its crowd on to the wharf, and
right down the front walked a most affectionate
couple. A lady close to me glared at it for a
moment, then seizing her husband’s arm, hissed :
“You wretch! that’s you in front with that
strange girl in the gay hat—don’t tell me lies ;
you said you were going to Homebusb. Ugh !
you beast!” It wasn’t in the least like him, but
the figure of the deceiver and his girl fled across
the canvas so quickly that he had no time to
defend himself, or to point out the discrepancies.
He asked her to stay and see the show over again
in a calm and judicial frame of mind, and pro-
mised that she would discover that the man
coming off the boat resembled him just as
much as a skirt-dance does the five books
of Moses. But she refused. She said
it was only a scheme of his to get at the pro-
prietor, who was doubtless his accomplice, and
induce him to alter the man in the picture. Said
also she had no doubt he and the cinematograph-
man kept a joint harem somewhere. Said they
were, jointly and severally, a Mormon, a bald-
headed wretch, a bigamist, and a deceiver. Also,
that he had never loved her, and that he and the
“cine.” proprietor had doubtless laughed in
secret at her trusting heart for many years.
Believed that they had got up the show expressly
to insult her. Said she would defend her character
to the last, and would prove her innocence of the
foul charges that they were no doubt conspiring
to bring against her, and would also have a
divorce, and her sainted mother would turn in her
grave. Then she removed her husband in custody,
and under dark suspicions.

A Maryborough (Q.) kanaka, lately con-
victed.of assaulting a white and threatening him
with a knife, pleaded that he was a Sunday-school
teacher, and that he was drunk at time of assault.
More “ converted ” nig.
Printed that the latest feat of the Broken
Hill “ pantry thief” was to visit the house of Sub-
Inspector Travers, where he was disturbed and
decamped. Judging from his fondness for cold
victuals, that thief is a policeman.
Sydney chessists are sore over the failure
of the projected match for the championship of
Australia between Messrs. Jacobsen (holder) and
Wallace (challenger). The cause was a quarrel
over the rights of the combatants to compel pay-
ment from the daily journals for publication of the
gainesi-played. Mr. Jacobsen, as chess-editor of
Sydney Telegraph, was sure of his emoluments,
and Wallace was trying to get his share from S.M.
Herald. The haggling over this point was so
protracted that Jacobsen was enabled to resign
without risking the loss of his title by defeat.
Wallace now professes willingness to play the
match on his opponent’s own terms, rather than
not at all. As Jacobsen declines to play on any
terms at all, he having resigned, the Wallaceites
declare that he is afraid to do so, and Jacobsen’s
friends accuse Wallace of cowardice in not play-
ing when he could. All of which is very pretty.
Government Notices.
PUBLIC SERVICE BOARD.
5 Young-street, Sydney,
21st April, 1899.
A COMPETITIVE EXAMINATION of CANDIDATES
(Male and Female) for employment as “POSTAL
and TELEGRAPH ASSISTANT ” in the Postal and Elec-
tric Telegraph Department will be held in Sydney, and
at various country centres which will be fixed as far as
possible to suit the convenience of candidates, on the 26th
June next. The estimated number of appointments to
be made is 40. Candidates must not be less than 16 nor
more than 19 years of age on the day of examination, of
good character, and in sound health. Persons already
employed in the General Division of the Public Service
will be admitted to the examination, provided their age
does not exceed 30 years. The subjects of examination
will be as follow :—(1) Handwriting; (2) English, Dicta-
tion ; (3) English, Parsing and Analysis ; (4) Arithmetic;
(5) Geography. The successful candidates will be ap-
pointed on probation as vacancies occur, and be paid at
the rate of £39 per annum for the first year and £52 for
the second year. On completion of second year of ser-
vice, they will be examined for permanent appointment,
on passing'which they will be entitled to receive £65 per
annum, with periodical increments under the terms of
the Regulations, copies of which can be obtained at this
Office. Application to attend the examination must be
made on a form for the purpose, which can be obtained
at the Office of the Board, and must reach the under-
signed not later than the Ist June. Candidates will be
required to pay an examination fee of 55., which must be
lodged with a Clerk of Petty Sessions or the Treasury,
and the receipt therefor forwarded to the Secretary of
the Board with the form of application.
By order of the Board,
J. W. HOLLIMAN, Secretary.

Melbourne, 18th April, 1899.
To the Shareholders of The Colonial Bank of Australasia Limited.
Ladies and Gentlemen, —We beg to report that we have audited the accounts of The Colonial Bank of Australasia
Limited for the half-year ended 31st March, 1899, the assets and liabilities at the branches being taken on the certifi-
cates of the managers and accountants, and that the accompanying balance-sheet is, in our opinion, correct. We
have also to report that during such audit we have not observed or become acquainted with any breach of the “ Com-
panies Acts ” committed by the company, or any director, manager, auditor, or shareholder thereof, and
that, so far as we are in a position to form an opinion, the balance-sheet and accounts have been drawn up in accord-
ance with the provisions of the “ Companies Acts,” and present a correct view of the state of the company’s affairs,
and that all our requisitions in regard to the shareholders’ as well as the private balance-sheet have been duly com-
plied with.
We are, Ladies and Gentlemen, yours faithfully,
THOMAS BUSSELL, I
J. JOHNSTON SMART, f
Directors.

Bayswater, Sydney, October, 1898.
W. H. MAlyon, Esq., Man. Chemist, Ballarat.
Dear Sir, —For years past (about ten), I have been
troubled with “ Acne ” in its worst form, and have sought
advice from the best Australian and London doctors, with
only fair results. A Victorian friend of mine, some four
or five weeks ago, sent me a tin of your “ Ligmolliene,’'
and speaking very highly of its wonderful curative powers.
I naturally took same “ Cum Grano Salis,” and was in-
clined not to use it ; eventually I tried your ointment
with truly marvellous results, and am now entirely cured;
and think people who suffer from skin diseases are greatly
indebted to you for your wonderful remedy. I shall
advise all my friends with skin troubles to use same.
Wishing “ Ligomolliene ” all the success it richly deserves,
Vours faithfully, GEORGE J. O’REGAN.

THEN IT WENT ON ALL RIGHT.
The writer of the letter which I am going to.
copy for you in a moment has a complaint to.
make. Rather, perhaps, a complaint to place on
record, as the reason for it is passed away for the.
present, and she hopes—and we hope with her—
that it may not return. The complaint does not.
refer to any relative, friend, or foe, but to her own
heart. It did not work well. It was weak, and!
for a long time she was unable to find means to
make it do better. Which was a serious matter,
inasmuch as the vigor of the circulation of the
blood always depends upon the force wherewith
the heart drives it.
Still, it seems to me we ought to be a bit indul-
gent towards the heart in view of the labor it has
to ]) rfomi. Remember that it never takes a full
minute’s rest at one time, night or day, from the
instant it begins at your birth until, like a muffled
drum, it stops for good and all—life’s funeral
march to the grave being over. During all this
while, ten years or a hundred, the heart has got to
keep on pumping blood through your body at the
rate of from 130 strokes a minute in childhood to
50 or 60 in old age. If you happen to have a
mechanical turn of mind, it may interest you to
figure out how much this stands for in units of
horse-power for a given case and time. If not,
you can take my word for it that, merely as a
machine, the heart deserves your respect. So long
as it goes ahead steadily, up hill and down dale,,
hammering away softly but strongly, you haven’t,
a word to say for or against it; but when it begins,
to get weak, maybe skipping a stitch now andi
then, you call in the doctor, who puts the tip of
his finger just below the base of your left thumb,
looks wise and solemn (as befits the occasion), and
says, “Ah, yes, yes; I see, I see.” But what does
he see ? He doesn’t tell you that; he leaves medi-
cine, and mentions when he will look in again.
But as to the letter I spoke of. “Eor many
years,” the lady says, “ I suffered from indigestion
and weak heart. Very little exertion made me
feel weary and tired. Cold, clammy sweats broke
over me. I had a poor appetite, and after meals
an aching pain at the chest and a miserable sink-
ing feeling at the stomach. I had also much pain
at the left side, and my heart would flutter so as
to frighten me. At length I became so weak I
was barely able to get about, being no longer able
to do my housework.
“ Owing to the trouble at my heart I obtained
no proper rest at night, and often walked about
my bedroom at night. Many times these attacks
were so bad I thought I was dying. During the
day a sense of suffocation sometimes came upon
me, and I was obliged to go to the door for fresh
air.
“ Year after year I suffered like this ; now a
little better, now as bad as I could be. In No-
vember, 1887, while on a visit to Croydon, my son-
in-law persuaded me to try Mother Seigel’s Syrup.
He got me a bottle, and after taking it I experi-
enced great relief. The pain at my heart was
easier, and I felt better as a whole. I could eat
well, and the food agreed with me.
“ I now felt encouraged to continue using this
remedy. Soon I was in better health than for
years, the heart trouble having disappeared alto-
gether. Since that time when I feel anything
ailing me a few doses of Mother Seigel’s Syrup
never fail to give the desired relief. I have told
many persons of the benefit I have derived from
it, and hereby consent to your publishing this
statement should you wish to do so.”—(Signed)
(Mrs.) William Harrington, near Wickford Hill,
Clare, Suffolk, November 12, 1897.
Now what ailed Mrs. Harrington’s heart ? Why,,
precisely the same things that ailed her lungs, her
nerves and her muscles — weakness. Therein she-
is right. It was a weak heart, but not a diseased ’
heart. The heart is a muscle, and (seeing the pro-
digious lot of work it has to do) necessarily a*
strong, active muscle. But it will not work with-
out pay any more than you or I will. With all the
rest of the body, it has got to be sustained and
strengthened by food. Here we have the point,
then. The lady was afflicted with chronic indiges-
tion. For this reason her whole body grew weak
—the heart, of course, with other parts of the
engine. Hence all the symptoms she names. Her
immense all-round weakness and puller-down is
that same old dyspepsia. When Mother Seigel’s
Syrup made the digestion of plenty of food pos-
sible, the heart went on all right, like a newly-
wound clock.’'

“ I am busy now, but if you will call m the
course of half-an-hour I shall be pleased to
answer any questions on the subject ol my
cure.” This was the answer furnished to
our reporter by Mr. J. S. Myers, proprietor
of the Auburn Bakery, Hairow Road,
Auburn, near Sydney. Conscquen ly our
reporter called again at Mr. My ers residence,
and was shown into the drawing-room,
where that gentleman shortly afterwards
aP £ e \Vhat was the cause of your illness,
asked the repoi ter ? ” ,
“ I cannot tell you with certainty, replied
Mr M\ers, “ but as I come of a pai ucularly
healthy and long-lived family I am inclined
to think my illness was the result of an
accident, A
Fall -from a Horse,
when I was about 14 years of age had, I
believe, no little to do with it, as I con-
tinually suffered from headache afterwards.
I was in the South Sea Islauds, too, for
some years, and the climate by no means
improved my health. It is since I returned
from the islands that I began to be troubled
with
Severe Nervous Headaches,
which chiefly affected the back of my head.
These we e much intensified by the confine-
ment and hard mental work which I had to
undergo in the conducting of my business,
and especially in keeping my accounts.
I was, in addition, obliged to superintend
the operations in the bakehouse, wheie
the stifling atmosphere had a tendency to
make my general health worse. I was not
able to take a holiday, and, as a conse-
q lence,
My Health Broke Down,
I viecame very nervous and irritable. My
headaches came onat more frequent intervals,
and were exceedingly violent. I couldu t
bear the children’s noise, even if they
chattered in my presence, and such small
trifles which once gave me no concern
worried me exceedingly. I began to lose
my appetite, and often when I was called to
my meals I could do no more than make a
mere pretence of eating. Then in ligestion
set in. Nothing I ate agreed with me, and
my food would lie undigested on my stomach
for long enough, giving me considerable
Pain and Distress.
My system was entirely out of tune. I was
completely run down. I pissed long, weari-
some, sleepless nights turning from side to
side, in vain trying to get some rest, and in
the mornings I was dull and tired, and felt
Entirely imafole to attend
to my business. In fact, I was compelled to
put my books aside.”
“Did you consult any doctors, Mr.
Myers?” , ,
“ Yes. Before I came here I was attended
by several doctors, but they faile I to cuie
me, though occasionally I obtained tempo-
rary relief. One of the doctors said I should
always have the headaches.
“ And did you get no relief ?”
“ No, I got no better, in fact, I continued
to get’ worse. My continued ill-health
caused me to suffer from
Great Depression of Spirits,
r could take no interest in anything, not. in
my business, nor yet in what was happening
around me. Nothing gave me any pleasure.
I lost greatly in weight, and, of course,
became correspondingly weak. A languid
and depressed feeling was upon me continu-
ally. I was very thin and my
Face was Parchment-like
in appearance.”
“ Did you try any other medicines in
addition to those prescribed by your medical
attendants ?”
“ Yes ; 1 tried various medicines which
were recommended to me by friends and
neighbours, and also medicines I saw
advertised, but I obtained
No sort of Satisfaction
till I tried Clements Tonic.”
“ And that you found beneficial?”

found sufficient to cause
A decided Improvement
in the condition of my nerves. There is not

better sleep at night.”

“Yes the headaches began to decrease

as I c ntinued with Clements lonic they

My digestive organs work, d properly again,
and I "once more began to take a renewed
