If you Love a Man: Love Him
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Can you imagine anything more beautiful,”
said the youth, lying back in his long chair with a
sigh of content, and gazing out over the range of
country, and the distant sea that stretched like
blue steel away to the horizon.
“ Very jolly,” answered the girl, who was his
wife, and who leaned over the verandah-railing
watching a snail on one of her pet bulbs. “ Shall
I order the Stilton or the Gorgonzola ? ”
“Oh, my dear, for goodness sake don’t worry
about cheese on a morning like this—it’s —it’s in-
decent,” he said, looking at her sideways, with a
burning match suspended over the bowl of his
pipe.
“ Well, I got it for you, and you don’t do much
for me now. Just look at that shell-slug eating
my narcissus.”
Geoffrey rose languidly, went down the verandah-
steps into the blazing sun, captured the snail, de-
posited it on a big stone, and with another smashed
it to atoms. There was an expression of disgust
on his face, but he looked up and said, “ Well, I
guess he never knew what struck him ”; and the
girl laughed and ran indoors.
* * * *
They had been married three months, and the
girl had never known life could be so fair. She
had duly written to her people, “Geoffrey is so
good to me.” He was her hero, and she raved of
him to all her female acquaintances, and they,
being matrons, and well into the toleration stage
of domestic bliss, shook their heads wisely and
said, “ Don’t spoil him, my dear—never let a man
know how much you care for him ” ; and she, con-
cluding that they ought to know, set out in her
silly, thoughtless way to ruin the life that had
begun so well.
He was a fool; there was not the slightest doubt
of that; and, besides being a fool, he was a senti-
mental, a sensitive, and a pig-headed fool, which
is a difficult combination to deal with. He had
duly recognised that in his marriage he had made
a fool of himself, and had just concluded never to
let her know, but to make the best of it, and to
make his married life fit, as nearly as possible, the
dreams he had dreamed.
And then she made a friend of a girl a year
older than herself, but yet younger, in that she
was unmarried ; and to this friend she preached
the goodness and gentleness of her Geoffrey, by
morning, by noon, and by night. And when the
friend saw the pain in his eyes, and asked her why,
then, she snubbed him so unmercifully, she wisely
shook her head, as she had seen the matrons do,
and said, “ My dear, never let a man know how
much you care for him.” And the girl-friend
sighed, for she liked this gentle fool who rode so
daringly and shot so straight and suffered so
silently.
A year later the end came; an advt. appeared
in the morning papers as follows :
A man, formerly a gentleman, seeks a divorce from his
wife, and desires to correspond with a lady who seeks a
divorce from her husband. Write, in the first place, to
A.8.C., G.P.O.
People laughed and wondered who was re-
BDOnsible for the hoax, and if he would get any
answers He did get a great many, a number that
surprised him, and among them one the hand wri-
ng™ initial of which were familiar -That
evening his wife told him she expected the girl-
friend to dinner, and Geoffrey, listening to his
conscience, expressed his sorrow that he would not
b6 ‘ J But you must, Geoffrey; you are always selfish
an « Yes; but'l told Smith I would see him to-
nl“hSmith’s away,”—triumphantly— I saw him
this afternoon, and he said he would not be back
f °“Oh, e then, he can’t have got my note; very
well ’’-Geoffrey laughed queerly— 111 stay.
The three of them sat on the verandah while the
full moon rose over the trees. Mrs. Geoffrey went
inside to get something, and Geoffrey leaned over
and took the hand of the girl-friend.
“ How did you know ?” he asked.
“ Yesterday she sent me into your den to get a
book of Kipling. ' I saw on your blotting-paper a
paragraph reversed. ‘ Advertisement struck me,
and I took it to the glass and read it. Then 1
tore it up. Oh, I’ve known for a year, the rest of
it—l’ve watched you together till I could have
Sb He dragged her, chair and all, towards him, and
kissed her wildly. „ ,
“ The early tram the day after to-morrow, he
said.
M.B.
Recently arrived at Mossman (N.Q.) 100
Kanakas in one batch. And this to a place the
colored population of which is already far in ex-
cess of the white.
An engine-driver on Cairns (N.Q.) line
saw a man lying across the rails. Pulled up the
engine and found the fellow fast asleep on the
track. Latter explained (with a hiccup) that he
had sat down on the line to take off his boots and
had fallen asleep. They sell marvellous beer in
Cairns district.
There was lately added totheN.S.W. Bar-
roll an old name in a new personage, the stalwart
Mr. Charles Badham Martin, fifth son of the late
Chief Justice James Martin, and nephew of Mr.
“ Bill ” Long, of racing fame. This young gentle-
man, who strongly recalls his distinguished father’s
personal appearance, is a member of the Middle
Temple.
M.L. A. Spruson’s agitation to permanently
reserve Dawes Point has led a number of persons,
politicians and others, to believe that Gov. Phillip
there read the proclamation establishing the pro-
vince of N.S. W. There were, in fact, two proclama-
tions read on two different days, having to do
with the establishment of the settlement. Phillip
read the first near what is now the Hotel
Metropole above Circular Quay, the harbor waters
then running up beyond the site of the present
Custom House. That was 26th Jail., 1788. On
7th Eeb., following, Judge-Advocate David Col-
lins read, at Dawes Point, the King’s commission
defining' the boundaries of the province. He
afterwards read the Act of the Imperial Parlia-
ment establishing Courts of Judicature. During
the reading Phillip stood by in silence, but when
Collins finished, he delivered a brief, informal
speech, telling the convicts that if they obeyed
him they would get on well, but that if they didn’t
their skins and necks would suffer. Circular
Quay was Phillip’s ground ; Dawes Point, Collins’.
A Nocturne.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“But where shall happiness be found?” asked
the young woman. The blasd man looked away
across the water, thought hard for a moment, and
replied, “ In memory ! ”
“ Is that where you find yours? ” she laughed.
“ Yes,” replied the blasd one.
“ Tell me your happiest memory,” said the girl.
The man shook his head. She pressed him ; and
suddenly the cigar went overboard, and the blas£
man seemed to change—get youthful and joyous
again—and commenced :
“ It was long ago, at Patne, just outside
Napier, in Maoriland ; we had a picnic and dance
out there in some pleasure-grounds. There were
three big six-horse ’bus-loads of them; but I and
a chum went down in a double Rob-roy canoe.
And at the picnic he fell suddenly in love, and—
well, in short, I got warning that I would have to
sail home by myself. This leaked out, and all the
afternoon I was beseiged by girls who wanted me
to take them home in that canoe. They didn’t
care one jot for me— it was the sail in the canoe
they wanted. But I was obdurate, for when I
first left home I had promised my mother never
to take a girl in a boat, and that promise I was
determined to keep. So, after hours of persuasion,
they retired, baffled.
“ There was a little girl there, with brown hair
and blue eyes—so blue,” and he pointed to the
soft Australian azure overhead. “She was a
singer, and no one seemed to look after her. All
the women and girls gave her the cold shoulder,
and the men seemed afraid to take her up. I was
afraid of women myself, and thought I had better
not interfere. Still I felt it was a shame. I asked
my chum what was the matter with the singer
that they all slighted her. He explained. Her
family was hard-up, and she had taken money to
sing at the festivities, and the 4 ladies ’ present
(most of whom had todotheirown washing) couldn’t
let slip so delightful an opportunity of trying
what it felt like to exercise authority over a wage-
earner. Well, she was not at all humble, and
gave them scorn for scorn, and all day long
glances of contempt flashed to and fro among the
camellias and verbenas.
“ It was over; the last ’bus had gone ; the lights
were out, the gardens deserted, and I was making
for my Canoe, when I saw the singer coming down
the moonlit road that led to Napier. I looked at
her in astonishment, and as she passed I stepped
out from under the willows and, overcoming my
shyness of women, asked her what had happened and
how she came to be there after the ’buses had gone.
She smiled rather sadly, and told me that some of
the women had hidden her hat and cloak, and
that while she was searching for them the last ’bus
had driven off. ‘ And now I shall have to walk
home. It is evident that someone is anxious for
an opportunity to talk about me ; but if I am seen
about Napier first thing to-morrow morning they’ll
be disappointed.’ Well, it was a long, lonely
walk, for a girl, eight miles over a rough, shingly
road. I mused, and the singer started off. I had
a vacant seat in that canoe, and —it was such a
long walk for a girl—at night, too ! I felt much
inclined to break that promise to mother, just for
once; then I thought of the singer’s face; it was
very pretty. I ran after her. ‘ Excuse me, but
will you trust yourself to me, I’ve got a seat in
my canoe—it holds two, you know—and if you’ll
come you will reaoh Napier about as soon as the
’busses.’
“ To my astonishment she was delighted and
evidently very much relieved. So we went down
to the canoe, and, when she was laced in, I shook
out the sails and let go, and we drifted down the
stream. There was no wind under th 9 willows,
and I had to paddle the first mile or so ; then we
left the willows ; the night-breeze came over the
paddocks, and we darted away through the moon-
light. Down the river, over the miniature bar,
out on to the dancing waters of the Inner Harbor.
The Inner Harbor is separated from the South
Pacific by a belt of sand and shingle about a quarter
of a mile wide, and along this belt runs the
Napier-Patne road. On the road, looming large
in the moonlight, were the three ’busses. The
pic-nickers were singing, and very sweet it
sounded: —
The poplars wave, the moon is low,
The waters murmur as they go,
Good-bye Babette, good-bye Pierrot.
“ I screwed my head round : ‘ Half those women
tried their best and hardest to go home as you are
going ; and perhaps Babette can sing something
to let them know that what they so eagerly sought
for was accepted by you as a favor to me.’ Even
as I spoke the nor’-west wind came rushing down
and caught the sails and drove the canoe faster
than a racehorse through the seething waters. I
ran-in close to the shore, till we were abreast of
the ’busses :
“Now, sing ! ”
“ And she did—something of tumbling waters,
flashing waves, and laughing winds ; and, as her
beautiful voice went sounding through the gale,
the refrain from the ’busses died away, and they
strained their eyes to find the singer. At last,
they discovered her, amid flying spray and lunar
sheen. Some of the better-hearted ones shouted
‘ Good-luck, Babette ! ’
“ Then away we flew, and all the rest was a
dream—a delirium of sirenic- singing, gleaming
waves, and bird-like flight; till, suddenly, we
were against the boatshed. I lifted her out
and stole one burning kiss.
“It burns yet!
“ Then we walked to Clive-square, and reached
it as the ’busses drove up, and her brother dropped
from somewhere and took her home.”
* * * *
The man tried to smoke an unlit cigar. “Go
on ! ” said the girl, eagerly.
“ That’s all,” said the man, regretfully.
“Dear me ! ” said the girl, in an injured tone;
“ the best part of that story never happened. You
ought at least to have got engaged ; and then her
triumph would have been complete iudeed ! ”
“By Jove! I never thought of that,” said the
man.
HERMES.
The M. L. Tory papers are much
troubled about the woes of the horny-handed
toiler now that a general election is close at hand,
and paragraphs like this are constantly meeting
the eye:
Premier Seddon still questions the wisdom of reducing
taxation though he boasts a surplus of half-a-million.
This is cheerful news for the poor working man.
The Bulletin also has a kindly feeling for the
poor working man. That is why it wants to find
a Premier who will screw out a surplus of a million
every year to be spent on useful public works, so
that it won’t be necessary to borrow money and
crush down the poor working man of the future
with the awful interest bill.


King O’Malley, the wild-haired American
gentleman who does the M.L.A. business for
Encounter Bay (S.A.), on hitnself :
I never lose my temper, but keep as cool as a'cast-iron
man on an iceberg. I have been a tiller of brains for over
20 years, travelling all over the world, meeting Presi-
dents, lords, and so on, and if any of those who are at
the meeting come to Adelaide I can show them an offer
of an appointment I had from London with a salary that
would so astonish them as to cause them to wear hands
round their heads for the next fortnight. Should I not
be elected—l have every confidence that I will be re-
turned—l propose visiting Texas, Cuba, Manila, and then
on to London to see my friend Dr. Cockburn, and possi-
bly afterwards enter the House of Commons.

Lazarus.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hear the Talker ringing changes on his ever-
lasting chimes:
“Hire and labor,” “work and wages,’’ themes
we’ve heard a thousand times.
Listen to the blatant liar —“There are better
times ahead 1 ”
But the bitter battle rages, fiercer grows the fight
for bread 1
Are the sweated slaves decreasing ? Is it men the
rich require?
Is the laborer regarded worthy of his wretched
hire ?
Bah ! the Talker’s trade is easy ! He is never one
of us—
He, the parasite of Dives! We, the kin of
Lazarus !
Once the world was wide and wondrous; brawn
and brain with sword equipped
Found a fortune for the fighting. Once the bold
explorer shipped
In his crank and crazy vessel, braving foul and
blessing fair,
Sailed for wealth and virgin waters, steered for
gold and God knows where.
Now the earth is known and narrow; Cunning
grabs his neighbor’s gold.
Pluck is listed for a pittance. Bravery is bought
and sold.
Blood is cheap and men are many ; earth is over-
populous,
There is barely room for Dives—none at all for
Lazarus !
Dives, Lord of all the Nations 1 Ave t great in-
carnate Greed !
Grovel to the great usurper of the crown of every
creed !
Or behold your children kneeling in the dust be-
fore his throne,
Broad-beseeching, and receiving for their piteous
prayer a stone !
Children ? Bondsmen, born to Dives ! ! Div s
has the prior claim
To the girl by you begotten, to the boy who bears
your name.
Make them study genuflection ! Make them
smug, obsequious ;
Better they should dine with Dives than with
dogs and Lazarus.
O, those “ better times a-coming ! ” They have
tarried overlong,
There is naught but Death before us, naught
behind but Want and Wrong.
Even when the mausoleum and the poor “ six feet
of sod ”
Send their souls to Judgment naked, equal in the
sight of God
Lazarus is damned, and Dives lies in Abram's
breast, perchance !
Who can say ? We are but mortal, Christ Himself
might look askance
On the aged, soulless, useless, utterly superfluous
Failure’s very incarnation ! Labor’s ending—
Lazarus !
W.A.P.
The Hawker.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Jonathan Vickers was one of the passing
curiosities of Waddy. He was an aged hawker,
who drifted into the township twice a week during
summer, and as often in winter as weather per-
mitted. In the summer days he came through
the old toll-bar on the north wind with the bil-
lowing dust, and departed via the Swamp-road,
still at the mercy of the elements; and nobody
knew whence he came or whither he went. He was
short, broad, and iron-framed, with a square face
set in a protecting prickly hedge of grey whiskers,
running from ear to ear under the chin. These
whiskers looked like a retaining band for his mop
of rebellious terrier hair. .
Vickers was a man of few words, and most of
these were unpublishable and wrong. His dress
in all weathers was a pair of brown moles, a blue
shirt, some forgotten fragments of a vest, and an
everlasting felt hat, black with age and service,
and higher and wider than any other known in
Waddy or mentioned in its traditions. In summer
he wore his shirt open at the neck ; in winter he
buttoned it up, and was obviously over-dressed
and wretched.
The tough old man hawked fruit, vegetables,
butter, and yeast, a little of each, in the ruins of
an ancient spring-oart that waddled on two bandy
wheels, and lurched endways and sideways, and
was always on the point of falling to pieces, and
always disappointing the boys of Waddy. He
drove a still more ancient ruin of ahorse, harnessed
with a strange assortment of old leather, old rope,
and scrap-iron.
Jonathan drove many horses in his time, but
they were all of the same kind and the same age—
thin pessimists, inhumanly detained from the
knacker or the bone-mill. The feebleness and the
many infirmities of Jonathan’s horses accounted
for the irregularity of his visits. The local belief
was that he always sailed before the wind, because
his horse could never bear up against a stiff
breeze, and housewives expecting him prayed for
a favoring gale. If the wind blew continuously
from the South, Vickers would sometimes dis-
appear for a week at a time, and we had visions
of him blown into strange, Far-North townships,
or camped helplessly under the lee of a hill,
awaiting a change.
At one time the old man had a horse called Star.
It was the most apathetic horse I have ever
known, and it was shaggy and leathery, with
hollow temples, in each of which you might have
placed an egg ; and its hoofs were beaten out
wide and fiat, the result of arduous labor on metal
roads without shoes. But Jonathan alluded to
the animal as “ the pony,” and evidently believed
that Star was young and foolish, and would grow
out of the before-mentioned defects. Truth to
tell, the horse was as venerable as Ahasuerus,
“ and chronicles of untold ancientness were legible
within his beamless eye.”
Whilst Vickers attended to his customers, Star
stood stock-still, with half-closed eyes and a
sagging lip. There was a prayerful, human weak-
ness in his knees ; he had a rump like a starved
greyhound’s, and corduroy ribs, and yet the in-
scrutable mystery of the beast commanded a
certain respect. There was something uncanny
about him ; he was like a haunted ruin, and con-
veyed to all beholders the same impression—an
unpleasant feeling that beneath his battered, way-
worn exterior there lurked a soul that rose superior
to mundane things, and harbored an ancient
philosophy. Many of Jonathan’s customers would
have dreaded to meet his horse after dark.
Star’s reputation depended upon his monumental
imperturbability; he was always buried in pro-
found thought, and even when his master thrust a
chance cabbage-leaf between his teeth, he would
indulge only in a few mechanical movements of
the jaw, and then drift again into deep reflection,
with the vegetable hanging from his lips. He
was difficult to start, and hard to stop. When
Jonathan wished to get him going, he climbed on
to the footboard, shook the reins vigorously, and
drummed bravely on Star’s callous ribs with a
supplejack, whilst delivering a dispassionate ex-
hortation :
“ Click, click, click. Ged up, blast yer bones !
Ged up ! Ain’t yeh got no sense ? Click, click,
111 learn yeh. Gee up, yeh blanky cow! gee.”
Then, to the interested spectator : “ He’s a bit
absent-minded, that’s what’s the matter. Ain’t
proper broke yet. Ged up, Star ! Blank you fer
a sleepy blank ! Click, click, click.”
By-and-by, without giving any preliminary
symptoms, Star would start off with a jerk, jolt-
ing Vickers down on the seat in a way that jarred
all his old bones, and there he would sit,- blanking
fitfully as the horse hobbled along with the energy
of despair, until it was necessary to think of pull-
ing up. Here, again, the difficulty of conveying
an impression to the introspective horse neces-
sitated much hauling and more profanity. Jona-
than started pulling about 50 yards before the
desired goal was reached, and finished on his feet,
hauling at the back of the cart, crying :
“ Woa, there, woa up, you dashed idyit ! Wo
there ! Wo, will yer? Gee back, blast yer blanky
old hide—gee back when I tell yeh ! ”
To the waiting customer, with honest pride :
“Ain’t he a whale fer work? An’him on’y a
pony ! ”
Jonathan’s belief in his “pony’’was genuine
and touching, and when he issued from the house
of a customer one afternoon, and found Star prone
on the road, half buried in the grey dust, his
amazement was too profound for language. Jona-
than stood over the horse, thumbing his gritty
chin, and stared stupidly down at the prostrate
brute. A vague idea that Star was playing off a
practical joke
took hold of
his mind, de-
spite the fact
that he had
never yet
known the
“pony” to be
guilty of such
levity. The
old man began
to expostulate
in the doubt-
ful tone one
assumes to-
wards a friend
who is prob-
ably drunk,
but who might
be dead:
“G e d up
outer this,
blast yer!
What yer lyin’
out there like
a fool fer, eh,
when yeh slep
all night, too?
Ged up !” He
kicked Star
lightly in the
ribs, but the
horse did not
move. “D’yer
wanter hang
out here all
day ? Ged up,
can’t yer?”
Jonathan hau-
led on the
reins, but
when he let go,
Star’s head
fell back again,
inertly flap-
ping the dust.
Then Vickers
went down on
his knees and
pressed his ear
to Star’s lean
ribs and list-
ened. Star
was not dead,
his uppermost
eye moved and
was not more
dim than
usual.
The women
began to gat-
her around
and offer ad-
vice and con-
solation. The
men were at
work or abed,
and the child-
ren pent up in
the little
school - house;
but the wives
and mothers
of Waddy
were a ble
women as a
r u le deep-
chested, and
big- limbed,
and willing;
and they gaily
helped in
stripping Star
of his tangled
harness and
hauling back
the loaded
cart.
Mrs. Dick-
sey was sure
it was a fit,
and Mirs. Ho-
gan ran for
vinegar, and
50 remedies
were suggest-
ed in three
minutes.
Meanwhi 1 e
Jonathan, be-
coming excited
and unreasonable, as the situation grew more
serious, blundered amongst his volunteers, and
bullied them, and swore consumedly.
Presently Star was divested of his trappings,
and lay outstretched in the dust, apparently con-
tent, and Vickers tried argument, and persuasion,
and the powers of evil language again, but the
horse heeded nothing.
“ Give him the taste av dthrink!” said Biddy
Elliot. Biddy found the “taste av dthrink”
efficacious in all her sorrows and afflictions, and
spoke with authority.
But the old hawker had taken up his horse’s
head, and was hauling like a hero.
“ Will ye lend a hand here when ye’ve done
cacklin’ ? ” he blurted fiercely.
They lent a hand readily. Every woman that
could get near enough to lift, lifted with great
heartiness, and presently Star was propped on his
haunches, sitting up with his head lolling stupidly
over Mrs. Best’s shoulder, whilst that long, iron-
framed female embraced him about the neck and
held him like a derrick.
“ Hold him so, ma’am,” cried Jonathan, “ till
we straighten him up at the back.”
Mrs. Best held like grim death, whilst the
hawker and the wives of Waddy fastened upon
Star’s hind-quarters, and lifted them into line.
But just then Star went down by the bow again
with a plunge, Mrs. Best underneath, and there
was a wild disturbance in the dust, and a desper-
ate beating of white-stockinged legs on the ground,
and muffled shrieks, until the rescue-party had
lifted the horse and delivered the stricken woman.
The dust blew about in scudding clouds, for it
was a pestilent day, and the sun flamed in their
faces like an open furnace, but Jonathan was not
deserted. He had an unreasonable idea that all
would be well if he could only get the horse on its
legs again; and yet, as they struggled with him,
Star was as limp as a wet shirt, and his legs swung
under him like ropes.
Mrs. Best was dirty and torn, but not discom-
fited, and lent valuable aid in the next rally. This
time they first lifted the aft end of the horse, and
then, leaving three women to hold the fort, Jona-
than and two others took Star by the head, and
brought him up standing. But only for a second;
he buckled, and went down flop, and Jonathan
lifted up his voice, and was unnaturally eloquent.
They struggled with the malignant “pony” for
an hour or more after that, pulling and hauling
valiantly. They brought and administered many
and various remedies. One, a bran-mash, was
applied hot to his breast in an. old petticoat of
Mrs. Dicksey’s. But nothing eventuated.
The last struggle was successful to a certain
extent. Mrs. Best brought rails from Cadden’s
fence, and they lifted Star up, a section at a time,
and propped him so, with eight brawny women
on each side holding him up. Jonathan sat apart,
and mopped his brow, and studied the situation.
“The devil a bit can you harness him up wid
thim props there,” said Mrs. Elliot.
“I can’t,” said Vickers, “but let be a while,
let be—perhaps ’twill encourage him.”
Tinker Smith had been sent for as a man of
knowledge and experience who had cured cows in
his time, and just here Tinker arrived from his
puddling-tub at the Peep-o’-day, and examined
the unnaturally perpendicular horse with a pro-
fessional air.
“ Dead,” he said. “ Bin dead more ’n a hour.”
Vickers collapsed, and sat down, staring stupidly
at the distance, a beaten man. The sixteen
brawny women instinctively let go the corpse, and
it fell for the twelfth and last time.
“ Give yer arfer crown f’r ’im,” said Tinker pre-
sently. Tinker kept fowls, and fed them on any-
thing dead. Besides, there was money in the
hide. “ Arfer crown,” he continued,“an’l won’t
charge nothin’ fer —fer medical attendance.”
Vickers closed with him, and ten minutes later,
holding the shafts, and tied between the traces,
tugging his cart himself, the old hawker went
manfully out of Waddy along the Swamp-road,
chased by the broiling brickfielder and the scud-
ding dust.
EDWARD DYSON.
